## 7 EXFORMATION A New Information Format

## WHAT IS DESIGN?

# A DIALECT IN DESIGN Dissecting Kenya Hara

Li EDELKOORT

Chairwoman, Design Academy Eindhoven and

Trend Forecaster, Trend Union

He is a person of character. He is a tall man formed by rigor and exacting good taste. He is ageless. He acts as an agent of tranquility and is dressed like a priest in dark, apparently simple, yet superbly cut clothes, or on weekends, like a monk, in hooded sweatshirts over soft drawstring pyjama pants. He is an arresting person with piercing all-seeing birdlike eyes that observe the world from behind intellectual, round-rimmed spectacles. For an instant, you might mistake him for a Swiss modernist designer of the last century, all red and white and angular. Yet he is completely the contrary, all white and red, with a softer edge.

Precise and rigorous, he discusses design like a philosophy of life while continually shifting his awareness of the process, always with a work in progress somewhere, challenging and changing his own wisdom. He is both the timely delivery-boy of communication and the inventor of exformation, struggling and toying with acquiring knowledge and deleting excess in both disciplines.

He is a truly Japanese creator grown from shadows and darkness, his soul bleeding from the scars of recent history, concerned with the roots of his culture and the DNA of its design discipline, and promoting its existence in the face of the other emerging countries of Asia. Therefore, he focuses on the islands' own special values and strives for the survival of paper, print and recently textiles, infusing tradition with contemporary content.

He is inventing a dialect in design, blending global ideas with local and regional color and taste. He is an idealist when it comes to the identity of Nipponese culture. He becomes a samurai in his pursuit of nothingness, an acupuncturist to cure visual excess. He preaches contentment with emptiness and simplicity and sees Japan as an extreme hybrid that absorbs all other influences, neutralizing all chaos, huddling alone on the edge of

![](images/3d1c0f7aad6a07136ac268f2b3a2276ff23f6f0bd866b6b6daca744b80093157.jpg)

Asia. Being a utopian, he believes in a future without artifice, imagining a Japan that liberates itself from the bonds of the American and now Asian dream, coming to terms with a new economic and aesthetic maturity. Like kindred spirits, we both believe in a better world with spiritual and humanistic values.

He is literally in touch. He is a teacher of texture, a leader in the field of tactile experiences, inspiring colleagues and disciples to invent new textures and employ surprising matter, a feast for the fingers as much as for the eyes. Soft-fingered yet strong-willed, he has trained himself and a generation of designers in sensory perception, putting Japan at the forefront of the design of new matter with new touch.

He is a color escapist because he knows that by avoiding color, tactility can and will speak up. He designs in white to express snow, space, floating, trust and rest, to whisper. As if white had become the skeleton of his body of work.

Again and again, he will express and explore the joy of humble design in the life of the everyday. As a sculptor of basic things, he uses, in his major design projects, materials such as paper, cardboard and textile to convey honesty and reliability. He designs paper to feel like snow and embosses it to look like ice, he dresses signage in freshly laundered cotton and continues to use whites for wrapping, storage, ceramics and architecture, imprinted with the impact of red: red flame, red seal, red type, red sign, red heart, red dot...

This is the design of dignity, a whispered value system penetrating the human mind through allthe senses. Emotion is tossed into the process by connecting the dots, by assembling the figurines, by connecting the past with the future and by fusing high and low technology, as in embroidering paper to create new posters. The abstraction of paper and its timeless tranquility make him believe in a future when books will carry information with the appreciation and added value of touch, weight, smell and memory. For him, paper is food, white is a concept, printing is a process and design is oxygen.

As an architect of brand strategy in a truly contemporary and collaborative way, he teaches his clients to reconsider and redirect their image in a softer and almost more natural mode, more fluid too, with the whole process running like a river, shifting in perception silently, without any apparent effort. He pushes his partners to work with excellence and introduces them to the best thinkers, photographers and designers; he is a networker avant la lettre, stimulating his peers to ever newer explorations in the field of the design of emotion.

As a prophet in the wisdom of nature, he turns to small ideas and examples to let nature speak. He will transform the contemplation of a vast and empty horizon in order to invite us to experience freedom of choice and liberation from the bondage of consumerism, and he will imagine our own human hands as a vessel for drinking water, as if being the first and most perfect example of the discipline of design. He is in fact a perfectionist with an eye for the imperfect and the decayed, the depleted nature of things, the random beauty of the changing seasons as well.

Although he believes that foretelling the future (which is my profession!) is a futile occupation, he has, to my mind, a natural talent for it; he becomes an oracle when he speaks of our life in the 21st century and the task he lays before us is vast and all encompassing.

He sees the Japanese aesthetic and way of life as a role model for the Western world, with its modesty, symbolism, tradition and darkness, discarding overconsumption to experience another joy, the joy of being a spiritual individual at one with the sun and the moon, the river and the city, the forest and the animals, including ourselves. The emergence of the growing well-being industry can be seen as an early sign of this philosophy.

As an artist, while drawing hieroglyphics, characters, scribbles or letters, his typography looks like raindrops, rice grains, snowflakes and butterflies... conveying the poetry in nature by a delicate rhythm alone. Therefore, he also seems to have a particular liking for dots, which comes as no surprise to me. Only recently I discovered why the dot is so powerful and prominent at this point in time; I found out that the dot is narrative when it represents an apple, wheel, balloon and moon, as well as abstract when expressing stain, drop, pebble and moon, and therefore has become symbolic of the major movement of the future which will merge everything that stands in stark contrast today: young and old, minimal and decorative, healthy and naughty, ecology and technology, craft and industry, natural and synthetic, abstraction and narrative. The dot hereby perfectly embodies the idea of the hybrid and is almost symbolic of his body of work.

Thus, I can only think of him in his own terms: archaic form, heightened touch and escapist color, seeing a snow white, absorbent, slightly irregular, soothing and immaculately blank piece of paper punctuated with a burning and passionate red heart in the form of a perfect circle. His national identity is embedded in his body and rooted in his soul.

# The Aesthetics of Invisibility

John MAEDA

Associate Director of Research, MIT Media Lab

Kenya Hara is a complex man. He views the world through his many lenses of seeing, tasting, smelling, erasing, evaporating, and all forms of construction and deconstruction as Japan's preeminent art director at the beginning of the 21st century. His potential is boundless, yet if there is one boundary that he has not yet broken, it is the international appeal for his work. For as in the case of many great Asian designers that are unknown on the world stage, Hara's work is tied directly to the language and culture of a non-English speaking nation.With this new book, the world now stands to benefit from decades of Hara's research into the unknown territories of new Japanese design.

The first book I ever read by Hara was his treatise on the relationship of macaroni and architecture. At first I thought that the work was some sort of odd joke. But Hara is a fairly serious man and as I flipped through the pages I enjoyed the mix of dry Japanese humor and serious dialectics about the state of architecture and humanity. There is a spirit of "serious play" in Hara that I find to be common among his similarly aged and empowered peers in Japan, but Hara's ability stands out among the rest. I would say that to Hara, "play" is not really part of his vocabulary. It is by some cosmic accident that within Hara's serious design work, there's always an element of silliness or humor that seems to peek out within the perfection.

My personal favorites for this book are chapters 4 and 7. I've always wondered about the topic of "white" but never thought to write about it at length. Given Hara's incredible history of design work that exhibits a rare purity in form and meaning, I'm not surprised that Hara can carry on a perfectly rational conversation about something so basic as the color "white" as if it were as important as a national crisis. The word "exformation" is new to me. Wikipedia tells me that this word was coined by the Danish writer Tor Nørretranders and can be defined thus, "Exformation is everything we do not actually say but have in our heads, when or before we say anything at all." I think the word describes a kind of unwritten spirituality with respect to information that implicity underlies the animist culture of Japan.

For a long while, I've given out Hara's books to friends as gifts but they've always lamented that by being written in Japanese, the volumes are just great books "to look at." Now with this new book published by Lars Müller, Hara can not only get people to see how he thinks, but to actually read how he thinks. And you will find in this book that his thoughts are incredibly complex and deeply rooted in concepts that span all cultures and civilizations with nothing lost in translation to the mysterious world of Japan. The irony of Hara's work is that in a culture of perceived "Japanese simplicity," he expertly reveals that what lies at the heart of a Zen-like nothing is an incredibly deep, complex, and meaningful something.

# Message Received

Jasper MORRISON

Product Designer

I knew something about Kenya Hara before I ever heard his name mentioned. It was the Matsuya department store in Ginza which communicated it to me on an early trip to Japan. It hasn't often happened to me, but this time in particular I was drawn to the store by its graphics; something almost magnetic pulled me into the shop, a mixture of curiosity and expectation. If I think of Matsuya now I think of the graphics; the two are inseparable. I remember thinking at the time "whoever made the graphics knows what he's doing." When graphic design is very good it's easy to know that it's good, even if we know nothing about graphic design. Good graphic design surpasses all requirements of education and sophistication, and delivers the message so simply that we receive it without registering that a communication has been deliverered. In the case of the Matsuya department store, it told me everything I might expect of the attitude behind the shop without my having to think about it. It's a good department store, there's no doubt, but sometimes I wonder if the graphics express Kenya Hara's idea of what Matsuya should be, and the store itself has to work hard to stay level with its graphics.

The first real meeting I had with Kenya was at his office in Ginza; by that time I had seen some books he had designed, in particular RE-DESIGN, and together with the product design Naoto Fukasawa had been doing, I had the very strong impression that Japan was way ahead of Europe in terms of what each presented as being modern. The work of both seemed icy cool and tasty, and, I felt, shared something in the way it communicated itself on some primary level of human response. Rather than alienating an audience unfamiliar with design, it seems to invite us all to see how easy and natural design can be when it's done right.

At that meeting Kenya showed me other projects he'd worked on, in particular his proposal for the Chinese Olympics. The idea was very clever, as you'll see for yourself in this book. Take a Chinese seal or stamp, the type which prints in red with a circle containing many complex Oriental characters, develop a new set of characters or pictograms, each representing an Olympic pursuit or sport, which can be used individually as signage and which can also be combined inside a circle to form a motif for the games. We all know the heavily systemized pictograms developed in the '70s to represent Olympic sports, but who would have imagined a way to update them in such an elegant and friendly fashion and at the same time create an image for the games which, to my eyes at least, appeared so Chinese? I'm still puzzled that this project wasn't accepted right away as the perfect communication for the Chinese Olympics.

My latest contact with Kenya has been at MUJI, where for some time now he has been art director in charge of visual communication. Not an easy job when you consider that MUJI is a brand that prides itself on not being one, but Kenya's concentration on communicating the spirit of MUJI has created a unique advertising campaign and the perfect complement to the company's philosophy. Since formulating the advertising concept he has completed a detailed system of labeling perfectly tuned to the products MUJI offers. The MUJI project is a great graphic work, but the great skill of Kenya Hara is not limited to the ordinary visual role of graphic design communication, but seems to reach us on some natural level where we don't need to ask ourselves the meaning of his graphic messages, we just receive them.

DESIGNING DESIGN

KENYA HARA

Verbalizing design is another act of design. I realized this while writing this book. To understand something is not to be able to define it or describe it. Instead, taking something that we think we already know and making it unknown thrills us afresh with its reality and deepens our understanding of it. For instance, suppose there's a glass here. You might know about a glass. But what if you need to design one? The moment a glass is proposed as an object to be designed, you start thinking about what kind of glass you want to design, and you lose a little bit of your understanding of "glass." Arrayed in order before you are dozens of glass vessels of gradually varying depths, from "glass" to "dish." What if you are asked to clarify the exact boundary point between one and the other? Faced with the objects, you're at a loss. And again you become a little less sure of your knowledge of a glass. However, this doesn't mean that your knowledge has been overturned. Indeed, it's just the opposite. You've become more keenly conscious of glasses than before, when you understood them by simply unconsciously calling them all by the term "glass." Now you actually understand glasses more realistically. The whole world looks different if you just put your chin in your hand and think. There are an unlimited number of ways of thinking and perceiving. In my understanding, to design is to intentionally apply to ordinary objects, phenomena and communication the essence of these innumerable ways of thinking and perceiving. Even if you lose touch a little with your understanding of design by reading this book, it doesn't mean you know less about design than you did before. It's proof that you've progressed another step deeper into the world of design.

# Preface

Kenya HARA

RE-DESIGN

Daily Products of the 21st Century

![](images/2c2886d308a05f3ebfbd88aea5f015d450ace7ee36af099a6d30110fb2f52399.jpg)

## RE-DESIGN

Exhibition first held in Tokyo, Takeo Paper Show 2000

# Daily Products of the 21st Century

## Making the Ordinary Unknown

"Re-design" refers to a redoing of the design of ordinary objects. You could call it an experiment, an attempt to look at familiar things as if it were our very first encounter with them. Re-design is a means by which to correct and renew our feelings about the essence of design, hidden within the fascinating environment of an object that is so overly familiar to us that we can no longer see it.

Producing something new from scratch is creative, but making the known unknown is also an act of creation. Maybe the latter is more useful in nailing down just what design is. This thought came to me some time ago.

![](images/745e1c4af4bb92ccf15b2f4d1e2936e620d80b4250f036478b70df51186b097d.jpg)

For the last ten years of the 20th century, this concept of re-design was stuck in the back of my mind. So I mounted an exhibition of new designs for macaroni. I designed fireworks. I came up with a new ball game. I put together and carried out several projects that revolved around the concept of "making the ordinary unknown," which takes "ordinary" one step further. Gradually my direction became clear. Below, I'd like to describe some of these projects.

The more firmly we're convinced that we've identified an object, the less precisely we understand it. The 21st century is an age of discovery— of astonishingly fine designs found right in our midst, in our daily lives. We used to design mere stimulation, but now we part ways with that past and look at the ordinary with clear eyes, to yield new thinking on design.

## Art and Design

All the objects that shape our living environment, house floor, bathtub, toothbrush, are comprised of basic elements like color, form and texture. The foundation of modernism is the concept that their shaping should be entrusted to the rational, lucid consciousness, whose aim is to organize those elements. And thus design thinking is, in a broad sense, construed to be the pursuit of universal balance and harmony of the human mind through rational, integrative manufacture; design refers to the will to interpret the meaning of human life and existence through the process of making things. Art, on the other hand, is the act of discovering a fresh human spirit. Both design and art employ molding or representation, which is the manipulation, one way or another, of objects perceptible by our sensory organs. This is why I am often asked about the difference between art and design. As I'm not particular about the significance of combining art and design, nor segregating art from design, I won't define them here. However, in order to help my readers understand the concept of design as well as rationally grasp the periphery of the RE-DESIGN project, I will say a little about their differences.

Art is an expression of an individual's will to society at large, one whose origin is very much of a personal nature. So only the artist knows the source of his own work. This loftiness is what makes art so cool. Of course, there are plenty of ways to interpret the expressions that artists give birth to. Non-artists commune with art by coming up with interesting interpretations of art, appreciating it, commenting on it, re-editing art as in an exhibition, or using art as an intellectual resource.

Design, on the other hand, is basically not self-expression. Instead, it originates in society. The essence of design lies in the process of discovering a problem shared by many people and trying to solve it. Because the root of the problem is within the society, everyone can understand plans for solutions and processes for solving the problem, in addition to being able to see the problem from the designer's perspective. Design is appealing because the process creates inspiration that is engendered by this empathy among human beings in our common values and spirituality.

The term "re-design" includes this theme of society's commonly shared and recognized objects or issues. Establishing daily products as the project's theme is not some novel idea but is the most natural, appropriate method for reexamining the concept of design, because design deals with our common, shared values.

## RE-DESIGN Exhibition

In April 2000 I produced an exhibition project with a title that was a tad long: "RE-DESIGN: Daily Products of the 21st Century." For the project I wanted 32 leading creators in Japan to design anew some very ordinary commodities, including toilet paper and matches. The participants are active in a variety of fields, including architecture, graphic design, product design, advertising, lighting, fashion, photography and literature, and all have a candid perspective on, and insight into, their profession. Each participant took charge of one of the "re-design" subjects. We produced a prototype of each proposal. The results were exhibited next to an example of the conventionally designed object to allow the audience to enjoy the contrast. I was the one who basically decided on the pairings of object and creator.

This kind of project is often likely to be misunderstood as a sort of gag. Of course, I had no intention of excluding amusement, but I didn't aim for it; the project is essentially serious. If it were a game of tennis, it would be as if I were intentionally serving balls to a succession of top players, each in a spot deemed tricky to return. And they were returned: shots far more exquisite and sharp than anything I had served. The serve was a design question, but the volleys, compensating for the nascent nature of my question, came one after the other, so creative that they included yet other, new questions.

To avoid misunderstanding, I would like to add that the RE-DE-SIGN Exhibition never aimed to actually improve daily commodities by enlisting the help of top-caliber designers. Daily products exist within a stable of fully mature design that's been cultivated through history, difficult for even famous creators to surmount in a short period of time. However, each of these creators' design proposals had a clear idea and exhibited a striking difference in the thought behind them and that behind the conventional products. With this project I hoped to decipher the essence of design found within this gap. I learned a lot from the series of procedures. Now I would like to track and introduce some of the subjects.

![](images/1a681f1595ad98fcddeb22de40bd1ae5b9c483e5c605fa26b074fef9f9ecee8c.jpg)

![](images/a31b0087f83b47207958a4ae539edcd844e86359287a8aeba704b2f49263dae5.jpg)  
Rolls of toilet paper began to be used in the U.S. and Europe in the mid-19th century. The photo on the right shows a temporary shelter made from paper tubes designed by Shigeru Ban at the time of the Great Hanshin Earthquake.

## Shigeru Ban and Toilet Paper

The subject I gave the architect Shigeru Ban was toilet paper. Ban is known around the world for designing buildings using paper tubes, and he has genuine reasons for this approach. Ban has found that paper, which superficially appears fragile, actually has enough strength and durability to be used in permanent buildings. More importantly, he has focused on its flexibility as a building material that can be manufactured in simple, inexpensive facilities. Because these facilities impose only a small burden, paper tubes can be produced anywhere in the world. They conform to universal standards as well, so that the same tubes can also be procured anywhere. And when they are no longer needed, they can be recycled. Ban focuses on the many latent elements of this material that could be vital in the times ahead.

Just after Japan's Great Hanshin Earthquake in 1995, Ban designed temporary housing as well as a church using these paper tubes. For a refugee camp in the Republic of Rwanda he lobbied the Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees and convinced them to use paper tubes as structural material for the shelters. Using wood instead would have immediately depleted the forest resources, and shelters too sturdily built would have attracted permanent refugees. Paper tubes were best suited for the structures of simple tent-like shelters. At Expo 2000 in Hanover, Ban built the Japanese Pavilion using paper tubes as well, which made possible a gigantic arched space several dozen meters high. This pavilion was based on the concept of recycling the tubes as soon as the Expo was over. All of these projects allow us to appreciate his clear vision in realizing necessary architecture based on universal and rational ideas while avoiding the squandering of resources. The next project to be tackled by this architect, working with these concepts, was toilet paper.

![](images/1553ad1b5544a70a8b390619180aa7236ed58e96d748d5fc05c216711b3d26fd.jpg)

The photo shows Ban's re-design of toilet paper. The square paper tube forms the core of the roll. Because the core is square, the paper rolled upon it ends up shaped like a square as well. Placed into the dispenser and pulled out for use, the square roll resists, with a clunky sound of "kata-kata-

kata." The conventional round roll, on the other

hand, moves along smoothly, lightly pulled to the refrain of "suuh-suuh-suuh." The traditional design gives you more paper than you need. The square toilet paper, on the other hand, generates resistance, functioning to reduce the consumption of resources and also deliver the message: economize.

![](images/a80c75fb866c9450ae272823f0ba2d4b7d198eb0245e0d02963e60242b215923.jpg)

![](images/fac9b43c6f30e3c6610af24a7f1d1f5fad6afb95f239c45339c19057dfc2e93f.jpg)

In packaging as well, the square rolls fit together nicely, saving space in transportation and storage, while round rolls leave gaps.

Thus a single re-design solution, a square-cored tube, produces dramatic change. Ban's proposal doesn't mean that all the toilet paper rolls in the world should be square. But I would like you to notice the note of criticism sensed vaguely at the periphery of the concept of square toilet paper. From the perspective of daily life, design passes criticism on civilization. Not that this is anything new. Design thinking and perception have been critical since the beginning. I'll be happy if you sense design's critical nature in the difference between two objects: the square roll and the round one.

![](images/aefeb80817293b0049f9cc10611d1e2741b83282e42700f2ee99980b08b4084f.jpg)

![](images/88507e4224b18ff5c7e15cffb9e594e09fe8f7a2b426d5ee119e0aff25fdbd18.jpg)

![](images/43233d5104c96ccf3dc806e92ec68d45c33617c8a57882264558c2c0657f9235.jpg)  
Actual Japanese exit/entry stamps.  
The right-hand photo is the "human Othello" scene from Sato's film, "Kino."

## Masahiko Sato and Exit/Entry Stamps

Masahiko Sato has directed a number of widely viewed ad campaigns in Japan, proposed the game IQ, and made the film KINO. As a professor, he gives a very popular seminar at Tokyo National University of Fine Arts and Music. A song he produced for a kids' TV program, Dango-San-Kyodai (Three Dumpling Brothers), became a huge hit, and helped confirm that Sato's message reverberates even in the hearts of children. The common thread among all of his projects, in all of these fields, is that he coolly pursues the rules of communication and applies them in an exceptional way.

KINO is an anthology of short stories. Among them is one entitled "Human Othello." Three men are standing side by side at a bus stop, facing right. A fourth man comes along. For whatever reason, he joins their line but facing the opposite direction. The man on the right end, who should have been standing at the front of the line, glances at the fourth man and contagiously turns to the left. Soon, the other two in the middle notice something wrong about their direction and slowly turn as well. Consequently, the entire line changes direction. It's an interesting story portraying an Othello-like phenomenon that affects human psychology. Sato has always looked for something like the seeds of communication. Around these seeds in the human mind, tiny movements occur. Let's call them the germs of inspiration. Sato discovers the principles of their germination. In everything he does, we recognize his sharp observation and the sophisticated precision of this principle.

The subject I asked Sato to work on was exit and entry stamps for passports at international airports. The Japanese stamps indicate the difference between exit and entry with a circle and a square. The idea is simple and the shapes function well, but I asked him to exercise his ingenuity by designing a stamp to warm people's hearts. His solution is shown here. The entry stamp depicts an airplane pointing right, and the exit, one pointing left.

![](images/9b8d55e7d542c167bc2a0eda56745126c1abf0dd914c4570db5adf8627374c14.jpg)

This concept holds the seeds of an exchange designed to inject a dose of communication into an office procedure via the intermediary of stamps, which would lead to further germination within the minds of travelers exposed to the stamps. You might see the moment of contact between stamp and traveler as an "ah-ha!" moment, when the traveler, not expecting the message, falls into a bit of a panic, which upon the instant of understanding turns into a small exclamation point in the back of his mind. This "ah-ha!" is positive and full of goodwill. If these stamps were used at our international airports, where 10,000 tourists a day begin their first visit to Japan, they could create

![](images/44433db0a1916fa9307c2dd355c7c7150c4147e6824c213f2c4f1af8a4d14f43.jpg)

10,000 positive ah-ha's a day, or 10,000 friendly feelings, borne via this small taste of hospitality.

It would be difficult to achieve the same effect via other media. It would not be very easy to produce a TV commercial targeting all the first visitors to Japan to induce them to think that our country lives up to their expectations. Besides, we have no media capable of finding and accessing only those people.

There's a story about a man who flew to a certain country. When the official handed his passport back to him, he said, "Happy birthday!" having noticed the date in the man's passport. He could have returned it without mentioning it, but he opted to say something. The man later said that this simple exchange made him feel good about the whole country. And thus lying dormant in these minor affairs are the seeds of communication.

Sato's stamps show us that such seeds exist and tell us exactly how to germinate them. To those of us who have been more than a little absent from actually using everyday communication, enveloped as we are in dreams about the possibilities of electronic media, and are thus at risk of meandering off into a kind of deafness to communication, I believe Sato has given a valuable hint.

On the other hand, at the end of the project, Sato mentioned that during the orientation he thought it would be nice to have stamps that convey our hospitality and so had created some that would give tourists a lively jolt, but on reconsidering, he began to think that the conventional, neutral stamps do a better job. We are surrounded by too many overdone designs, he thought, and the existing ones show just enough ingenuity. Sato thought it would be a shame to overdo it and strike a wrong note; the more realistic approach, he concluded, would be not to stick his finger in the pie at all.

He may be right. The prudence and sincerity that allows him to be unafraid of making a minor change even after coming to his conclusion is part of the same sense of precision that allows him to make use of and put into practice the rules of communication in the first place.

## Kengo Kuma and the Roach Trap

Kengo Kuma is an architect of intelligence. But he is distinctly different from the usual brainy architects who use all their intellectd to interpret their own architecture. Kuma feels ashamed to present to the world, under the term "architecture," any object that is too splendid, and uses his mind deliberately and carefully to bring quality sophistication to his professional commitments.

Kuma believes that to evaluate the quality of today's architecture in particular, the point is in figuring out how to curb two things: the destiny of monumental architecture that asserts authority, and the individual's desire to create idiosyncratic, aesthetic depictions via architecture. In this respect, Kuma has been working to create a highly cultivated sophistication. His manners of restraint are never the same. In one project he created a strange object that was paradoxically designed to disprove his refinement. In another he saturated the architecture with light to lessen the sense of its existence. In yet another he designed a building so it would be invisible from the outside.

The Kiro-san Observatory is a representative example of the invisible architecture of Kengo Kuma. While the observatory allows for a panoramic view of the Seto Inland Sea from the top of a mountain, the building itself remains invisible unless you fly over in a helicopter to see it from the sky. Even looking up to the mountaintop from the base, you can see only the mountain itself. Kuma was asked to design an observatory on land that was originally reclaimed from the mountain. After construction was completed, the observatory was buried and trees were planted on the site, which functions successfully as a spot from which to command a view, but the tangible building sinks out of sight if viewed from other locations.

![](images/8dd768300ebb9003e15573b1bf796e7b60a5cd717350bdd36a88e9291e92fbcc.jpg)

![](images/732f2eac147b10847aaa16578cbc05912a98526f218e328519c79a805e9c1db7.jpg)

![](images/9676e3133283ec5da0a634d4a91ae5569fd4a5487dc64db03fac5411f4cf28d3.jpg)  
A house-shaped cockroach trap has been a popular item in Japan since it debuted in 1973. The middle and right photos are examples of invisible architecture designed by Kengo Kuma. They are the Kiro-san Observatory and the Water/Glass guesthouse.

![](images/ec7e1fd2babdc1c14d0e18d27c7019a339537f4387e5cc7ae458897d00077a34.jpg)

One of Kuma's characteristic works is the Water/Glass guesthouse on the Atami coast in Shizuoka Prefecture. The structure is situated halfway up a bluff extending into the ocean. Its external appearance is also invisible without taking a ship out and searching for the structure through binoculars. Instead, visual dynamism is injected into the interior of the building; there is a waterfilled terrace on the eaves covering the outer edge of the glass structure. When its surface is viewed from inside the room, it creates continuity with the Pacific Ocean afar. The structure is designed as an instrument serving only to view the outside from the inside, candidly banishing the idea of an external appearance.

From an architect working to design buildings from this intelligent perspective, I requested a design for a roach trap. Widely available in

Japan, cockroach traps have a wily ability to capture the unpopular insects. The cockroach, to enter the trap, wipes oil off its feet on the entrance mat, soon becomes attached to the adhesive, and eventually starves to death. These products are designed to look like the home of some happy cockroach family, which sounds absurd, but gives us some relief by dismissing from our minds the real, brutal nature of the product's function: killing bugs. This conventional cockroach trap is quite popular and actually sells very well.

![](images/f234a3285dbbfb8ede325e06db3ad94de26a00bf4c6f4f5e2b3c73c0f1fe91e3.jpg)

Kuma's re-design proposal is a roll of adhesive tape. To use it, you cut off the desired length and fold it into a square tube. Because the inner part is adhesive, it becomes a sticky translucent tunnel. The outsides of the section joints are coated with an adhesive mixture as well so that the tube-shaped roach trap can be placed vertically on walls, too. Long and thin, it can be easily set into chinks along kitchen cabinets and such, from which roaches are tempted to emerge.

Kuma's product goes well with modern interiors; from now on, even roaches will be captured within functional beauty. Though very small, this product is definitely architecture and gives us a clue to understanding this architect's way of thinking. It's just like Kengo Kuma to negate a monumental house, choosing instead a changeable tube.

![](images/20fbd9b7db79bcef0b5e086791986d2e9a6ad065d401005f3d675b2aefc4df23.jpg)

![](images/ffc5583570a3f361b59cc6fbce386f008bbdd40a96076aae1c2fdf381963e788.jpg)

![](images/54af94ff559886f83081cb3d857fc40ea76ba04e7e03ab4be4aff0b550a91c00.jpg)

![](images/0a8e51b405f669d31a67cda4d096f91d0b43e21861db4abb9dfaa54aae88c87a.jpg)  
The number of matches has been dramatically shrinking since 1975 as a result of the advent of disposable lighters. The right-hand photo is Sendai Mediatheque where Kaoru Mende designed the lighting. Toyo Ito was in charge of the architectural design.

## Kaoru Mende and Matches

The subject here is matches, which ignite when you strike them. Lately, it's unusual to find matches in the home. Even for cigarettes, we usually use lighters. There are fewer and fewer occasions in which we actually handle fire. A gas range gives off light, but electric cooking equipment is more and more popular. This being the case today, you might wonder why I chose matches as one of the subjects of the re-design project. Basically, the theme here is to design the fire that lies closest to home. Guessing that this probably falls within the realm of illumination professionals, I asked the lighting designer Kaoru Mende to work on matches.

![](images/70189f1b71363dd30c016af954c84f37fe8a0526a31f6e12bbf605ad947cd25e.jpg)

![](images/350533e558b228018aaa75655d2769499189a02d0d28d89f0ca6b8f1a53f8aec.jpg)

Mende has worked on several lighting projects for large-scale public spaces, including the Sendai Mediatheque and Roppongi Hills. It's appropriate to say that Mende designs not simply lighting appliances, but light itself. That is, he is a designer of light as well as darkness. He became somewhat of a celebrity by organizing a team called The Lighting Detectives to conduct research on urban nighttime lighting. According to the team, vending machines emit the strongest light on city streets. The Detectives also report that convenience store lighting is abnormally intense. That intensity then probably makes a dominant impression on the nighttime streets of Tokyo. The Lighting Detectives team proves that taking sharp notice of these facts is a primary step towards urban design.

Mende came up with the following solution for matches. First, they are natural twigs whose tips are coated with a combustible substance. Specifically, his concept is to entrust these dried twigs with a final role (matches), prior to their return to the soil. The design asks us to consider the relationship between humans and fire, which spans tens of thousands of years, letting our imagination run through time, over our ancestors' life as it was intertwined with fire, and then place fire in the palm of our hand. Now as we take a closer look, the shapes of sticks are quite aesthetically pleasing. Our busy life usually exiles such aesthetic objects from our mind. Nature, fire and humans: Mende's solution impressively evokes their individual existences.

These are called Anniversary Matches. It would be quite effective to use these matches to light the candles on birthday or anniversary cakes. After all, burning fire is a powerful symbol. Perhaps this is because flame enshrines the possibility of both a ferocious, destructive power that can grow immensely, and the essence of creation. This giant imagination is infused into a match-sized design solution.

## Kosuke Tsumura and Diapers

The subject here is the disposable diaper, but not for babies: diapers for adults, particularly the aged. Disposable diapers were first put on the market in 1963. Since high polymer absorbent material was applied in 1983, they have become better fitting and easier to wear, as well as remarkably compact. Functionally, disposable diapers have reached a high standard, but I still felt that the conventional product was missing something. If I became incontinent tomorrow I'd have to use what's on the market today. Imagining the scene, I start feeling a bit miserable. You'd agree if you were to stand in front of a mirror wearing such a thing. The sight reflected there hardly maintains human dignity, because the pattern is basically the same as that for babies. I'd hate to wear such a thing, and I'd be very sad to have to ask my parents to.

I entrusted this subject to the fashion designer Kosuke Tsumura. His brand, called Final Home, is based on a completely different concept than that underlying conventional brands, which are concerned only with fashion and trends. The Final Home concept came about from an experimental attempt to find a new kind of relationship between clothes and human beings.

![](images/1dd37003754453faa9400328410620e3a762a1ce5e46c48b3e1e25b60ae7a4d1.jpg)

![](images/06beaafb4338c5ecbce2100377182e9734c9d96111fd1076167c3cd0058f60ce.jpg)

By employing a polymer absorber, disposable diapers have made a major step forward in terms of compact design and improved comfort. The right-hand photo shows clothing designed by Kosuke Tsumura featuring many zippers and pockets.

![](images/6b780231b12d51872726c8ff01d2d7db73aafee26b4b9c9d3256e91c244123a2.jpg)

![](images/a9070195dfad9ff3c354c041206fa551705eefa9a206d2d995e943b2a8cd8c5c.jpg)

Let's take an example from his fashion line. The article of clothing pictured at the top of the page has zippers all around, each opening to a pocket. Stuff them with rolled up magazines or newspapers and the silhouette of the costume changes. At the same time, it retains heat. If you stuffed the pockets with crumpled paper, you could sleep outdoors wearing this alone. Of course, this wasn't designed for the homeless, but it's so flexible that we can envision this use. The design has several varieties. One has removable zippered sleeves, quite refreshing. Trying it on could change your consciousness towards clothing itself. The Final Home approach, initiating an exploration of the diversity of relationships between clothes and humans, has gained popularity and international acclaim.

![](images/fa01115e7656509405e17c468c2144d5db6038185faf79445022959275dbf418.jpg)

Here is Tsumura's solution to the diaper problem (see page 41). First of all, he's styled it as a pair of trunks, thus making it fashionable. Please note that the images on the previous page are not before- and after-use photos. The photo on the right uses transillumination; the darkened part is the high polymer absorbent material. Thanks to its effectiveness, there is no leakage, even with the trunks' styling. And so an aesthetically pleasing diaper has come into being. But Tsumura took the idea farther.

Proposing an entire set of clothing to absorb sweat and other bodily secretions, Tsumura has included variations like running shirts, T-shirts and shorts. Each garment bears a letter that indicates its absorption performance ranking. Shirts and shorts are set at protection level 1. Diapers are set

![](images/4486b3cf87a0c4b53ec2e2b064ab5047900e06a8c1abfa2f182d40112382c28d.jpg)  
at the highest level, 3. Now, even if I had to start wearing diapers tomorrow, I wouldn't mind. I'd just choose protection level 3 trunks. Basically, this product completely removes the psychological resistance to wearing diapers. Tsumura's diapers have proved that design can delicately solve even our psychological concerns.

![](images/d68b1c740b818f9ad82b4f77d3eab39b9420aab8be32e034297d503f471df78e.jpg)

![](images/1957f5684781cd7085219050f9771bc7db175754bb20a4c8a8eaab0f6d46533f.jpg)  
Tea bags were commercialized in the early 20th century, with one spoonful of tea leaves packaged in gauze. Today, major industrialized nations use tea bags more than loose tea leaves. The right-hand photo is a CD player designed by Naoto Fukasawa. Just like a kitchen fan, the disc begins to rotate when the string is pulled.

## Naoto Fukasawa and Tea Bags

Naoto Fukasawa is a product designer, but he works in a delicate, elusive place that other designers don't even see. It's as if his designs affect the unconscious realm, so that people using things he's designed are hardly aware of design's function. A designer like this, capable of not only creating hit products, but also inducing the user to take certain actions, without allowing us to recognize exactly where his strategy operates, is a menace! Before his mastery is exploited towards some evil end, we need to work hard to unlock the secret of his design.

Say you need to design an umbrella stand. Some sort of tubular object immediately comes to mind. But Fukasawa insists that we should eliminate this idea. He says all we should do is cut a groove 8mm wide and 5mm deep into the concrete floor at the building's entrance. Visitors looking for a place to put their umbrellas would be quick to look for a spot to stick the top end. As if the umbrella itself were on the prowl for a place to stand, it would no doubt easily discover the groove that had been set there in anticipation, and all the umbrellas would stand in a neat row. And yet people using it may have no idea that the groove is an umbrella rack. The orderly row of umbrellas would be the result of unconscious behavior. Fukasawa rests his case: the umbrella rack design is complete unto itself.

The Fukasawa method is to examine our subconscious behavior and design for that. This manner of thinking reminds me of an emerging cognitive theory called "affordance." Affordance is the comprehensive understanding of both the subject of an action and the environment that "affords," or allows for, a certain phenomenon. For instance, standing seems to be a behavior imbued with the will of the subject (the standing person), but in reality, standing wouldn't occur if there were no gravity and no decently solid surface on which to do it. Under a weightless condition, we would float. Even in a deep swimming pool full of water, standing does not come into existence as an action. In the case of standing, both gravity and a solid surface are said to "afford" the action.

The following story cites Fukasawa's explanation. Suppose you're on a drive with your girlfriend and you feel like having some coffee. You stop at a vending machine. You insert a coin and press a button. The first paper cup of coffee is served from the machine. Holding this cup, you can't get another coin out of your pocket to put in the machine. You need to find somewhere to set the cup. Your girlfriend is still in the car. There is no suitable place to put it. But you realize that the roof of the car is at just the right height. It seems a bit bad mannered, but, with no alternative, you place the cup on the roof of the car and insert another coin for the second cup of coffee. In this case, although the car roof is clearly not designed to serve as a table, its ideal height and its level nature "affords" the action of putting down the cup of coffee. This results in the generation of an action: placing the cup on the roof. This attitude of both comprehensively and objectively observing the variety of environments and situations that are tied to various actions is called affordance. Fukasawa doesn't deduce a solution with the strict guidance of the theory of affordance. However, his focal points are close to the concept.

Consider the CD player Fukasawa designed. It looks almost exactly like a wall-mounted fan. The CD is inserted in the center. If you pull the cord that on a fan would be the on-off cord, the disc begins to spin, just like a fan. We know it's a CD player, but our previous experiences and memories of fans, entrenched in our minds, cause us to prepare ourselves for that object. Particularly the skin on our cheeks, with its extraordinary sensitivity, begins to activate the "sensor" of the sense of touch, standing ready for the wind we expect to blow. What comes wafting over us is not wind, though, but music. Thanks to the use of the fan-shaped design, this product, although its performance as audio equipment alone may be a little lower than average, may in fact provide relatively better performance by inducing the users' sensors to stand ready for the flowing music. Fukasawa's methods attempt to establish this kind of magical relationship between the object and its design. Whatever the case may be, the MUJI CD player, which boasts a concept that differs completely from that of conventional audio equipment, has been a popular product around the world.

![](images/93ffe7a607af610ccf732f61e29981b9588285ea91f8ce1d55732dea2dbf5fd0.jpg)

Fukasawa's re-design subject was the tea bag. Nowadays 90% of the world's tea ends up in tea bags. What kind of approach would Fukasawa take on such a standard design? Let me introduce two of his three proposals.

The first is a tea bag with a circle (like a ring) on the end of a string. The ring is the color of perfectly brewed tea. This isn't to tell the user to brew the tea till it turns this color, however; no unsolicited advice is intended here. Fukasawa imagines, though, that as they use the product, people would gradually become conscious of the relationship between the color of tea and that of the ring. It would engender comments like, "I like my tea brewed until it's darker than the ring," or "I think I'll have weaker tea today." He doesn't mean to specify the meaning of the color, but prepares for a meaning to grow from it. In short, Fukasawa designs the potential for affordance.

![](images/6c88c759a0054e72c30e676b9b2f36ca8d752a6ffef1f6e753d72c08b71ec122.jpg)

Another of Fukasawa's design solutions is a marionette-shaped tea bag. This concept comes from the motion of dipping a tea bag in hot water, which reminds him of a marionette dancing. The handle on this humanshaped tea bag looks like a marionette handle too. When the bag is dipped in hot water, the leaves swell to fill the bag, creating a deep-hued doll. Repeating the dipping action, the user is engaged in a wondrous world of puppet play. Thus, design intrigued with the unconscious emerges through the medium of an action.

![](images/2f503ee08b0d92e1e4cae2676493df26b8600f78e0d722c62e96c52b9c822f1d.jpg)  
Tokyo, Japan—April 2000

## The RE-DESIGN Exhibition Travels the World

To conclude the story of the re-design solutions, I don't think it's necessary to interpret all 32 subjects and their solutions. I believe the reader has fully understood the project's goals through the several examples introduced already.

Our daily surroundings seem completely wrapped in design: our floors, walls, TV monitors, CDs, books, beer bottles, lighting appliances, bathrobes, coasters, and so on and so forth. All are the outcome of design work. The talent of the designer is to reexamine these daily surroundings at any time, with a fresh eye, as if they were as yet unknown.

I used to wonder what kinds of novel things would be created in the 21st century, and what kinds of innovations we'd experience, doubtless one after the other. But today I would say that this very idea should be left behind in the previous century. Instead, a new era should arise, as the ordinary that we think we know so well is made unknown, one object after another. Just as

![](images/682d9b25f6d5fdad2195fc25fe14d1b40a91eb4166381c4086cba7e75b9a5dc4.jpg)

Display system designed by the London-based Azumis. It allows the scale of an exhibit to be altered according to site size, and is easy to assemble, disassemble and transport.

![](images/c6d0056e15ae2f67ceab253ff2f30ca2edac100fbd55fdf2626d38dbc9d8fec6.jpg)  
Top: Glasgow, U.K.—November 2001 Bottom: Copenhagen, Denmark—April 2002

the cell phone has taken its position at the forefront of communication, unnoticed, we shall realize that we sit right in the center of the future, as it reveals itself to us little by little, from within every aperture of our ordinary life. The idea that new things will come crashing upon us from afar, as waves begin in the distance of the sea, is an image that belongs to the past.

At the same time, the idea that technological innovation sweeps over the entire planet and shapes our daily lives is also an illusion. Technology will bring forth new possibilities, but it is still only an environment, not creativity itself. The question is how to use human wisdom in the new environment afforded by technology. What should we aim for? What kind of plans should we realize?

![](images/5ca75cc668c4d744e5e3745a5d445f0c2af4561955ef75a3857f0ede00a5fefc.jpg)

![](images/6420e72786506810664d35c13a7a83127d275360bc963d65a786732de9c3fe30.jpg)

Raising our point of view regarding daily life is like discovering decimals. The future is not where everyone looks for it, "there," right after

the present. It's not an integral number in a line: 9, 10, 11.... It's somewhere like 6.8 or 7.3. The new design scene nestled in ordinary life will be created by the intellect that perceives the unlimited numbers between any two successive integers.

After the RE-DESIGN Exhibition was held in four Japanese cities, it was sent off to art

![](images/f064e8c6d926101b5c5a2157ea56cc2d2ed91439a80e1cd7423540330f8df4d0.jpg)

galleries in other parts of the world, with a bit of a push from our side. The invitations were sparse at first, but it finally got into the world tour mode, and as it debuted in various cities, requests began to pour in. Over a period of several years, RE-DESIGN visited Glasgow, Copenhagen, Hong Kong, Toronto, Shanghai, Beijing and Shenzhen. Contrary to my expectations, the public response was huge. In the beginning this anything-but-ordinary exhibition was misunderstood as something humorous. The enthusiasm of the articles on the whole, which sometimes included this misinterpretation,

![](images/c04654d16b3610ed4a65f827f5668b0ec1a99416c1b7c97461ec57ea6e54df60.jpg)

Left  Top: Hong Kong—November 2002

Left Bottom: Toronto, Canada—February 2003

was surprising. As the import of the exhibition was gradually ascertained, though the contrast with the lighter interpretation seemed to create a greater impact in the end. At the museum in Glasgow, where the exhibition attracted more than 20,000 visitors, students from eight elementary and junior high schools participated in a RE-DESIGN workshop. In Toronto the exhibition was extended two months more than originally planned.

The world is just now starting to get it. We have to get functioning in every part of society a sense of value and insight that can lead the entire world to a rational balance. It's beginning to change. The world is now keenly in need of an awareness that can cope flexibly with issues on every front: a truly fair economy, resource conservation, dealing with the environment, mutual respect for ideas, and so on.

From the beginning, the concept of design has always occupied a position directly adjacent to this awareness and this rationality. The RE-DESIGN tour made this perfectly clear to me.

![](images/e2a8a85e23b9b710811fd43c6843207e094cd6973bb4116defd1575efd27629e.jpg)

![](images/f0ec19fb7c8d4decbf7e3c7609b2fdf0bb6facb054cda9ff615e38ae4293770e.jpg)

Pipe rigate, a commercial variation of tubular pasta, is charmingly molded to look like a human face when viewed from a certain angle.

## The Architects' Macaroni Exhibition

Food Design

In 1995, about five years before the RE-DESIGN Exhibition, I planned and produced the Architects' Macaroni Exhibition in Tokyo. Looking back, I see that since it was not premised on practical commercial production, it was a bit too romantic as a product design show, but I also see there my sincere interest in designing food.

The macaroni project arose from my curiosity about whether food can be designed. Cooking might be considered, in a broad sense, as design. However, since it has already been deeply cultivated through a very high level of culture, it doesn't seem that significant to dig around in it by intentionally bringing up the concept of design. Boiled, potatoes can be eaten in their original shape. If you crush them, they're mashed potatoes. Strained and mixed with milk, these become soup. Floating among images of the tremendous number of elegant French dishes, great quantities of Chinese recipes, let alone sausage, pizza, sushi and curries, design is compelled to silence for a while, because once we've labeled these cuisines and dishes as design, we're stepping into an incredible maze.

![](images/03fc21b38b6bd66d3be43679f2cccd44472d396ef970b724daee334be65a42f0.jpg)

What about macaroni then? After the grain is reduced to powder, this kind of pasta is shaped into manmade forms. Macaroni is an object that has already undergone some preparation, so because we're just giving form to a powdered material, it doesn't really matter what shape we give it. Therefore, the process of shaping it can be called design, right? But the macaroni shapes we're all used to have not deviated from their traditional forms, nor do they seem to have gone through frequent style changes as have cars and clothing. Why? Because in shaping macaroni, there must be an interesting interface design that unites the act of eating and the raw materials for food. Is the designer present in the process? If not, is there some connection in our future? I was intrigued with this question for a while, and developed my interest through the Architects' Macaroni Exhibition.

The idea for this exhibition came to me when the Japan Institute of Architects asked me to represent the creativity of its members in some way that would make it more understandable to the general public. It's surprising that most people don't correctly understand either the occupation or the individual character of the architect. Probably because each project of a great architect is in itself so luminous, so overwhelming, it's hard for the public to understand his or her individual talents. So my thought was that if I asked architects to move to the periphery of the kitchen and compete with one another on a theme that even ordinary people could appreciate, the creativity of the architectural profession, the differences among the concepts of individual architects as well as their originality, would be revealed to all.

Macaroni was the theme of this competition. Creating a blueprint for macaroni seemed simple, but in fact, it was quite difficult. Why is macaroni shaped the way it is, as a short hollow tube? Naturally, there are specific reasons. First, there is heat conduction. Macaroni is boiled. If it were solid rather than hollow, the core would never cook thoroughly enough. So macaroni has a uniform thickness and no core.

Second, there is the area to be covered with sauce. As macaroni is nearly flavorless, it needs plenty of sauce, and ample surface area for that. The hollow tube design creates inner surface area as well. Some macaroni has grooves on the surface. This tactic may also be understood as a means to increase the surface area and make it easier to fully apply the sauce.

Third, there's ease of production. This is an industrial commodity; it is crucial that it have a shape that is easy to manufacture. Conventional hollow macaroni is made by squeezing the pasta material out of a hole that looks like a circle written with a fat marker. Of course it's important that it look delicious and boast a certain simplicity that allows for our long relationship with the product without our growing weary of it. Hollow macaroni fulfills all of these requirements.

Incidentally, the Asian counterparts to macaroni in Japan are string-shaped masterpieces of udon (wheat noodles) and soba (buckwheat noodles). In Italy they have spaghetti, but the simplicity of udon and soba is more Oriental. The square cross-section, created by slicing the noodle dough with a kitchen knife, is a perfect fit for chopsticks. The way we pull the noodles from their immersion in the broth, and slurp them up is based on the idea of balancing the amount of noodles and broth while eating. If you eat too slowly, the broth is left behind. The diner needs to have some skill as well. The correct way is to take in the noodles with some air, quietly slurping, with a refined "sluh" sound.

Given this perspective, it's clear that macaroni, though simple in shape, also has complex criteria. We might call it "edible architecture." In fact, in Italy many distinguished designers have taken up the subject of macaroni. Giorgetto Giugiaro and Philippe Starck have created interesting macaroni designs. However, instead of elbowing out conventional macaroni, these maestros' designs occupy only a minor position in commercial production. Most macaroni shapes are all too typical: hollow, twisted, shell, ribbon, alphabet...

Explaining this background to 20 Japanese architects and designers, I asked them to come up with their own macaroni designs. Each design was then displayed with a model 20 times the original size, as well as a suitable recipe devised by the food critic and illustrator Hideko Kogure.

Now I would like to present some of the solutions. The architects who tackled the subject so earnestly proposed the following macaroni designs.

![](images/65e145b9d5425bc64e613c2da3f841dca08860737acb348c0141185081fc4964.jpg)

## Norihide Imagawa: SHE & HE

Norihide Imagawa is an expert in structural design. He's the one international architects very often commission as a partner on projects they do in Japan. Imagawa's macaroni has a convex-concave mesh on one side and is flat on the other, so that each side has a different surface area. As the expansion rate of one side gains advantage over the other when it is boiled, the macaroni warps. Shaped like a pair of icons, one female, one male, the piece warps to suggest some eroticism.

The recipe Kogure created for this macaroni is Seafood Soup and Pasta. The SHE & HE pasta is poured into a hodgepodge of seafood and tomatoes, mirroring society itself, with its mix of people and all kinds of things bustling and jostling about together. It's a miniature version of society, with the pasta vitalized only as it warps.

![](images/03cbe32b191f86a39a714ef5c51f8465c270a21fca5eb1a03486e9c6aa3c20ae.jpg)

# adasu he: AVE RIPPLE.LOOP.SURF.

Tadasu Ohe refuses to design architecture that trumpets its concept, instead exercising his skills by bringing the issues into an elegant, sophisticated space. His macaroni proposal, based on the motif of waves, included three types: ripple, loop, and surf. The photo shows RIPPLE. The ring part maintains the look of ordinary macaroni and the grooves are designed to hold the broth.

Kogure's recipe for this macaroni is Ravioli: Fruit of the Sea. Sealed between two layers of wavy pasta is time itself. "Fry mussels, salmon and scallops in butter, steam them in white wine and add egg yolk. Seal the mixture tightly between the waves." As the ravioli hovers between plate and palate, you can imagine the flowing waves of time and the cosmos. According to Ohe, this dish could convince you that there is nothing in the world that follows a straight course.

## Akio Okumura: i flutte

Akio Okumura is known for residential designs based on rational thought, including the installation of photovoltaic (solar energy) systems, and for his profound designs of other objects in the living environment, such as furniture and fireplace surroundings. This architect has long studied how we live. Okumura's macaroni design satisfies the teeth's desire for the density of al dente and the tongue's love of the smoothness of noodles. The rising crest of a wave is designed to be cooked just enough to retain a somewhat firm texture, while the part of the wave that tumbles down upon the shore is cooked soft and smooth. His solution generates both of these textures simultaneously. We can easily imagine their graceful dance in soup, and the bite-size penne are easily eaten with a fork.

Truffle Pasta is the attendant recipe. The word "truffle" symbolizes elegance, grace and exclusiveness. If this pasta is ever commercially produced, both the macaroni and its recipe will surely whet our appetite the most.

![](images/2e63fe79e2d1deabcd06de1306542e7a8060b000c16534c70100e019785662bc.jpg)

## Kaoru Kasai: OTTOCO

The art director Kaoru Kasai participated in this project as a guest. His extremely delicate sensibility is usually occupied in advertising for popular imported spirits and tea. His work, exuding tranquility in every respect, represents one of the sophisticated elements of today's Japanese graphic design. While working towards a design for cylindrical macaroni, he came up with a solution shaped like a classical Japanese dance mask, with a familiar protruding mouth. Kasai says we should imagine the little mouths exhaling the steam of very hot soup.

The recipe is Pie Stuffed with Pasta and Carbonara Sauce. Because the pasta is comically shaped, the recipe was meant to baffle our expectations. When the box is opened on the table, the pasta pops up from a pie, and every charming mouth exhales a little puff of steam.

![](images/afedfcb106a7f021ec6f01c072f90c526dd640372b4751916b9cadf5a757d3f4.jpg)

## Kengo Kuma: Semi Constructive

Kengo Kuma takes an abstract approach. He calls spaghetti "non-constructive," in contrast to "constructive" macaroni. Macaroni has a shape, spaghetti does not. Today's architecture must strive for non-constructive spaghetti, transcending constructive tangibility. So Kuma's object is to disorganize the constructive property of macaroni. Aiming to obscure the border between the two kinds of pasta, he finds a solution of endless unity with strings of spaghetti. His solution is articulate and meticulous in how it reflects his philosophy in architecture.

The recipe for Kuma's macaroni is Cold Pasta with Scallops and Caviar, an interpretation that claims that a cool dish is the most appropriate for making a cool observation about the wavering border between the constructive and the non-constructive. Immerse the boiled pasta in ice water, garnish it with slices of scallops and scoops of caviar and sprinkle it with plenty of virgin olive oil before serving. Might its flavor surpass the spaghetti border?

![](images/b12d1f7d3bdc3474c9eb43d0f5dcd50df47d667be69690d6a2e3f71e0600c2e2.jpg)

## Atelier Zo: MACCHERONI

The studio Atelier Zo is known for building architecture that radiates human warmth. Staying aloof from cold modernism and avoiding the clash of harried hours, they do their job at their own pace and with their own sensibilities in the bosom of tranquil nature. Their macaroni proposal gets its twisted shape from the human hand: Form a piece of dough into a bunch and quickly twist it perpendicularly. Voilà. This design brings to mind a picnic with family or friends, enjoying one another's company, the appearance of the fresh pasta in a big dish raising our expectations. They say anyone can make this shape. The recipe for Tortellini Pomodoro is a genuine Italian dish, but the roughly hand-shaped macaroni seems typically Japanese.

## Kanji Hayashi: Serie Macchel'occhi (Macaroni  Eye)

Out of all the participants, Kanji Hayashi, who studied architecture in Rome and is married to an Italian, is the most knowledgeable about Italy. He says, though, that the longer he stayed there, the more conscious he became of Japan. As Hayashi faced the Italians' powerful love for their country day after day, and their firm conviction in their deepest core that Italy is the most pleasant, beautiful country in the world, a strong desire to counter that claim began to simmer within. Hayashi's macaroni has a cross section that reads &), or "eye." As the first macaroni he saw was the tube type, he was particular about creating a hollow macaroni while working to find a shape that would still yield the al dente consistency: soft but with texture. He assures us that even in a small factory in Sicily this type could be manufactured. The slicing of a long tube allows for various lengths: 20 cm Lungo, 7-8 cm Rigatoni and Penne, 4 cm Standard, and flat Letter. The recipe for Squid Ink and Saffron Sauce displays the "" pieces in stark relief.

![](images/f9a4161c1b5bfb2b5103c48040c7cb97d267256f8c7b334d36e0eba31c47845f.jpg)

## Mayumi Miyawaki: Punching Macaroni

Mayumi Miyawaki says that if an architect is designing macaroni, it should show in the pasta's shape. His proposal calls for punching out a solid square pattern, an intelligible form that bears some mark of the architect. On the other hand, he has set its thickness at one millimeter with due consideration to the final appearance of the cooked macaroni; it would waver voluptuously on the plate and be plenty ready for the sauce.

Miyawaki has designed a great number of residential buildings. According to him, the architect's occupation destines him to design buildings one at a time, each of which is a first experience. Therefore, when he was offered an opportunity to design macaroni, which used to be only a food to him, he was happy to take it on. Anyone engaged in residential design has to be extremely familiar with the kitchen and with cooking. Miyawaki wanted to make a crucial contribution to the project, so he proposed not only the macaroni design but also its recipe, Quattro Formaggi, which satisfied "Chef Kogure" as well.

![](images/fa5bb23453d30e220b622488a83796c9381a0ce478d5d39ade7336c7e0d4c89e.jpg)  
From top left to right: Macaroni designed by Noriyuki Tanaka, Kazumasa Nagai, Shoji Hayashi. From bottom left to right: Macaroni designed by Jun Harada and Hiroyuki Wakabayashi, and the book for the exhibition Architecture and Macaroni.

This is how we ended up with 20 new macaroni designs. It wasn't long before our Japanese Architects' Macaroni Exhibition gained notoriety in Italy and was invited by the pasta machine maker "PAVAN" to join in the Milan pasta fair in 1997.

The goal of this exhibition was to showcase the creativity of architects. Macaroni was simply a secondary theme. However, the architects' solutions are more or less producible. And the exhibition did achieve some popularity, at least at the pasta affair in Italy. But we haven't had any offers from manufacturers to commercially produce any of these designs. Pasta manufacturing machines are on an even larger scale than I expected. When I saw those machines in Italy, I realized anew that the image of food design I had entertained at the beginning of this project was too romantic. I came to recognize that macaroni has a specific design based on sophisticated planning. The various gaps between my expectations and reality included the mammoth quantity produced and the degree of elaboration and exactitude in planning.

![](images/896ed107de09101ddea3aacd3bc2bd3b1031f3b9b036b468e06b7d0d22be4c65.jpg)  
Widely available regular macaroni. Collective wisdom that designers and architects cannot overcome in a short time.

A design that is fairly good but not excellent will never motivate the dynamics of commercial production or the market. The survivors in today's macaroni market are strong in design, masterpieces that have been supported and won their place either on the plate at home or for the efficiency of their production. By dint of their happy passion in pursuing flavor, the Italians have sustained the evolution of macaroni. The various macaroni shown in the photos above are available around the world today. Seeing them afresh, I was profoundly moved.

As I have said, some very strong design lurks in the objects around us that have gone unnoticed. Even architects, full of curiosity and intelligence, who see themselves as professionals skilled at problem solving, find no easy way into the world of macaroni.

If we were to trace the long path from production of the grain that is its raw material to the moment when steam is rising from it on the plate, we would surely find a "macaroni tale" on a global scale.

This realization was the fruit of our project.

## 2 HAPTIC

Awakening the Senses

![](images/49143640c1876f33a0b8ac95e5a6e4ec6dc7e975a48711f9eca78c1b5ca72790.jpg)

# HAPTIC

Exhibition first held in Tokyo, Takeo Paper Show 2004

Awakening the Senses

## Designing the Senses

In 2004 I planned and produced a project entitled the HA PTIC Exhibition. The term "haptic" means relating or pleasant to the sense of touch. For the exhibition title, HAPTIC is used as a guideword of sorts. To put it more plainly, the term here indicates an attitude that takes into consideration how we perceive things with our senses. While dealing with shape, color, material and texture is one of the more important aspects of design, there is one more: it's not the question of how to create, but how to make someone sense something. We might call this creative awakening of the human sensors "the design of the senses."

Two HAPTIC logos.   
The one on the left is made from animal hairs on silicone.   
The one on the right is drawn with cultured mold fungus.

![](images/29af735070fbc0d4fe6e5529bf1e5476d20a796d22f3bf92163c6bfd5a768712.jpg)

A human being is a bundle of senses working hard to perceive the world. Eyes, ears, skin and the others are called sensory receptors, but the images carried by these words are much too passive for sensory organs. Human sensors are boldly open to the world. They aren't "receptors," but active, positive organs. An unlimited number of invisible sensory tentacles sprouting from the brain are exploring the world. Let's think about human beings with this image in mind.

Taking that realm as a field of design, we came up with an experiment we called the HAPTIC Exhibition.

For this exhibition, I asked various creators to design an object not based on form or color, but motivated primarily by "haptic" considerations.

No sketching was allowed. Instead, they started designing something that would tickle the human senses. We invited participants from multidisciplinary fields, including architecture, product design, fashion, graphics, interior design and textiles. Also invited were a traditional Japanese plasterer, an electrical appliance manufacturer and a science-and-technology expert. Once they had assimilated the aim and intention of the exhibition, all were perfectly ready for the task.

Observe the environment, or ecology, of the senses. We deliberately take the results obtained through our senses and turn them to the processes of making things and communicating. This attitude reveals a new field that unites design, science and the senses. This exhibition does not stand for nostalgia. It's creation headed straight for the future. Thanks to our distinguished participants, the haptic concept has gained complete clarity in a single burst in this exhibition. I'll now introduce several of their pieces. I hope you enjoy these haptic presentations with every infinitesimal hair of your senses erect.

## The HAPTIC Exhibition

The photograph on page 68 shows a close-up of the "H"of the exhibition logo. I drew the letters by planting porcine bristles on a silicone surface. I designed this spiny logo to introduce the concept to potential participants. Does it give you a little chill? When we think about design, it's harder than we might expect to start designing without thinking first about color and shape. Take a coffee cup for instance. Any designer would be itching to do a sketch as the first phase of the process. Not for this exhibition. I asked all the participants to start from the point of thinking on how that coffee cup could stimulate and awaken the senses, before ever coming up with an image for its form or anything else.

![](images/2c2fe608323d1fb6569fa24584b6597f9e42328c229f0b5ec7bbec6d259f02f9.jpg)

HAPTIC Exhibition. Tokyo, Japan April 2004   
This exhibition was planned as part of the Takeo Paper Show 2004. The subtitle is "Awakening of the Five Senses." The exhibition was an experiment : searching for unexplored design fields in "feeling" rather than in form.

![](images/aa609b1117dbfd79ea70545c86c6a474e1fd151a6254100cb61f06cc756e2405.jpg)

## Kosuke Tsumura: Kami Tama

The fashion designer Kosuke Tsumura designed some lanterns. After we asked the creators to participate, we gave them information about several new technologies applicable to design and which we'd already conducted some research into. Tsumura chose the hair implant technology and applied it to traditional Japanese lanterns in cooperation with a wig maker. Because the paper used for traditional lanterns isn't strong enough to support implants without tearing, Tsumura pasted a reinforcing silk mesh base onto the paper and tied the hair to the base in a variety of amusing styles, including a bob and a medium-length cut. But the hairy surfaces, with their tiny perforations that you can see so well when the light shines through, look slightly eerie. Some call them "devil lanterns." Leaving preferences aside, Tsumura's lanterns wouldn't ever have come into existence without the perspective of the underlying question: how do we kindle the sense of touch?

![](images/b08cb92b3d43c6ec069791ab41249ce81cdcba49cf3b1089e63e50b28346200b.jpg)

![](images/144a914710429688b798aa05b8f438cb04c8f84021de0b8770f54f2cb21e4271.jpg)

![](images/dc36ab77de76de2729f790f7dddf7a697e244c941413692a690c9e2e5fe8adc8.jpg)

This solution was based on Japan's traditional Gifu paper lanterns. Silk was attached to paper to reinforce it and craftsmen manually attached hairs one by one using wig techniques of a professional implanter. Hairstyles can be created freely according to the capabilities of individual hair artists. These lanterns give you a slightly eerie feeling since they seem to be almost alive, but if you focus on "how you feel," you will be able to recognize that here is an unexplored field of design.

![](images/73f892a6f4e7db0a392ee94d02bb001a08da2ee0924a60e5e01676445d59b1df.jpg)

## Shin Sobue: Tadpole Coasters

Graphic designer Shin Sobue designed coasters. Layed out, they look like upended Petri dishes at some biological research institute. They bring to mind spilt water swelled with surface tension. Tadpole-like patterns can be seen inside. Sobue once told me the story of a friend's shocking experience, a story that freezes the senses. He had been taking a summer nap, but was awakened by an itching on his elbow. What sight met his eyes but a butterfly laying her eggs. Not only that, but the eggs were tightly packed in a neat geometric

Analyzed and controlled chill.   
Designs that observe and use that power to awaken the human senses.

![](images/5c981de6872aa320927a682ecd600377fde82e43f5c84118d0da1e09c2132c1c.jpg)

pattern. Totally surprised, he brushed off the eggs hurriedly. I can imagine the shudder. The feeling of something bionic like an insect's eggs, geometrically arrayed on your own elbow.. It really does give you a chill. This story provided Sobue with the idea for these coasters, with their tadpole-shaped objects inside the transparent silicone. It's not the first thing that would come to mind. Notice how broad the conceptual field becomes when the haptic aspect is the focus.

![](images/c5da1944fe41ef7d2b67bf817cf790c196574354e6e762c827f577a0ef2fcb71.jpg)

![](images/a50d3ee69f52d346e74fcc1e8343d94c36e76b1896ece93ba114dbf7196abe0f.jpg)

![](images/7737b67926be1fcfff313c414aa4dc07afffe0dc3571d3a49c5a454244e99ce6.jpg)

## Jasper Morrison: Wall Clock

The product designer Jasper Morrison, in his thorough pursuit of a universal form, proposed this wall-mounted clock. It's molded out of a very soft paper called "WAVYWAVY."
