The paper is extremely thin and frail, but once it's fixed neatly to the wall and its hands are moving, it gains creative and structural stability. The appearance of the soft paper immediately after it's been molded is particularly haptic.

When I first asked Morrison to participate in the HAPTIC project, he had a superb response. "Haptic means to make the senses drool, doesn't it?" he said. When we're hungry, we salivate at the sight of an inviting dish or savory grilled meat. That's a gustatory response, but Morrison meant haptic as the experience of seeing something that made all the senses "drool." What a fantastic metaphor.

![](images/3027f5b75e65e93ef3e6fbef0a19a6fac3d0175beee830d75be209bdecab11e4.jpg)

![](images/44f8808c2da0c2b00d3fe5a757c04f2f318dbdac23d40ca952502bcdf0138591.jpg)

![](images/08b2b05829bd9f99e35c2e69dd78b7d2d7d94f7257ce9122ee04aa4c6cad7e19.jpg)

A watering mouth is a taste response to sizzling meat. What kind of design can induce the reaction of a watering mouth from all five senses?

![](images/7480a6bc994b4b1fe16d1fe5d9bba3fe5ec5f6ef10ea68c6f23003504accee47.jpg)

## Toyo Ito :

## High Five with a Hand from the Future—Gel Doorknob

The architect Toyo Ito designed a doorknob. It's made out of a very soft material called "hydro-gel." Conventional doorknobs are hard, but Ito's goes "squish" when you grasp it. Ito's architectural work has always tried to represent architecture in a sort of abstract manner, by eliminating tangibility as far as is practical.

For instance, Ito thinks walls don't need to have massive materiality. A wall could be something thin, a membrane that partitions space.

![](images/d0d8db73e8adad2788d029372d527c367d36aecfbc6c33fe781779afae88a787.jpg)

As long as it's strong enough to support the ceilings, even something like plastic wrap would make a fine wall. That's how far Ito has manifested the perception of an abstract handling of architectural space. When I invited him to participate in the HAPTIC project, I posed this question: "Your buildings are very abstract. But the people who live there have a very physical, vital existence. Perhaps their beards grow just a bit each day, or they excrete sticky sweat. What do you think about the physical aspects of human beings?"

![](images/57c6735eb10f40eacc30a2a863e239d9eb2767b88ceea90ff8601b5a0c35dbd6.jpg)  
The creator uses hydro-gel, with an image of continuously changing forms and an infinite ability to return to its original shape. This handle would welcome you home with a gentle high five.

He replied, "We cannot abstract from living flesh." Ito has a long history of abstract representation, but has just recently begun to take notice of physicality. Still, he continued, "Though I'm interested in material objects, I'm not going back to the point of saying that I like the feel of wood." That's such an Ito Toyo way of dealing with physical materials. If we get into a nostalgic mindset, we're likely to cuddle up to the satisfaction of familiar materials. But what if we left nostalgia behind? If we opened up our sensors to physical objects, wouldn't that allow us to sense another reality? Might that be a way of handling physical materials that is directed by neither nostalgia nor abstraction? Ito wonders, "Should we call it a 'haptic abstract"?"

![](images/696c25d71c7371cc9a5431451a696dca09300b554c071ab07f6237a8ea2016da.jpg)

![](images/33f0e23df667985e89bd7c18daf14b2e89b039f3516dd8c06b506a205c88e72e.jpg)

![](images/1dbd34e0f04840061e7c31612f8ef3e4f4ba121e622e5e21b62dd0d629c8bbfd.jpg)

## Panasonic Design Company: Gel Remote Control

For the HAPTIC Exhibition, the electrical appliance manufacturer Panasonic Design Company proposed a remote control that when switched off droops as if it were dead. Have you ever seen the melting clocks of the Spanish Surrealist Salvador Dali, with one hanging limply in the branch of a tree? The Panasonic remote control looks like that when it's off.

But when it's switched on, it becomes animated. First, it begins to breathe, as if asleep, its belly rising and falling, resuscitated from death to breathing life. When someone reaches out to use it, the sensor instantly feels the hand's approach and the whole thing begins to glow. Then it stiffens. Precisely attuned for use, it solidifies to the perfect hardness. A concept model, this remote control is not ready for commercial production, but it's a unique idea.

The focus in this design is not on its appealing form, button layout or logo placement, but the way it feels to the touch. This is a great example to show how, with the birth of the haptic focus, the breadth of design's possibilities expands in unexpected directions.

![](images/e19ae5fcc384024bdc912d49b0f37edf28380622d6dee4b360e2ac55030adb72.jpg)

![](images/a7e8fe74d0391da5160c39c04646c6d061fcbfc57dc2feadc8343049bca8e5bb.jpg)  
It looks completely dead when turned off, but when turned on, it looks as if it's taking a nap as its belly slowly rises and falls. If a human hand comes closer, it instantly wakes up and stiffens.

![](images/9911213e665eaf3ae6d8065a0c671842f81fcc6b16dd3b31725a7cb7674b267d.jpg)

![](images/270bf61a7ed641aea34bfe48954395d52a0d8b10ffc234f02e0077a2e41fd1a7.jpg)

## Naoto Fukasawa: Juice Skin

These fruit juice packages are the work of the product designer Naoto Fukasawa. As his design is very easy to understand, it probably needs no interpretation from me. The banana juice package is particularly superb. For this, Fukasawa used the familiar Tetra Prisma® Aseptic package. The gentle angles of the packaging reveal something in common with the feeling of holding a banana. When a stem is added to the flap, it can't be seen as anything but a banana. Fukasawa has dexterously edited only the unique features of his subject.

![](images/a0c0396d4c77ef7e603a76a7a1383082e44c16c804810c4677b07a8e9edcabdd.jpg)

![](images/8dbfccb7168ef6014a9c3b791f6eb6a6d42bd425855a6f2b5d9557a73bcf9643.jpg)

The series includes kiwi juice. If you were to shave the fuzz off of a kiwi, you would be left with a dark green-skinned fruit. When fibers are applied to paper using flocking technology, a very kiwi-like texture is produced. The attached straw makes it even clearer that this is a package of juice. There's soymilk too. Its package has the rough cheesecloth-like texture of tofu. No doubt drinking from this package would feel very strange, as if you were drinking soymilk directly from a piece of tofu.

![](images/61a002dbb0ef83c725cc12c58ec3558f9a24f70d86ec9c2685c1a7500b3a56dd.jpg)

To design is to "build" a structure with an image inside the mind of the recipient. In this case, the materials are not only external stimulation but also massive amounts of memories awakened by stimulation. Designing highlights subtle differences between recalled memories and reality.

![](images/3c6da9f74ca52697e42005917384739f0a9f20e362b2e413b499179ca2a0de12.jpg)

![](images/2e00f6f903c4c8bf4ebc8382210975d1b207d056ede667d8fe8a2ec09d2c5849.jpg)

## Shuhei Hasado: Geta

Next come geta, traditional Japanese raised wooden sandals that are worn barefoot. The plasterer Shuhei Hasado works from his studio in Hida-Takayama, a mountainous area in central Japan. He is a superb plasterer who has taken the championship at the World Skills Competition.Envisioning the possibilities in combining traditional technology with creative frontiers, he has also taken on various new challenges. When I visited him at his studio, he showed me several samples of his experimental wall-plastering methods. His participation in the HA PTIC project was covering the surfaces of several pair of geta. The geta shown here received a moss treatment. Because geta are worn barefoot, imagine how it would feel to place your bare feet on this moss —the feeling of saturated moss.…

![](images/4680c9c07530c71e54694d7a17424ebb477a3daf2c93ce9108d02c0a44b73732.jpg)

There's also a pair of geta here that seem exactly as if the floor of a pine forest were applied directly to the soles. Before they started wearing shoes, humans walked barefoot. The sole of the foot acted as an interface with the ground. In those days, through our sensitive feet, we truly picked up a great variety of information from the ground. We might have also learned that certain insects were beneath our feet or that if we dug in a certain spot, we'd find a spring. Amazing sensitivity lies dormant in the soles of our feet. Looking at these geta, I feel a revival of this kind of sensitivity. Do you?

![](images/09e09382ebd4ec52b6427c83993a8134920e2f63b4eae9432fcf50defa303aa0.jpg)

![](images/79b0524906e3b88618de9d782596f07e5853b982bcecae9a9a9e2a5708ee5a8d.jpg)

There is a reason why the bottoms of human feet are sensitive. The bottoms of the feet are the only body parts that always come into contact with the ground. They are used to detect delicate, detailed information at all times. Today, the bottoms of feet are covered by socks and shoes, so these sensors are now wrapped in covers.

![](images/493703573bde6689be3cc782a3c891d6d0f131a97bc291592b59c60b7e9c8e5e.jpg)

## Events on the Skin

Let's think a little about the human senses. They're often signified with terms like "the five senses" or "the five sensory organs." I gave the HA PTIC Exhibition the subtitle "The Awakening of the Five Senses." But who on earth came up with the figure five? Is it meant to apply to the simple icons of eyes, ears, nose, mouth and hands? As for the sense of touch, brushing softly against something with the fingers is not the same as grasping a doorknob tightly with the whole hand. "The sense of pressure" would be a better description for the latter. And as for the sense of taste, the feeling of licking whipped cream with the tip of the tongue is completely different from that of packing a whole slice of bread into the mouth. Minutely examined, the senses would number at least a hundred, not five.

On the other hand, we might be able to limit the senses more by narrowing them down. If we think of the sense of taste as a combination of smell and the oral sense of touch, the sense of taste becomes part of each smell and touch. If we set the hurdle for discerning separate delicate stimuli as language, then sight, hearing and touch could pass, but smell and taste are a little iffy.

The physicist Hermann Ludwig Helmholtz (1821-1894) said, "Everything is an event on the skin." Come to think of it, the sense of sight is a response to the stimulus of light on the retina, a circular membrane 4 cm in diameter. The sense of hearing is likewise a response to movement of air that's perceived by the eardrum, a membrane only 8 mm in diameter set deep in the ear. The senses of smell and taste are responses to the components of matter that touch the surface of the mucous membranes in the nose and tongue. So membrane perceptions are not limited to those occurring on the skin. All human perception originates in the responses of membranes to things physical, stimulation transmitted to the brain through the nervous system. A human being is like a rubber ball wrapped in an extremely delicate membrane. Different areas on the ball's surface elicit different senses. Our image of the world is based on the multifarious stimuli that are perceived on the membrane and transmitted to the ball's nucleus, the brain. Design is a service for these sensitive membranes.

In a way, the five senses are interrelated. The sense of sight is not self-contained, but works in conjunction with the senses of hearing and of smell. Now that I think about it, our senses have developed in concert. If we think about the process by which the senses of newborn babies gradually begin to function, we might be able to understand their interrelation.

We often say that babies can't see, but it doesn't mean that their eyelids are shuttered. Surely the light comes through the lenses of their eyes and the retina is exposed to the light, and then transmits communication signals to their brains via their optic nerves. Therefore, saying that babies can't see is really saying not that their eyes don't function properly, but that their brains can't understand the meaning of the signals that are transmitted. In the same way, babies who can't see also can't hear or distinguish flavors. The sensors of humans who have just come into the world do function, but their brains are unable to discern the meanings of the stimuli. By "meaning," here, I mean the value to the person himself or herself. This is something that is understood by and by. A baby instinctively suckles his mother, drinks her milk, hears her voice, and feels her skin, gaining in experience until the visual stimuli of his mother and the scent of her milk, the feel of her breasts and so on are bundled up together, acquire value, and develop into awareness. This is how I think consciousness comes about. Furthermore, experiences in hearing, touching, seeing, tasting, and smelling acquire meaning all together. It seems to me that hearing, touching and seeing are interrelated, so come to fruition simultaneously.

This might resemble the way a three-dimensional object becomes clear with "binocular vision." The instant the brain manages to interpret the composite visual impression captured by the eyes, which are working together, the image abruptly becomes a vivid solid figure. Likewise, when sensory stimuli assembled by more than one sense come together, fitting tightly in the brain, an image is born. Neuroscientists term these qualities of sensory experience "qualia."

Mossy geta soaked with water appeared in the previous section. We come to a general idea of what it would feel like to wear them, without actual experience. If you were to lick the table you're using right now, what would it taste like? You can pretty much imagine the taste, even without following through with the action. Even if you did lick it, there shouldn't be much of a difference between the actual result and your speculation. Or what would it feel like to have the mike you're using to give a speech fill your mouth? This too would feel much as we expect. Why can we assume without actually doing? Because since infancy, we have had a tremendous number of sensory experiences through which they have built up interrelations. Our experiences and memories of licking, touching, smelling the world, through which we have attached meaning to it, have given us the background for our senses.

There is a famous question in the field of sensory research. If a person were born blind, then suddenly gained the ability to see, and you placed before him both a cube and a ball, could he tell the difference, just by sight, without touching them? The researchers say no. If it were a shape that the subject had understood through touching, the shape might be interconnected with sight due to the occurrence of a certain "synaesthesia" (a sensation that normally occurs in one sense modality when another is stimulated). But the meaning of a visual stimulus experienced for the first time has no ties to any other sensory perception. The meaning of the stimulus of the light that is received by the eye has not yet been evaluated in relation to other sensory perceptions. It is not until one has touched the shape, or tried smelling it, that there is created a correspondence with other sensory stimuli, the person begins to understand the meaning of the light stimuli and the ability to see is established.

I used to believe that design was information architecture, and also that this architecture was built in the brain of an information recipient. Recently I've come to think that, although the materials of that architecture's construction are indeed the information brought from the outside by the sensory organs, at the same time some very important building blocks are also the recollected experiences, the memories, awakened by these external stimuli. People imagine the world and interpret it when outside stimuli awaken the mountain of their internally stored memories.

The action of walking is the forward motion of the body created by the pendulum-like swinging of the limbs and the body's balance with gravity, but we do not undertake each instance of this activity in any planned manner. Except when we think to, say, step on every crack in the sidewalk or take care not to step on any ants, we usually don't examine every external stimulus involved in walking before we take the next step. It's more like we are recalling the walking that's stored in our memory, while not paying much attention to the actual walking we're doing. Basically the same can be said of seeing and hearing, too. When you meet someone new, you don't reserve a fresh look for his face, voice and features, but focus your attention only on the disparities you discern from an internal collation of the vast number of memories about human beings in your recollection; you look at him as, after all, just a human being, save for those disparities. The parts you can bundle into the idea of human being are represented by the memories you've stored up. A huge amount of information is transferred to the brain through the sensory organs, yet our interpretations of the world are not based on new information alone, but on the collation of new information with copious memory. Our sensory perceptions are incessantly fusing together in the brain, amalgamating and interrelating.

Design is not only concerned with color and form. Research into how we sense color and form, or research into the senses, is a critical subject of design. And observations of how the human senses work will give design new pointers.

![](images/6cb8573eaf79e224f674a6e6bbe5c9440dabb8602a224cd32a0167e0496362f3.jpg)

## Yasuhiro Suzuki: Cabbage Bowls

Let's go back to the haptic designs. The artist Yasuhiro Suzuki made cabbages out of paper. He created the shapes by accurately molding real cabbage leaves out of silicone, then reproducing these in paperclay. The leaves are designed as bowls. All of the leaves in a single cabbage have been reproduced, so Suzuki's reconstituted cabbage weighs almost as much as a real one. It must be fun to use these bowls at a dinner party. Imagine the feeling of holding a paper cabbage-leaf bowl. It's strange to the touch. The human mind takes pleasure in complex things like these, which carry a plethora of information.

![](images/83554aa23fe8bcbfa8ff44a3061073ed5c0854cb010c628c3f9422e20d911779.jpg)

Speaking of the pleasant feeling of complicated information, I'm reminded of the time I went to Bali on business with the interior designer Takashi Sugimoto. There are plenty of fancy resort hotels there, so I kind of figured we'd be staying at one of them. I was a little disappointed to hear from Sugimoto that he'd reserved rooms at an old lodging complex. "Those new resorts wouldn't be very interesting," he said, by way of explanation. The site of the more mature lodge covered a vast area, dotted with cottages and paved with old stone in every direction. Guests walk barefoot there, and I was rather surprised to find myself enjoying the feeling. When I thought about why that was, I realized that the stone pavement was worn away by decades of barefoot travelers, polishing the stones as they tread. My feet rejoiced at the feel of the stones. It was a wonderful feeling of familiaritylike cuddling my old cat-that came to me through the soles of my feet. When I think about it, I sense an extreme delicacy in the feeling I got from stones worn away by the walking of fellow human beings. It's hard to explain, but it's probably a composite of minute discrepancies from stone to stone. Intuitively, I felt that the stones held a great amount of information. If I were able to input into my computer all the information coming from those stones, I'm sure it would freeze.

![](images/1acd6995ced1abc7290fc718a6af311a3eb777c8dbe6de3724651e4756ad2b53.jpg)  
Each leaf of the cabbage is to be used as a single vessel. Assembled, the leaves make a whole cabbage; disassembled, the cabbage becomes a set of paper dishes.

![](images/d2524ae2493154c530a1a663e9b83f63ee282c810313b96369d70e6e12ea7e97.jpg)

![](images/59ed7c7ae0fdae6397f85e5d0b21607e6c9ac31833f00401d91ea855cfda494d.jpg)

I realized something then; although today's society is said to be in a state of information overload, in fact it may not be an excess. It's just an overflow of odd and fragmented information in the media. The amount of information in each fragment is in fact quite small. In this slew of half-baked information, isn't the brain oppressed? The stress on the brain isn't because of quantity, but because of limited quality.

Against the backdrop of the evolution of media and its powerful gathering of news material and data, all of the world's happenings are trimmed like a lawn by a mower, with fragments of information flying about from place to place through the media as grass flies through the air. These broken pieces of information adhere to our tofu-like brain like spices sprinkled so thickly that they obscure the entire surface. For a moment, this makes us think we're quite knowledgeable, but information tacked on the surface of the brain doesn't amount to much when you add it all together. Conversely, the amount of information we receive through a sensual, pleasant experience via the soles of our feet is enormous. The human brain likes anything that entails a great amount of information. Its extensive capacity waits eagerly to perceive the world by completely exhausting its great receptive powers. That potential power, though, remains today in a state of extreme constriction and is a source of the information stress we're all under.

![](images/1bc1e1de2121c9f9e8fa88963da5cfa3a77eb3eb62b0c7e5ac2eb67d076c3570.jpg)

## Shunji Yamanaka: Floating Compass

The product designer Shunji Yamanaka devised a floating compass with the structure of a water strider. Yamanaka is a robotics expert. Through designs ranging from robots to smart cards at automatic wickets, he has long worked on the integration of technology and human beings. His design solution for this project is a small compass shaped like a water strider.

Ultra-hydrophobic coating technology makes the surface of the normally absorbent paper highly water repellent. Placed on the surface of the water, it floats like a water strider. In the center of this small and precisely cut paper creature is a small stick of magnetic iron. For the exhibition, the compasses were floated on water in a very thin acrylic basin about 2 mm deep. Controlled by a magnet under the basin, the compasses rotated in harmony, fluent and smooth. This extraordinary accord is really haptic, in that the senses are also evoked by especially smooth sensations, not only those that are hairy or bristled.

![](images/52d302997f0eb893d33c7c4be5b657acc6fca01cd7da8e219ced62b3055bc470.jpg)

![](images/c445380c54a0940cce5d82aa824089b00e98c7fd64d504c82c35c37624c0353c.jpg)

![](images/1ac4691eda0e62fb6361f0a9891e53efe670733b12c7bfff449b23de8d9d81f0.jpg)

![](images/8846ad62100ccb8594a436184b7bdd4691b708df8a790d1ccfbafb3702dde9cb.jpg)

![](images/9341731221fa37c8997b9b3a338171265da04171fbf16c819ece25f42deffcf0.jpg)

![](images/816c736efb600d6ea4016ff136cc26da0855fd5d587dc17cf4e32a19fa1bf654.jpg)

## Matthieu Manche: Mom'n Baby

The French artist Matthieu Manche, who lives in Tokyo, designed an electric outlet. The outlet has several plugs, but as they're not arrayed geometrically, they evoke an organic image of proliferation through successive cell division. Manche used a material employed for artificial body parts involving the replacement of flesh, not just bones and muscles. These are called epitheses. This material is applied externally to supplement damaged or lost body parts like breasts and ears. An expert in crafting epitheses helped finish the outlet so that it has a startling, fleshlike texture, one that can hardly be imagined from its lovely appearance. The first sample actually looked too realistic to be exhibited, so the final product was colored in somewhat abstractly, like a cute Kewpie doll.

![](images/61a9d28178e3a1433d1c67ec10cf5b7b5bf4d14ac71cc7ec1c05bb8e43da35c4.jpg)

![](images/0afb360b90e5b88b38140343f125ad0ceda6a2dd1798c293c6a4d7f7803b19ea.jpg)

![](images/50ebc80c1fe93656527dcecf177933460e8aab9e403d579db277f2b9efde9af8.jpg)

A power outlet gives the impression that it is multiplying through cell division and also looks like a baby emerging from its mother.

![](images/445b8c4be4568bd0ffb9f7e6b844c8ad39b8bb460f0164039e508785ffd2918b.jpg)

![](images/0f157ec661f21c39a4f7fab1939c13691a887a06e3a3d4d5d0b336af62667d1a.jpg)

## Keiko Hirano: Paper Wastebasket

The photograph shows the paper wastebasket produced by the designer Keiko Hirano. Made of vulcanized fibers, when viewed flat, it looks like a paper coffee filter, but is generous in size when the two sides are sewn together. The fibers soften up in water, then harden when dried. Each wastebasket is crumpled by hand. Dry, the wastebasket is so hard that you'd expect it to clatter if it fell. This paper wastebasket is truly haptic in appearance. Imagine the rustle of a ball of paper tossed into it; it's a tactile inspiration.

![](images/52dbdbcb957f809c4c04f6edca25dcdc6028c964bafa796ad1b69e608ca2bbf2.jpg)

Sew together two sides of a shape similar to a coffee filter and soak it in water to soften and shape it by hand. When it dries, it will harden like a rock. Now you have a wastepaper basket.

![](images/74ce9badd51c1b1ddd0850ed59d909983784578f3df53edd2f52f8d3db15c330.jpg)

![](images/68f8f7dd7656b589a1e5a3de2994de6b1b084b5cb2c9e809ee9ca2234c8fa72f.jpg)

![](images/b9891d960873dcf6867b1bb9954497e28ee91243b78705403e746a0aefceb226.jpg)

## Kenya Hara: Water Pachinko

This product is mine. When producing an exhibition, I usually don't create products for it, but this time I broke my own rules by making this "water pachinko" game. (Pachinko is a Japanese form of pinball.)

For this exhibition, we did some preliminary research into various technologies that would be appropriate and available to the participants' designs. At the time, I was so interested in the technology and phenomenon of super-hydrophobic surfaces that I couldn't suppress my creative appetite. The aspirinlike nubs shown in the photograph are cut out of heavy paper, 5 mm thick. I stuck the paper nubs on the base paper, which was mounted on an aluminum panel in order to keep the surface completely level, and sprayed on the super-hydrophobic coating.

![](images/2a9375657acdfc8712398c04c031f7ec2bc0b2630b25d3e3fffba98385aa5363.jpg)

![](images/54518932440ee37e4fe00d9daf8a952cf4eca05724e600e2229c7064e30ca6f5.jpg)

Super-hydrophobic technology was developed to keep snow from accumulating on satellite dishes, because when snow piles up, their antennae don't function as well. The result is the invention of a surface that accumulates neither snow nor rain. The water-repellent phenomenon is called the lotus effect, working on the same principle as water droplets rolling off of lotus leaves. The same phenomenon occurs on paper. If you were to spray the same super-hydrophobic substance on a business card, water droplets couldn't even hold on. That's how strong the water repellent is.

![](images/116f67e5d2590c9794f8d915811f239b89620f5e5c0c5cd4b1b119d4793612b6.jpg)

Just like any pinball machine, this one is at an incline. The player fills a dropper with a good amount of water and lets it fall onto the panel. The droplets roll down, weaving their way between the paper impediments, each in its own way, and sometimes fall into the holes. It's entertaining to watch their meandering movement. Spent water runs through a long narrow trough into a bottle on the floor. Paper and water, naturally mutually attractive, run counter to one another here. The droplets move down the complex paper structure like pinballs, but silently. It's a very haptic scene. As long as the diameter of the droplet doesn't exceed 5 mm, it's relatively tenacious at maintaining its globular shape. But once it grows to over 7 mm, it starts moving like an amoeba, more squirming along than rolling. At one point the droplets may divide, and at another junction, unite. What an exquisite sight.

![](images/40feb53b65b9f4305430fe2ff34f8738d83bb79a9923430bead0fa1e59705781.jpg)

![](images/f4ca54e4b08190f6e95d67d9f3a57be3c8d35a8e0776b2966978cafbf1a5f61a.jpg)

Take some water from the basin in the pipette and squeeze it out at the top of the panel. Countless droplets soundlessly navigate the complicated paper structure treated with water repellent and sprinkled with aspirinlike obstacles.

![](images/888214e557ed438f913459b90f511b5d0909c48c115feb39ca2bb4edd2b0dec7.jpg)

## Masayo Ave : 800 dots—Paperback Cover

Masayo Ave, the product designer working from Milan for no less than 16 years, moved her base to Berlin in 2006, where she also puts great effort into education. She proposed a paperback cover. The paper is printed with foaming ink used for Braille. The pattern is a traditional Japanese one called same-komon (sharkskin dots). Book jackets usually feature patterns and logotypes, putting us in mind of graphic design, but Ave's design stands on simple tactility alone. Actually using it, I found the smooth yet uneven texture really pleasant to the touch. Employing only the sense of touch to effect the design has made a book cover into an extremely comfortable object.

![](images/3e032a8920ea7e43b12b60be31b3dc1cfa3d34d7022f5412b09a6b08ca53e678.jpg)

Based on her participation in the HAPTIC Exhibition, Ave became passionate about the comprehensive development of the concept of haptic, an interest that had existed only subconsciously until the exhibition project. Now she finds herself in charge of a course called Haptic Interaction Design at a Berlin art university. There also seems to be a movement in Berlin to put together a sequel to our exhibition.

![](images/386ba06377b642d94cc116e38a009007bee1d5b7261ae1d4329774c2975b925b.jpg)

## Kengo Kuma: Cast-off Snakeskin Paper Towel

Next is a paper towel proposed by the architect Kengo Kuma. Made of washi (traditional Japanese paper) so thin and light that if you toss it up, it will float in the air for a few moments. The paper is embossed transversely with a texture that, from its scale, suggests a snake about 2 m long. It's hard work to impress such soft paper one sheet at a time, but these towels are in fact finished by hand. Here, they're set neatly on a thick board of Japanese cypress. After washing up, dry your hands with one. That's what they're made for.

![](images/77b16221c390182595bbca25174522376db3146463c43c63b174d3c8036afe26.jpg)

When I talk about them though, people often remark that they're too good to use, that it would be a waste. But if they were not embossed, with a snakeskin pattern or anything else, we'd normally toss them in the trash. If some people think it would be a waste to use a paper towel that has become haptic through embossing, does it mean that the object has thus gained some kind of memorable value? I suppose that it would feel quite elite to dry your hands on a towel like this.

![](images/e27d094414d667721fd0f367c806916e4016a4990ed409bec4b56317358f4cd5.jpg)

A snakeskin pattern was traced and laser cutting was used to make an acrylic model. Japanese washi paper was then pressed against the model to shape and create individual sheets.

![](images/2f73c2f211df3f7133e99d991cb351249a3d089c3b68dfe263ddf86586f05320.jpg)

![](images/06facb5289039a0f158b1ce353cece060af71650bf13fdb117f56792c2dd28af.jpg)

![](images/04ca0352fade67df1b1b808b0b4d380a8f2cc501638ef55f794f392cf54fe403.jpg)

## Reiko Sudo: Gazelles

The textile designer Reiko Sudo designed a table. Actually, it's more like the membrane of a table, made to cover the table structure (an extremely thin iron panel), and be fastened with zippers. Placed flat, it looks like the skin of some four-legged creature.

Sudo engages in the pure design of texture. "I design a texture, but when it reaches the customer, it becomes clothing or a bag," Sudo has said, with some dissatisfaction. At first I thought she was always conscious of the end use when she was designing, but I guess not. When she told me that she designs texture not for clothing or bags, but for its own sake, I finally understood her vision. So I suggested we have a thin, bone-like table produced to maintain the purity of the texture she had designed. We asked a product designer to help with the practical design of the table. It's so thin it looks as if only the surfaces of the table are floating in the air. The name Gazelle probably refers to this thin, fragile presence.

![](images/7aa0bbd7f6868f4406a99c7c75bf8d3518b5cdeff4ee4a4b4652ef2e096e484a.jpg)

![](images/747a5db89730c22cdc08d7c05cf83a95c642800c3398275c0b75e16a68a90c9d.jpg)

In the photo is Sudo's three-dimensional textile design, woven from thread made from the fibers of OjO+ paper, which has brought a number of tables into the world. I'd hate to spill wine on any of them, but the design is certainly a successful haptic interpretation of a table.

Later, I heard that the architect Jun Aoki spread out in an architectural space one of Sudo's textiles that was more than 100 m long. Just imagining the existence of a plane of supple textile at such a scale inside of an architectural space gives me the feeling that some brand new space has been born.

![](images/f5ac567cc615bf2735ffd2e3ae42f2ec66ceb9aa731b327a784ea76ac8b538ed.jpg)

The table on the left is covered with a paper-fiber thread called OJO+, woven with depth, like a building. The table on the right is covered with a combination of Echizen rice paper and rayon velvet. Spots of fluffy velvet form a sporadic pattern.

![](images/464ed2b8f7ce2d858c806bdd64d7a86785f53e1cfb481220f81611dbb1a87038.jpg)

![](images/5025e27875b24c5ccfed122ad5c11f54f2fc831f290e1937a362de12dfe4a278.jpg)

## Kazunari Hattori: Tailed Gift Cards

The art director Kazunari Hattori designed these tailed gift cards. This also unites paper with the wig maker's hair-implant technology.

Ribbons are for presents. They give the package a gorgeous appearance and add glitz to gift giving. Tails lend a pretty mysterious impression. What if there were a tail on a present? Artificial fur comes in all kinds of colors and patterns, so you could express yourself with a striped pattern like those of cats' tails, or skunks'. Each of the tails has room for a message on the flip side. The one in the photograph reads "Sorry."

![](images/75e89fdf3eea51719c0cbf9bcb96c3df1f8f50edfc96dae8170a10c3c73193f9.jpg)

When a tail is attached, a package suddenly carries the image of an animal in a fable. It makes one feel the animal represents the sender. Anthropomorphic images add mysterious sensations to gifts.

![](images/1f9b27643e4bf09604dc915c8f193f00a0c73337b1a4552507eca0a35f9e7eaf.jpg)

## Kenya Hara: Humidifier

Finally, let me describe the humidifier, another of my own products. Like the water pachinko game of the previous section, this humidifier is made of super-hydrophobic paper. When you pour water on the paper, most of the water materializes as droplets. When you shake it, the droplets sway in concert. I devised a humidifier in which all of the water takes globular shapes and so a larger surface area materializes. The exposed surface area on a cup of water vaporizes slowly, but the aggregation of small globular droplets dramatically increases the surface area and vaporizes quickly. In a dry environment the water vaporizes in two or three hours. This humidifier is of enormous significance not only because of its interesting display, but because it consumes absolutely no energy.

![](images/61d99f495f1a4b28dd08fb83ce054186a197a1c5d5830bf8db85006087a07dd2.jpg)

At first, I thought that it would be beautiful if water droplets were tightly packed, each in its own section in a box partitioned with small boards about 5 mm apart. I had to give up on this idea because it was hard to keep the droplets in their own spaces, but imagine the haptic scene: thousands of pearllike water droplets crowded in a small partitioned box. Tap the box lightly and the droplets sway to and fro.

![](images/924ef401c59150a3b62984324d023d067917500e26b0094d06336f3245d55726.jpg)  
When the angle of contact between an object and a water droplet becomes more than 150 degrees, it is called "super water repellent." A water repellent object has different characteristics from something that is waterproof, and when water is dropped on the surface, droplets instantly scatter and become almost completely round. This means the surface area will dramatically increase for a specific volume.

![](images/f51dde7c7b2fc9d97a48cd8ff8966a648d5f56f80a0175e508a811f9f4680b90.jpg)

![](images/ae1d6f444a3e5ffd4896256de46c2f297b8123806b6d3df7f461587441565582.jpg)

![](images/aa7620fb12e8be9e39fe08e27ece41fa6761152e4210f2a320702295a9a8ed16.jpg)

## Sense-driven

Long ago technology began to lead society and the economy. We describe this situation of technology motivating the progress of all sorts of things with the term "technology-driven." Design and technology have faced one another on the question of how to utilize the media and materials produced by progress in scientific technology: Web design for the Internet, the competition in architecture to use new materials, impressive forms in product design using new materials and technologies, computer graphics generated by numbers.

This may just be the appropriate form of design for an era in which technology has been a driving force for development. And we've also enjoyed plenty of superb designs thanks to our technology-driven society. But if we were to reseat the motivation for the manufacture of objects on the side of the senses, we would have a design process that begins not from technology, but from sensory perception, which would progress parallel to the various scientific disciplines. From this we can imagine a world that would be called "sense-driven." We are not able to make our bodies abstract and we should not forget the fact that the greater part of science is about human existence. We used to fill our imaginations with the glamour of achieving happiness by sending a virtual self to live in cyberspace, but we've realized that virtual happiness cannot become real happiness. In time, dust will cover even modern structures designed to be aesthetically light, and products made of new materials will gradually evolve into antiques. We don't know what to do with our physical bodies, which need massages as much as messages.

## The Progress of Technology and the Deterioration of the Senses

Technology has no point unless it subtly awakens and activates the senses of its recipients. Looking around, I notice that on the contrary, people today have been gradually developing thick skins because of technology. They wear elasticized or fleece clothing, sit on comfortable sofas and eat potato chips while watching large-screen TVs. They don't take lessons in cooking or the tea ceremony. They can't even be bothered to arrange flowers in a vase. Thanks to the calculator, they've ceased doing math in their heads or on paper, and are losing the ability to think quickly, and in Japan, the word processor has diminished their memory for the kanji (Chinese characters) we use in our written language. Because there's e-mail, they've stopped writing letters by hand. Common greetings used in written correspondence slip their minds. Losing these phrases means they also lose compassion for others. Even the skill of peeling an apple in one long strip, once common proof of dexterity in Japan, has deteriorated because it's such a nuisance to use a knife.

Leonardo da Vinci created magnificent paintings. There is absolutely no one today who can paint this way. We must believe that this fact is due to the loss of sensitivity and wisdom so obvious in his work. The same can be said of the skills of skinning apples or writing letters by hand. The necessary sensory perceptions and receptivity have begun to fade away.

Marketing is the business of scanning and analyzing the various aspects of peoples' desires. And these aren't only positive desires, but also the unconscious desire towards idleness: slacking off. In convenience stores and supermarkets, products are in constant competition. Only those that sell survive. The development of POS technology has spurred this trend. Not long ago, people would buy roasted coffee beans at a shop, grind them with a coffee mill at home and percolate it through a flannel bag. This is now a rare scene, thanks to the spread of electric coffee makers. But today people are too lazy even to make coffee with these. There's been a gradual increase of disposable single-use "cassette-type" coffee products. Basically, consumers unknowingly move in whatever direction allows them to lead an idle life. We can't always claim that everything that's convenient is no good, but certainly the human tendency to slack off has intensified remarkably, due to marketing backed by technology.

Some are of the opinion that we gain far more than we lose. It's true that newly invented technology and media have immense possibilities for cultivating wisdom and our senses. Anything that matures changes shape. Human beings and cultures both mature and metamorphose. But our desire is to metamorphose into better beings.

"Sense-driven" describes a situation in which progress pivots on our sensory perceptions. Let's pit this concept against that of the technologydriven world. Reviewing the world based on our senses would be sound and meaningful for technology as well.

In the past, graphic designers had to acquire the skill of drawing ten lines in between two lines one millimeter apart, as training for writing the kanji characters. Not long ago, we practiced this skill using a bow pen or drafting pen. I belong to the generation a little younger than the one that carried out this training to its fullest extent, but I remember that our generation's graphic design programs included similar training. Today, thanks to the computer, in theory we can draw a hundred lines, or a thousand in between those two lines. And so we can laugh off as nonsense the training of the old designers. But this isn't a matter of skill. It's a matter of sensory sophistication, or enhancement. The ability to draw ten lines in the width of one millimeter proves that the individual's eye is so enhanced that he or she can perceive the width of one millimeter divided into ten parts. There are senses that are absolutely unattainable without undergoing the necessary training. The union of such a highly sophisticated sensory perception with the computer would bring into existence a stupendous power. As it is, it's meaningless if a person who has completely inept eyes also has a tool for drawing a hundred lines within the width of one millimeter.

Of course, the computer can bring us sensations that were beyond the reach of designers of the past. It inspires in us such a dynamic and uplifting motivation that we're persuaded to abandon our antiquated sensory approach. The computer develops our sensory dexterity on a different stage than the one cultivated with our conventional method. On the other hand, however, unless we intentionally keep our senses active as well as receptive, software will only blunt them. Fettering the senses to an overweight body that has neglected physical activity not only means giving up the extremely delicate sensitivity accumulated over a long period of time, it also threatens to destroy the possibility of a new field of design in which delicate senses and technology are encouraged to interrelate. A situation in which we awake one day to find that what is active is not our senses but only software, is not some kind of science fiction tale; it's already here, and it is prevalent.

Before high technology began to drive society, there had been a balanced tug-of-war between technique and sensory perceptions. We used to judge issues and phenomena based on our sensory perceptions. If the motivation for driving the world turns into something aiming at an even higher technology, human senses may degenerate just as inactive muscles lose strength. If we don't quit this ongoing generation, we should at least work harder to awaken our sensory perceptions. It would be better if high technology were to evolve in a more delicate manner, subtly correlated with the periphery of sensory perception. I use the word "haptic" to describe this way of thinking.

## Spreading the World Atlas of the Senses

In the world of design, color and shape are at the apex. Every time some product with a striking shape or made of extraordinary materials comes along, we're dazzled. We're amazed by the metamorphosis that a different shape can give an ordinary utensil. This power to bewitch the consumer at first glance with our solutions is one of the allures of design. But it's not just that. The field of design is human perception. As I stated at the beginning of this chapter, there is another possibility for design besides focusing on color, shape and texture. This is in the quiet observation of how to sense and how to make the viewer sense.

And so I wonder, in this context, if there may be something yet dormant in our sensory perceptions, an undiscovered American Continent of the senses. Has no one begun the work of exploring this undiscovered continent on the world atlas of the senses? I am eager to set sail for this hidden realm of sensation. Each of the design solutions in the HAPTIC Exhibition suggests the possibility of its discovery. The gel remote control presents the degrees of softness. The juice skin manipulates textures in our memory. The design of the paperback cover was accomplished with protruding dots alone. All of these designs are like the islands that portend the existence of an undiscovered continent. Let us imagine through these prophetic islands a more stupendous continent of design. I believe that technology will bloom when planted on this sort of sensory perception. The HAPTIC Exhibition delivers this message.

Today information spreads all around the world before we even notice. If I only whisper "haptic" from the tip of East Asia might the people in London understand its meaning or those in Barcelona catch my murmur, and "haptic" be communicated. I think I'll whisper it, though my intent is hardly ambiguous. As long as the message is pure, the more gently it is spoken, the more forcefully it will reverberate into the world's receptive places, as a penetrating whisper.

3 SeNSEWARE

Medium That Intrigues Man

# SENSEWARE Medium That Intrigues Man

What Evokes the Senses

I have coined a term: "senseware." To me, this indicates any familiar thing that inspires our sensory perceptions. I use "ware" as it's used in "hardware" and "software."

Take the stone implements of the Stone Age. These are examples of senseware. If I grip in my hand a stone axe from a period removed from us by 400,o00 years, it becomes obvious to me why human beings used stone objects. The weight, the hardness and the texture of stone all appeal to us humans. The Stone Age spanned a surprisingly long period of time; for no less than one million years, humans transmitted from generation to generation the form of a single stone axe. It is difficult for us today to wrap our minds around the time and the endeavor of this continuous inheritance, spanning tens of thousands of generations, of the form of a single tool.

But when we actually touch the tool itself, we intuitively know with our very beings why the weight, hardness and palpable texture of stoneware inspired the human senses that drove the culture of the Stone Age. Even today I myself feel thrilled to handle these tools. This sensational feeling is like an impulse inciting us to create. Paper today belongs to "senseware" in the same way as stone belonged in the Stone Age.

## A White, Tense Material

Paper is white. This fact seems to be quite natural, yet paper that is white is anything but ordinary. As paper is too common in our daily lives, we have entirely familiarized ourselves with its extraordinariness. At its inception, paper was something extraordinary and special. White manifests itself in but a few natural objects: bones; a very small number of minerals; and the snow and ice of colder climates. But for the most part, the world is earth colored. The same goes for paper's raw material, tree bark, which is beige. Crush and unravel this earth-colored bark. Wet it, scoop it from the water, and dry it in the sun. The dried bark turns into a white sheet. And then, what's more, paper has a particular tautness. It's unique in that when we hold it in our fingers, it is tense. Paper's texture is pleasant to the tips of the fingers. If paper were the green of new leaves or the color of ripe persimmons, or if it felt loose like vinyl, human culture might not have made such rapid progress as we have experienced by taking advantage of the beginning of paper. However, paper accidentally came into existence in human history with no attribute of color, but instead with a glittering whiteness as well as a perky tensile state. I believe that humans, having reaped this harvest of paper, would itch to represent something on it, inspired by its embryo-like possibilities.

![](images/4baf5d18b00360bd169a17b825f6562851a791d63e835bee27b27737f65c1f4f.jpg)

![](images/b51a0f822ee2f19fe89f421eddf8b2558cc68a45e59ff88b0bae856aeb270370.jpg)  
Left: Stoneware from the Middle Acheulean period, from 700-800,o00 years ago Right: Stoneware from the Late Acheulean period, about 400,000 years ago  
Woln unooe

White brims with immaculate, serene silence as well as an embryonic enchantment that can bring about an immensity of achievements. On the other hand, this thin, homogeneous material is fragile and appears ephemeral. Once soiled, it cannot be returned to its pure state. On this white paper, our ancestors drew symbols in black Chinese ink. Right here I believe was evoked the most important awakening of the senses in human history. Here lies a peculiarity of this image, glittering strikingly in the history of culture.

![](images/8fe78bef7426417b1bd330e27d86e60662739019176a47291e70cd2f391d0489.jpg)  
A completed sheet of Japanese traditional washi paper just removed from the mold, Modern Age.

Today paper's role has been metamorphosed by the progress of electronic media. These days we even hear people say things like, "The Gutenberg Galaxy has come to an end." The world of communication, a synergistic result of paper and printing technology, is related to the eruptive birth of a universe, but does this Galaxy really have a severely limited time to live? The metaphor sounds interesting, but I believe that the thinking that ponders the end of the Galaxy in paper as a medium grips the material in a smaller sense. Paper is senseware; it serves, more than a material for writing and printing, as a perpetual medium of intelligence inspiring the human senses. Even if the invention of paper were to occur after that of electronic technology, still our imagination most likely would have been greatly inspired by an encounter with white sheets of paper that tickle the senses and foster our creativity.

## Communicating with Materiality

It's true that the medium is the message. In our history and culture, media have shouldered the reality of communication realized by media themselves. However, on top of this, media are simultaneously senseware, an everlasting facilitator of our creative activities which acts directly on the senses. Electrons are also expected to build such similar relationship with the human senses. Electrons are among the examples of senseware that carry no tangible medium, in which we envision a plethora of possibilities.

Unlike electrons, paper has an essence that lurks in the medium itself. By incessantly communicating with its whiteness as well as materiality, we have been able to nurture and secure a constant domain of representation. The book is an example of a tool that has come into being in our culture as a communicator with paper. Today, even as we explore the meaning of diversified media, it seems necessary to reevaluate via the senses the meaning of media that have been as close as air, incessantly giving us strength. My work as a designer is done at the far reaches of this idea.

![](images/7f4d07da409466715e3bad61bc1f3532de12da93b691aeed63ddbee82ae5b291.jpg)  
Materials for architecture in the brain converge from various sensory organs. The memories accumulated in the brain also serve as very important building materials.

## Architecture of Information

## The Field of Sensory Perceptions

A human being is a bundle of very delicate receptor organs and at the same time an image-generating organ equipped with a vigorous memory-playback system. An image generated in the human brain is a spectacle orchestrated through multiple sensory stimuli and revived memories. This is precisely where the designer works. As I've broadened my experience as a designer, I've grown more and more aware that I work in this field of sensory perceptions. In this chapter I'll describe some of the projects I've been involved in, each from the viewpoint of forming images through the accretion of sensory perceptions.

## The Architecture within the Brain

A designer creates an architecture of information within the mind of the recipient of his work. Its structure is comprised of the stimuli that enter through the mind exists everywhere in the body

Let's consider the brain as existing everywhere in the body. This is a vision, not a theory.

![](images/b1a3d7caabd095b9f3d96c121be21d3b7f01d794c77c155fc8b890f8c8fe0cd8.jpg)

assorted sensory perception channels. The stimuli, which are brought forth by the senses of sight, touch, hearing, smell and taste and various aggregates of these senses, are set up in the brain of the recipient and there emerges what we call "an image."

What is more important is that this structure being created in the brain uses as its building materials not only the external input supplied by the sensory organs, but also memories that are invoked by this input. In fact, the latter may be the primary material of the image. Memories not only lead the recipient to voluntarily ruminate on the past, but, called up in succession as the brain receives outside stimuli, also act to flesh out an image for understanding new information. That is, an image is something that is combined with and connected to not only external stimuli transmitted through the sensory organs but also the memories invoked by these stimuli. Postulating generation as a sort of aggregated image, the act of designing means to actively take part in this process. I call it the architecture of information because generating this aggregate image is intentional and calculated.

Another illustration demonstrates the same thing with a completely different conceptual expression (see page 157). It looks almost like the meridian diagrams used in acupuncture and Eastern medicine. The mind is not singular and seated within the head; it exists throughout the body, like a system of tsubo, or acupoints. We approach our work by targeting this multitude of brains. If information architecture is a Western, analytic concept, this may be an Eastern interpretation. I don't know which one is real, but either way, this illustration is a personal conceptual diagram of the field in which we work: the human being as information recipient.

## The Programs for the Opening and Closing Ceremonies of the Nagano Winter Olympic Games

Designing Paper

I'd like to continue my story of information architecture by quoting some practical examples, beginning with my design solution for the opening and closing ceremony programs for the Nagano Winter Olympic Games, held in 1998. My task was to represent hospitality to visitors around the world with modern graphism that still rests on Japanese tradition. The basic role of the program was to interpret the contents of the ceremonies along with their procedures. I graphically represented the steps of the opening ceremony, beginning with the sound of the gong of Nagano Prefecture's symbol, Zenko-ji Temple. This was followed by the ritual of tate-on-bashira, in which four pillars are raised to create as well as celebrate a holy space; the entering of the champion sumo

![](images/38e582f692e06bd6cbec07a206dfda5604c360ec583ac92548f1104d2bcf260c.jpg)

The Programs for the Opening and Closing Ceremonies of the Nagano Winter Olympic Games, 1998. In the program layout, the French and English texts are horizontal and the Japanese is vertical. Their contrasting directions create a unique spatial dynamic.

![](images/0a8c76c7c309c502d9fde7ad78348d490165fd4930f840db2fc408d163edc1e5.jpg)

wrestlers; the parade of athletes and the Olympic flag; the opening announcement and finally the lighting of the Olympic torch. I'd like to say more about the design, because the layout of this program, in which the Japanese is vertical and the French and English are horizontal, was the first attempt of its kind made in the international arena, but here we are concerned with not the visual contents but the material.

What I envisioned for this project was a program endowed with unforgettable elements: a medium of memory for the Nagano Olympic Games. The participation in the opening ceremony should be a compelling experience for everyone: athletes, audience and officials and attendants alike. So I wanted to design a program that would function as a package in which they could

![](images/3258f5228d3a88745080f8ed99e0f77c15f70add410ad2427a25d6d72f4b372f.jpg)

Top and Left: concave, half-transparent letters/characters and illustration   
debossed on the puffy paper   
Right: footprints dot a snowscape

store their exciting experiences. This idea is consolidated in the cover material. That is to say, I designed the very paper—one befitting the winter festivities, one that would evoke the images of snow and ice. I did this by asking a paper manufacturer to develop a white, puffy paper that allows the characters and letters on the cover to be debossed, a process by which a heated mold is applied and the fiber of the concave, debossed area is partially melted, becoming translucent like ice. Thanks to the paper manufacturer, a company highly motivated to take on this design project, I was able to realize my initial goal of "a paper of snow and ice." Here I am only able to show you a photograph, but please view it with your tactile perception switched on! On puffy, white paper are imprinted concave letters of French and English, and Japanese characters, all limpid, like ice.

## Invoking the Memory of Treading on Snow

I'm sure we all hold in some corner of our memory a scene like this, one that may have taken place on the grade school playground, or on our town's main street the morning after an overnight snowfall. Before you lay a white, puffy plane, covered with untouched, new-fallen snow. You worked your way forward, step-by-step, on the new-fallen, cotton-ball snow. Your footprints showed through to the dark soil beneath like semi-translucent ice. Behind, you left dotted prints. For anyone touching this paper, this is the memory that is invoked and enmeshed within the images in his mind. The "paper of snow and ice" is a trigger. This contrivance is design.

Seated roundly in the center of the puffy snow is a brilliant red flame, created with a foil-stamp imprint. The cover is made complete by with this tactile contrast, an aggregate image that opens the program, and continues into the graphics introduced in the previous section. Of course, the program itself is not the architecture of information. That will come into existence only in the minds of those who handle the information series we call the program.

![](images/f502c0bce9c0f0bd4cdd9a107eac247af2ef08bcf65f7782cf50f2e1300977e2.jpg)

The Cover of the Programs for the Opening Ceremonies of the Nagano Winter Olympic Games, 1998. On paper that gives rise to images of snow and ice, the red flame is created with a lustrous foil-stamp imprint.

# Signage System for a Clinic

Signage System for the Umeda Hospital

The Umeda Hospital in Yamaguchi Prefecture in western Japan is a specialty hospital for obstetrics and pediatrics. The architect Kengo Kuma, who designed the building, introduced me to its director, who then asked me to design its signage system. From this project as well I gained some insight into the architecture of information.

The most distinctive feature of the clinic's signage is that all of the signs are made of cloth. The first reason for this choice was my desire to create an affable space. Most of the people who spend time in this clinic are not "patients" per se; this is a place where pregnant women and nursing mothers spend time before and after childbirth. Places meant for the sick or injured necessitate an atmosphere so tense you could prick it with a pin, one reflecting a high level of reliable medical skills, supported by doctors and nurses and inspiring a sense of ease for the patients, who have entrusted their injured or sick bodies to a hospital. Some people like the idea of medical care in which the patients are treated in a friendly manner, but I wouldn't be about to submit to an operation in a hospital that felt like a country inn. The excessive intensity with which a fastidious, responsible head nurse executes her duties is essential to hospitals. But for those relaxing before and after childbirth, I figured I might be able to look at the environment from a slightly different angle.

On the one hand, today's low-fertility society has instigated changes affecting even hospital management. The dwindling number of pregnant women has become a challenging market. Clinics trading on hotel-like service and furnishing luxury-brand everything, from bed sheets to vanities, have stimulated customer discrimination. I've even heard of pregnant women flying all the way to some beautiful southern island to give birth to their babies underwater. Delivery seems to have become some great, extraordinary event.

![](images/ff47d7ee3d662fef951b27844657232e2c2db0ff519344bcc255708f36118859.jpg)

But I wasn't asked by the Umeda Hospital to do anything unusual. Both UNICEF and WHO accredited the clinic as a "Baby-Friendly Hospital," due to its efforts to encourage breastfeeding and, should the natural method not work out, bottle-feeding emphasizing frequent physical contact between mother and child. This clinic uses advanced techniques and provides progressive thinking on maternity healthcare. The management's request to me was to create signage that conveys the clinic's concepts to both adults and children in a natural manner.

## The Communication in Keeping White Cloth Clean

So I used white cotton for the signage of the Umeda Hospital. The supports are affixed to the wall or ceiling, while the signs themselves, carrying the necessary information on rooms and the facility, are made of white cloth. Some of the signs are easy to attach and remove, like socks, and others are like bed sheets.

Cloth is basically soft. So a space with cloth signage has a soft countenance. But there's a more important point to this design solution; signage made of white cotton is easily soiled. If there are cloth signs hanging down, curious kids are going to try to touch them. And naturally, they've got to touch them with fingers from which they've just eaten chocolate. In this case, I've used white cotton with full knowledge of its tendency to soil easily. All of the signs are designed for easy attachment and removal, like shower caps. The elastic band makes it simple. So as soon as it's dirtied, it can be replaced with a clean one and laundered.

But why did I resort to such a troublesome solution? If soiling is the problem, isn't it common sense to begin with a material like vinyl that's hard to dirty, or a color that doesn't show stains? That might be the normal way to approach the problem. But I threw my mind into reverse. I thought, a friendly atmosphere is nice to have in an obstetric clinic, but what's more important is supreme cleanliness. I dared to choose white, a color easily soiled, because I wanted to demonstrate the actual practice of keeping clean something that

![](images/ed54578203c43ae7a00a363f7a289be891efb0995798cb7ced0e123f68d65c47.jpg)  
Signage System for the Umeda Hospital, 1998. Signage made of easily soiled cotton facilitates communication.

![](images/4766a4b2041fe25c8f91f8d55ef2196be5dcdf839074cdcc1da5b33566596987.jpg)  
SENSEWARE

![](images/4d883f67cac6550fc88b2e43722fdbf3ddd05081835e08603cf8750a2e18c559.jpg)

Keeping such an easily soiled material as white cotton constantly clean demonstrates the utmost hygiene to both visitors and patients.

![](images/a7b4e1881df259da2dfe4f6ba70585c8c5f324675a6922fbbb6c1a677c5f43f9.jpg)

![](images/fe7e69e9a6d2d187e25a2f7de27d2b0250b785cbe60b246f9405f9d05cf7cf64.jpg)

is easily soiled. The fact that the clinic constantly maintains the cleanliness of this signage illustrates its dedication to utmost hygiene for the sake of both patients and visitors. This provides increased peace of mind to those spending time here before and after childbirth.

This design solution is based on the same principle as the use of white tablecloths by first-class restaurants. If these establishments wanted to conceal the unavoidable soiling of a restaurant tabletop, they would use darkcolored or vinyl tablecloths. But high-caliber restaurants dare to use pure white cloth in part to make obvious to the customer that their service includes first-rate cleanliness.

Signage is intrinsically a mere "indication," functioning as a guidance tool. And as long as it exists in space, signage cannot escape from being some kind of physical object. Letters, characters and symbols cannot stay afloat in mere air, so signage is generally displayed on a substance like acrylic, metal, wood or glass. This is one aspect of the destiny of signage design.

In this project, by using the physicality that is the destiny of signage towards another purpose, I tried to produce communication different from that achieved by mere guidance signage; through the use of white cotton signs, I meant to imprint the message "cleanliness" upon the minds of those experiencing the Umeda Hospital.

## Katta Civic Polyclinic Signage System

I would like to introduce one more clinic signage system. The Katta Civic Polyclinic is a horizontally expansive three-storied complex with a very spacious footprint of 100 square meters. Unlike hospitals in crowded cities like Tokyo, this compound is situated in a luxuriantly verdant northeastern Japanese town on a plateau with an extensive view and makes sumptuous use of this space. On the first floor is the outpatient department, including the reception desk, consultation rooms, laboratories and a dispensary. The second floor is used for operating rooms, doctors' offices, etc. The third floor, benefiting from brilliant sun, is the medical ward for inpatients. The complex was very rationally designed, thanks to the collaboration of three architects. The flow line for the most crowded area, the consultation space, is as economically planned as if it were for an airport. And so the design of the signage system went relatively smoothly.

The distinguishing feature of the signage system hinges on the use of larger type so that even the elderly can clearly recognize the signs. As far as was possible, I avoided using complicated support structures jutting out of the walls and ceilings, instead setting it up so that the type was laid directly on the floors and walls. Floor signage had to be very large to maintain an unobstructed view, even as people walk over it. To eliminate the problems of fading and erosion from foot traffic, which would surely arise if we had printed directly on the floor or lining the floor with some kind of adhesive sheet, I came up with the solution of inlaying red linoleum type and symbols into the white linoleum of the floor. We were able to do this thanks to a laser-cutting technique. So in this hospital, people walk right on the signs.

![](images/fa7392cc6ee1624a52a196d8d18b2163468f5594a66f471e58a6a952fca2f251.jpg)

![](images/429a7787cf2850c9c55db86e230206b5e37ba887b373d3de393c7361a5bd6d66.jpg)  
Signage System for the Katta Civic Polyclinic, 2002. Large-type signage inlaid on the floor helps repeat visitors memorize the hospital layout.

On the one hand, the flow line from reception counter to waiting room, then to consultation room for a check-up, and then perhaps to the dispensary and finally to the payment counter, is quite clear cut. The role of signage is to indicate the flow line in such a way that it can be clearly figured out in the minds of visitors and patients. The red crosses laid out on the main points of intersection act as indicators as well as traffic control tools.

The length of the trunk of a red arrow sign relates directly to the distance to the destination. After traversing the entire trunk, the patient stands before the door at the end of his journey, poised upon the arrow's head. This signage system serves as a sort of guideline designed to help visitors and patients (most of whom will probably be repeat travelers within the facility) remember the hospital space, by helping create the flow line within their minds. With just a few experiences, they should be able to figure out how to use the facility and no longer need to read the signs on the floor. This is our goal.

![](images/9dd769f9a52e3308fd617e80d86ce6f5ea1f67e28b5b7c37774c1acc5b8aa36a.jpg)

Unlike the Umeda Hospital, here there is tension, more than friendliness, a perfect tension, in fact, befitting the facility's high-quality, specialized medical treatment. The white cotton signage used in this space also plays the role of depicting cleanliness.

# Matsuya Ginza Renewal Project

Palpable Media

Between 2001 and 2006, the Matsuya Department Store, occupying an entire block of the central street of Tokyo's Ginza district, carried out a multi-phased renewal project. Ginza Street is one of central Tokyo's main thoroughfares, with a long history, but the successive establishment of storefronts here by international brands like Chanel, Cartier, Prada and Apple Japan has ushered in a new stage.

Over these five years, through the gradual renewal of its interior and exterior, during which it continued to conduct business, the venerable Matsuya Department Store metamorphosed into a new, white Matsuya. This was both an integrated design project encompassing a variety of fields (commercial space, packaging, and advertising, among others) and an effort to imbue each of these aspects with texture, which could also be termed "character."

For publicizing as a fresh message such a well-known commercial environment as a department store, conventional methods wouldn't have had much impact. I thought that rather than resorting to cheap operations like conventional media campaigns or video displays in the windows, it would be really efficient to restructure the department store, which exists as a physical object, as "palpable media." Department stores are not virtual shops, but places people go to the trouble of visiting in person, to physically experience shopping. This is why I thought that I could, by designing a tangible texture to the space, create the presence or impression of an entirely new shop, one that had never existed before.

The focus of the renewal project was shifting the axis of the department store's image. Matsuya's existing image was centered on a true picture of a high-quality life, or design for daily life, rather than fashion. The common impression was that people visited Matsuya to look for a high-quality lifestyle, not to experience the forefront of fashion. I thought this was a fine precedent, but the management team had determined to introduce droves of fashion brands. What's more, before the renewal got underway, Louis Vuitton opened a shop on the corner of the Matsuya block. Basically, Matsuya was bound to be involved in fashion both inside and out.

At the beginning of the project, I was asked to plan advertising to announce the renewal. However, as I mentioned before, it's doubtful that advertising alone could replace the conventional image that is a department store with fresh information. I felt that it was essential to consolidate the design of everything, including space and supplies, within the renewal project.

The concept model appears in the photograph (see page 176). If we hew to the theme of this chapter, we would call it a model of the architecture of information. The structure is simple. I piled up fine white paper embossed with the Matsuya logo as deep as the thickness of a sofa cushion and associated this white mass with the building. I made nine square holes in it and into each inserted a translucent acrylic cube, each representing one of the nine intended fashion brands. Each of these carried on its back a photo of a brand image. Thanks to the refraction of the acrylic, the photos seemed mysteriously solid.

Making the base out of white paper has a symbolic meaning. It suggests the change of Matsuya's corporate color from bright blue to white. It would have been impossible to bring fashion into a place so deeply dyed with the department store's previous image of lifestyle design. The worst scenario that could arise from the combination of fashion and that image would have been the birth of an image like that of a duty-free shop. Had the base had to play second fiddle to the brands, it would have obscured any reason to call this commercial zone "Matsuya." The challenge was finding a way to create an identity that was one size larger, one big enough to include all of the brands. As for "white,"as I will explain in the next chapter, it is bestowed with a special quality. In this project, I designated white because of its function as a backdrop, its power of inclusion, its modernity and its dignity, as well as the balance of all of these.

![](images/e4eb6a64cb2a628d8168e46255df30e6e874966809943b1af9690cfb9420380e.jpg)  
Matsuya Ginza Renewal Project, 2001-2007. Information architecture model for renewal project presentation, 2001.

The embossed texture of the paper suggests the use of a white that has both physical texture and tangible depth. In other words, it's based on the idea that the roots and trunk of the new Matsuya image are sustained by a tangible white.

The proposal I presented using this model was accepted, and the plan was moved into the implementation stage. From here evolved a multitude of practical matters, such as the change in the store's visual identity, including the corporate color, the innovation of signage, shopping bags and gift wrap, the arrangement of color tones in the interior, ad planning, the design of a temporary enclosure for the store, and in addition, the design of patterns for the exterior walls.

## The Collaboration of Tactile Designs

The first part of the project to be translated into reality was a temporary enclosure for the construction phase(see pages 178/179). In urban Japan, scaffolding is erected around buildings that are under construction and then draped with material, both to conceal the disarray and protect passersby from debris and danger. This temporary enclosure covered the colossal area of 100 meters in width and five meters in height, facing Ginza Street. To illustrate this surface, I designed an image of a huge zipper, horizontally running the length of the building. As the construction progressed, the temporary enclosure panels were rotated to the right, step by step. The leading, closed-zipper image was replaced with a panel showing the zipper at the beginning of unzipping, a Vshaped opening revealing type stating the opening date. As this image gradually proceeded to the right, more opening-date graphics were revealed in its path, until one day all imagery and graphics were removed, exposing the new

![](images/007361b2196e9f761373a5b9b69d697112b37c14546701efa38e40ac6cfba6fe.jpg)

![](images/fa4ede9a5766d4c9d788355d5eea1bc8171f6b4bc00edee010b4c76461c0f395.jpg)  
September 30, 2000

![](images/5a51f236096cd35cd59d565e05222cc5fce563b9a8548a3ec904e21a37359d9c.jpg)  
October 16, 2000

![](images/b96766fa1036c2370990e070b47dd87f1f735f38f32b5e1adc509f1d145f8d9c.jpg)  
January 10, 2001

![](images/97256769da1041f91b5f799478375d17b955c8025a5caa808c71b686e1f31df3.jpg)  
March 8, 2001  
Matsuya Ginza, after renewal, 2006.

![](images/8903c509704dbf2e605b19fd6be28d2ef1d11538c579b3bfab4afbebacce20e1.jpg)

The temporary enclosure for the renewal construction site, 2001.

A gigantic canvas more than 100 meters wide appeared on the construction scene facing Ginza Street. Due to strict regulations on temporary enclosures erected in public spaces facing national roads, we were not allowed to express any intention of communication (advertising) on this fa§ade. Therefore, we designed the image of a huge zipper, whose panels were shifted to the right, together with the enclosure, as each stage was completed, making it appear as if the zipper were being unzipped little by little. The zipper is a simile for fashion.

![](images/0f1d68eab230e8b1ab341d1b714416034925fe92586ef6a4c1780438a632ea9c.jpg)

design to the street and its travelers. This unzipping performance was meant to raise anticipation for the completion among passersby and active customers.

I was not involved in the building itself, but the front of the finished building is plated with glass. Applied to the back of the glass are aluminum panels, painted white, whose surface is covered in a regular and tight array of convex dots. The only thing I proposed was this design tool of protruding dots, to help stir up tactile perception. The glass exterior walls are set at top and bottom with lights, which are switched on at night. To effectively reflect the light, the white exterior walls required the installment of some sort of concavo-convex reflector, but these hemispherical dots play the same role. Comprised in these dots, then, is an interrelation between the architecture and the overall design. Reflecting on the aggregate of the hemispherical dots, the light is transformed into elegant particles emanating onto Ginza Street and producing graceful textures on the exterior walls.

![](images/f103b8ffa1e08ba4e0e1097f48d8fb3f1a232f7056aaea6e4e48cad870776d8d.jpg)

![](images/6101bffab6e45bef9054bc5789bc5f22ef6d670526be0656c724206a16de13a5.jpg)  
Top: Matsuya point card, using the same dot pattern that was applied to its exterior walls, 2003 Right: The texture of the paper shopping bags conveys the store's concept, 2001

In the photo is the Matsuya point card. This is the only project that I know of in which the same design is applied to a shop's card and its exterior walls. With the same dot pattern, the card is like a part of the architecture. And the moment the customer touches it, the image of the exterior walls is called to the tips of his fingers.

Shopping bags and giftwrap are two other important media by which to communicate the store's concept to its customers. As anyone who has shopped at a prudently run brand retailer must have learned from the experience, the key to a brand image is careful selection of the quality of the details that touch the body. So we developed a paper that conveys a rich sense of touch to the fingertips (see page 183). The perfected "M wrap" feels very pleasant to the touch. Do you recognize in the paper the pattern of the conceptual model?
