I also designed the line of shopping bags. The photo shows the Matsuya logo in white emerging from a pattern of small pale grey dots on white paper. The same pattern is used for the giftwrap. The material is inexpensive craft paper, but thanks to the dots, it has the perfect texture. Careful readers might have already noticed that this pattern is linked to the dot pattern of the walls.

Following pages: Posters with embroidered illustrations on felt-like paper, 2001

White unifies the renewed interior, and the walls and flooring are close to white. All of the signs are new. The signage system emphasizes pictograms, and its white, plain material subdues the physicality of the signs.

## Information as an Event

The photo shows two posters (see pages 186/187). Except for the letters, all of the patterns here were created with embroidery on a felt-like paper. With the advances made in computerization, industrial sewing machines and a largescale embroidery machine for sewing sheets can be used like a printer. Depending on the quantity, embroidered posters can be produced at a lower price than printed ones. And so as with our textural message, we used embroidered posters for the advertising.

Since department stores are situated in specific locations, in many cases it is more effective to make deliberate use of local media rather than mass media covering a wide area. It's much better for a department store in Ginza to aim its striking message at those who are actually walking in Ginza. This message is delivered as "a Ginza happening," an event that can't be experienced anywhere else but on this very spot. These posters have a zipper sewn onto either end, and the pieces of the message announcing the store's opening were fastened together in a series, spreading over various areas, lengthwise on walls and columns in the underground markets. Zippers used for the temporary enclosure again took an active part, this time in the posters. The series must have been entertaining for the Ginza-going crowd, because they transcended simple visual information to become "events."

And that's how the new Matsuya Ginza performed its renewal in the minds of the customers who visited this particular place.

![](images/2eb442ca0d97f62d0299304b0e7b94304f707ec44905010e5699bb7c402d39ba.jpg)

## Nagasaki Prefectural Art Museum: Visual Identification

## Ripple-like Information

The Nagasaki Prefectural Art Museum, completed in 2005, centers on vertical stone louvers. Long, narrow stone panels hung consecutively cover all external walls. The architect is Kengo Kuma, who in recent years has shown a tendency towards design that uses not closed, but flexible walls, and architecture exhibiting some translucence, with multiple layers creating profound depth. Depending on the project, he may use anything from bamboo to wood, glass or stone, but all of his work shares a mysterious and breezy semi-transparency of space.

![](images/374c3f90d0632340690c7bad2e4d5ec499ab2e3738197e08fba9650e60e90ed7.jpg)

A mysterious visual effect occurs as passersby view the logo and symbol marks, expressed through parallel placement of two comb-like layers, 2005.

In making choices for the museum's identification, I gave careful consideration to unifying the symbol, logo and signage into a single, memorable image, with the architecture's louvers as the foundation.

The symbol for the museum (see page 190) is a figurative arrangement drawn from the louvers as they appeared on the architectural blueprint. The mark is an accumulation of lengthwise lines. In a book I can only show you a static image, but the salient point of the work is that as a motion graphic, it is always moving, like ripples in water. On the website, the image is in constant motion, with occasional changes in the motion pattern. Because the museum site is like a seaside park, I focused on images that might relate to such an environment: rippling waves and leaves trembling in the breeze.

The entrance sign is distinctive. Arranged like two metal combs separated by open space, it exhibits a mysterious translucency because of the added translucency of the parallel placement of the two layers. If you look at it as you walk by, the logo and the symbol, each positioned on the surface of one

##

# Nagasaki Prefectural Art Museum

![](images/bafcff85a316c051a3fcc8fc321563eded9f089e0b48e77ce6b5568a3f2e760a.jpg)

![](images/3286527788760a9e7eaa4acaae85977a71a36871fef6a1ffc09f71b3e6892750.jpg)

![](images/efed9af13d8e88146bd8839b9c4cde6cbae48522b93f47c4f35e34b9f8087a6b.jpg)

![](images/cf5095de6f36b2d9d27eea07463a42020c2b71d53126c502c73a4a4c218b3f88.jpg)

![](images/04b84acdce0df03d4a14c0cd357e94a93991110110ea3d4e1c7bd5f99b6f42eb.jpg)  
Left: Signature for the Nagasaki Prefectural Art Museum, 2005 Created as a ripple-like motion graphic. Above: Signage, 2005 Three-dimensional arrows clearly indicate directions.

of the two combs, flicker together in mutual interference, creating an optical illusion. This is no virtual visual phenomenon but a ripple-like visual effect occurring in real, tangible space.

The guidance signage is set three dimensionally against the backdrop of the louvered walls. By virtue of floating in space, the 3-D arrows gain true indicative function, as they point us in 3-D directions. Please note the reality of the arrows that actually turn corners, or those that seem to be thrust directly into the wall above the restroom doors. The identity of this museum comes from a combination of the visual effect of ripple-like movement occurring in rhythm with the architecture, and the arrows drifting in layered space.

# Swatch Group: Signage System for Nicolas G. Hayek Center

The Watch Hovering in the Air

The Nicolas G. Hayek Center building, the Swatch Group's branch office in Japan, designed by Shigeru Ban, was completed on Tokyo's Ginza Street in 2007. Plans call for this building to be afforded quite some weight as a showroom for the Swatch Group's brand.

Like a continuation of the street, the ground floor, being without walls, is an open environment. The showrooms are each on separate floors, and visitors reach them directly via dedicated elevators. There is the same number of transparent cylindrical elevators as there are brands. This means the visitor doesn't step onto the elevator and then select the button corresponding to the desired floor, but chooses instead, the right elevator. Visitors are then transported nonstop to their preferred destination

The name "Swatch Group" brings to mind casual wristwatches, but in fact it is the world's largest wristwatch conglomerate and the umbrella company for such high-quality Swiss watchmakers as Breguet, Blancpain, Jaquet-Droz and Omega. It's as if the casual Swatch brand was created as a business structure designed to protect these mechanical watches. At one time Swiss watches played second fiddle to Japanese quartz watches as far as accuracy was concerned, but Swatch has garnered a new position by redefining for the world the appeal of mechanical watches as well as the meaning of wearing a wristwatch. Certainly, compared to wearing a watch that, while supremely accurate, is also dowdy, it may make life more fun to sport one that attracts affection, or does a fair job of reflecting our status, especially if it's one that gives us some enjoyment on the fashion front.

The signage system for this project needed to precisely explain both the mechanism of the elaborate showroom complex and the elegance of the brands. However, my solution goes further than addressing these basic issues. It absorbs them and creates an identity in which the image of the building is positively imprinted on the minds of visitors.

## A Watch in the Hand

It might make more sense to call these "floating watches" than signage. A projector embedded in the ceiling projects a simple image the size of a wristwatch. The reflected image is such that when it reaches something far enough from the ground that it will appear in focus, it becomes an analog watch that tells the time. On the floor, which is of a rough stone texture, it remains a dim red light, but when someone comes along and catches the reflection on his body, he sees something, briefly, that looks like a watch. The passerby puts out his hand and the bright, clear image of a watch appears in his palm. It is the experience of physically catching time. In this instant, communication is complete. A new identity as a famous spot on Ginza Street could easily be born from this plan, as it's very likely to be used as a meeting point.

Some may wonder about the use of digital technology in the signage, since Swatch makes mechanical watches, but taking strength from the poetics of expressing time, I'd like these doubters to consider the fact that mechanical watches have survived in the marketplace because they draw their strength from the lyricism of "articulating time." The nucleus of this communication is "how best to present time."

![](images/2cee9b7546d6eccffb6270b723e7b29df57231797a2420191b8c99b76069b656.jpg)

![](images/47797566b2591b19db1b964d2edc0014af705417fbdf082681a76019505d913f.jpg)

The watches hovering in the air designed for the Swatch Group, 2007. The watch images projected from the ceiling tell the time accurately and come into focus at heights corresponding to the bodies of passersby.

![](images/80123e0cff29271c4e1cc8b8669cd9eff7ece50a795add9a142aed4d5c65e1e0.jpg)

# Books as Information Sculpture

Rediscovering Books

I do a lot of book design. And I like to compile things in book format. But the accelerated speed of the evolution of information technology has led to the foundation of more and more information formats. Under these circumstances, it may be time we consider the possibility that books have already stepped down from their traditional role as information medium. As far as speed, density and volume go, we can't even compare books to electronic media. Still, it's hard to come to the conclusion that the role of books has collapsed. Perhaps it's time we reevaluate what a book is. If we keep designing books in the conventional manner without going through this reevaluation, it would seem we're a bit out of step with the times.

Let's take a long, cool look at paper as a material. As a medium, paper has assumed great responsibility. Especially with the recent speeding up of the circulation of information, we could view paper, prior to its consideration as a material, a kind of unconscious surface, always right there for us, as a neutral, white surface, whether we were writing a letter with a fountain pen, or printing out a photograph from a computer. A white screen that is efficiently sized (in Japan, at one to the square root of two), its materialistic aspect paid scant attention, paper was understood as an abstract medium that carries images and letters. The esteem we place in paper, one of the world's three greatest inventions, is also no doubt because of this very nature as a neutral medium and not for the materiality that so pleases the fingertips when they brush against something natural. So it was when monitors became the norm that people, completely disregarding the appeal and nature of paper, started using the term "paperless."

![](images/cd911ed6428c0a3183c54d4b04bb8ad08d32dedebe4da1ee3d766bdb63613542.jpg)

![](images/1b06629edc699bc44c8f3cd8e9de3a8ee0385f85c0102c00264b419c611dff54.jpg)

![](images/ef1cb2f36394d82e83c4959ca72a2712c82052fc8ce9afea2cd65b86ce356672.jpg)

If we look at it from this perspective, it may be that today, paper, stepping down from the principal role of medium and freed from practical duties, can once again be allowed the charming behavior of its intrinsic nature: as material.

## Information Is a Single Soft-Boiled Egg

When you think about it, books as a medium for stocking definite information may be inconvenient. They're heavy, they're bulky, they get dirty and they fade with time. Their size is specifically tailored, yet they house an amount of information that could be easily contained in a tiny digital memory. But information is not just something to store in bulk or transmit at high velocity. If we coolly observe the relationship between information and the individual, what becomes important is how deeply we can appreciate information. As far as books go, we may have a more pleasant user experience and be more satisfied with information presented on a material of moderate weight and texture than with information whose presence has become rarified through compression into a tiny space.

This may be like the relationship between food and human beings. Our ancestors put their abundant experiences and insights to work to figure out how to best enjoy the taste of an egg. Just think about the enormous number of instruments to prepare eggs, the diversity of recipes to cook them, and the myriad methods and tableware invented to serve them. A device that cooks a thousand eggs at a time or a warehouse that stores 500,000 may be useful, but our "personal appetite" for an egg is not at all concerned with these. When we want to eat a soft-boiled egg, we use a pan to cook it to the degree of firmness that we prefer. Then, we really should put it in an eggcup, peel it diligently with our fingers and, after shaking salt onto it with an elegant saltcellar, eat it with a silver spoon. Even when it seems too much of a bother, we will absolutely enjoy the egg most when served this particular way. The relationship between human beings and information is something like that between humans and food. To choose paper, with an understanding of the quality and nature of the material, is to enliven, relish, and appreciate that.

![](images/ab2711a848aa1a9b5b2a956bf64737fa67942aa846e0dbcc0fa862aa036d8057.jpg)

<table><tr><td># morphopoiesist   E</td></tr></table>

![](images/2fc92fc185fa54098dc16d1a4c8b76eaa7ab4d61f06af2895befac81f930603c.jpg)

## Information Sculpture

I'm still convinced that the book is an effective medium, and I don't think that its efficacy has declined as much as society thinks it has. That goes as well for the book you have in your hands now. If putting as many words as come into your head in some place that's convenient and easy to access is your goal, you can house them on the web or on something like a CD. But here I've chosen the medium called a book. That's because I want people to enjoy this information as letters printed on paper, and because I want to hand it to people as an object with a resistant weight. And it's because, after you've taken it from your bag on the train, I want you to be able to turn the pages at your own pace, and because I think it would be nice if it faded with the passage of time and became an antique. Of course, as a designer, I am exercising my ingenuity to the end that this book may create a pleasant atmosphere in the palms of my readers; I am conscious of the appeal of the book from the perspective not of transmitting information from left to right, but of cherishing information. It is not that I favor paper because I'm fettered to nostalgia. It is not that I dislike electronic media. I'm already so deeply dependent on information technology that if I lost my email service I'd get pretty upset. In fact, this is why when I do use paper media, I want to deal with this not unconsciously, but with clear intentions. Thanks to the rise of electronic media, paper can finally behave as it can and should—as an intrinsically charming material.

If electronic media is reckoned a practical tool for information conveyance, books are information sculpture; from now on, books will probably be judged according to how well they awaken this materiality, because the decision to create a book at all will be based on a definite choice of paper as the medium. How fortunate an issue this will be for paper.

## Not Future but Present

I don't think it's cool to spend the present anticipating the future. The entire 20th century was filled with these sorts of discussions and projections. Reading old magazines, we come across pages of bold predictions about the future, but they don't excite us today. At the time, it might have seemed fashionable to be predicting the future, but whether their predictions hit the mark or missed, and even if by some chance something they said was right on the money, the whole attempt strikes us as superficial and arouses neither wonder nor respect. What we're likely to recognize instead is the great value of the achievements or attitudes that felt what needed to be felt in each era, and put into the appropriate form that which could only be expressed in those circumstances.

The society of the future will probably be like something out of a story from science fiction. But I want to be proud of the fruits of my existence, as those from a life lived fully in my own era, in which I have mobilized all of my senses, regardless of which stage of the future they may be reviewed. Just as Leonardo da Vinci left to posterity paintings that reflected his era, I resolve to leave behind information sculpture indigenous to my times, fully conscious of its materiality (made rather conspicuous with the arrival of the computer). To this individual that I am, books are a highly esteemed medium.

The evolution of electronic media is still on the rise. For the time being, electronic media and books will continue to influence one another, in parallel paths that grow deeper together.

## FILING—Managing the Chaos, 2004.

For this book project, we researched and inquired about practical ways to file not data, but physical objects, and proposed various filing solutions. These proposed not hiding information away, where it would be as good as dead, but finding an innovative organization method inspired by tangible objects.

![](images/33664c9c53f7981907b98a6ead310004e799030e8fbd2aba0b076a3df590f15c.jpg)

![](images/9ec3fb3b8a1fc2263933f85d773689ff8dbe35b467d2852b30ce8821f58ba671.jpg)

Optimum, 2002.

In 2002, the Japan Design Committee held an exhibition entitled "Optimum (An Original Form of Design)." Because the original form is the optimum, or most favorable, committee members selected products that precisely monitored every aspect of the human environment, in the same way that a living creature evolves in adjusting to its environment. The white of paper allows for highly flexible usage, becoming a floor, a wall, a platform, or a space with depth.

![](images/6e9938dc88f608e3025a1d572dc38e5a02bbe523917359852dc77d218b385dc8.jpg)

Z0-#6670-3405\*8\* GASEOMSHLTRELTLS. MEOEnTLESB.THURRRSnABM.

Its simple but fine beauty is a fruit of elaborated skills. The four thin legs supporting the large thin top table create an excellent balance, but at the same time, you may doubt if they can keep the strength and the steadiness of the table board. This seemingly impossible design is made possible by changing

the thickness of the bottom surface of the table board.   
From the edge, the closer to the center, the thicker it smoothly becomes.   
This part is not visible from those who look at the table. This form appeals to everyone's heart. Its magical charm is an embodied OPTIMUM QUALITY   
impossible to replace.

## 4 WHITE

# WHITE

White as a Design Concept

Design is the controlling of difference. But while working through countless projects, I came to realize that I was weaving meaning not out of the big differences, but out of the first, smallest ones only. That results in a much more delicate tapestry. Particularly with the abundance of color in the streets, with the ability to freely manipulate hundreds and thousands of colors on paper or on the computer, I've grown less enchanted with the easygoing use of color. When I'm laying out the necessary materials, the colors have fallen into order before I even realize it.

Of course, colors are wonderful. Monochrome photos are beautiful, but how desolate would we feel if color disappeared from the earth? I also wouldn't say that artificial colors are ugly. In fact, I actually feel envious of some people's talent for liberally using a variety of primary or vivid colors. And I do see the possibilities in the world of color computing, where it is possible to purely manipulate colors within a virtual reality that is void of the feeling of real life. And, of course, in my usual design work, I don't shun color either. I am neither a designer who likes white nor one who doesn't use color. As a professional graphic designer, I naturally use color. However, when I do so, it's with a consciousness of its function, or because it represents an emotion I'm after: the red of emergency call buttons, brand colors that have to be remembered, color-coded indexes.... But if there's no particular reason for them, you'll find no extra colors on my design table. Whether it's a high-tech or a natural material, I always look at the color of the material itself. Doing this, I gradually became conscious of white. White is a color from which color has escaped, but its diversity is boundless. When I encounter a really good white, I get this feeling that the collection of senses stored in my brain has increased by one.

White is not just a color. White must be called a design concept.

## Discovering White

White is not white. The receptivity that senses white is what gives birth to whiteness. So we cannot look for white. We need to search instead for a way of feeling that will sense white. Depending on this search, for the receptivity that senses white, we will be able to aim our consciousness towards a white that is a little whiter than the average white. With that ability, we will become conscious of white. And then we will become aware of white enmeshed in an incredible diversity in the world's many cultures. We will become able to understand words like "tranquility," or "emptiness," and discern the meanings dormant within them. As we turn our attention toward white, the world gathers more light, and shadows deepen in degree.

The black of type is not born of the blackness of the letters, but the black coupled with the white of the paper. The red cross in the Red Cross logo is red not only because of the red of the cross. The red glimmers by dint of the quality of the white on which it is placed. A margin, be it blue or beige, inherently comprises white. A margin is neither a mere absence nor a remainder. It is white that has come into existence as the result of the filling in of a space. Sometimes, because of the margin's necessity for existence, its nonexistence imports a stronger presence than an actual margin. Furthermore, white is easily soiled and is hard to keep clean. White impresses us as more beautiful still thanks to our feelings of empathy towards anything transient.

When I act in concert with a consciousness that is subtly receptive to white, and in that state of mind look at architecture, spaces, books or gardens, I feel as if I am able to make sense of all kinds of things. I believe white is a special message meant just for the roots and trunk of human sensibility.

## The Implicit Color

Is white a color? It could be said to be like a color, but not a color. So what is color? As a result of modern physics, today we have clear systems by which the mechanism of color is organized: the Munsell and Ostwald color systems, established by Albert H. Munsell and Friedrich Wilhelm Ostwald. Their systems represented in three dimensions based on value, chroma, and hue (the degrees of lightness and hue, as well as a scale of hue in circular form) lead us to an easy understanding of color's structure as a physical phenomenon. But we don't sense color in terms of these systems. The glossy freshness of a deep yellow yolk spilling out of a broken eggshell, or the russet color of tea in a cup are not only colors, but also textures, with materiality, in which taste and smell are also deeply enmeshed. People sense color as an amalgamation. So color is not just visual, but something relating to all of our perceptions. The standard color systems are only orderly arrangements created by taking the physical and visual characteristics out of color, which is intricate and complex.

Today, color sample books are used to indicate and specify colors for printing, textiles and industrial products. Most of the books reflect the Munsell or Ostwald color systems, which are convenient for practical location and specification of colors. As the systems offer both order and objectivity, they help us communicate necessary colors with absolute certainty.

On the other hand, one of the color sample books I use a lot is called "Traditional Japanese Colors." This book is compiled from the names of the traditional colors of Japan. Because a consistent system was not the goal of this compilation, it isn't always suitable for strict specification of color, but is highly reputed for its power to lightly evoke images of individual colors. Leafing through the pages of this book, I easily and naturally recognize the characters of the colors, indicated by terms invented to describe them. At the same time, I feel enlightened, as if every infinitesimal bit of my sensory perceptions were awakened, and then I get a sense of security, like when I hear my native dialect spoken. On top of that, I feel a little twinge of melancholy. Why? What are the roots of these emotions?

The book does express the subtle emotional aspect of color, but it's not just that. Perhaps the nucleus of my deep emotions is partly my awareness that color is something that is detected by each person, and partly the impression of sharing this point of view with others. Color is not something that exists independently in nature, but is a property exquisitely outlined in words that portray the vicissitude of subtle changes of light. I greatly admire this apt diagnosis. Traditional colors are in fact a way of treating color, a way of savoring color that has been retained, as the words that name them, within a particular culture.

The traditional colors of Japan originated in the court culture of the Heian period (794-1185). It was during this period that there developed a culture in which people carefully observed the transitions of nature, made these a pretext in the colors of clothing, goods and furniture, as well as in common expressions and greetings. Today we refer to the four seasons, but in those days a person was considered cultured if he could appreciate with heightened perception the delicate transition of natural phenomena in a year divided into 24 solar terms or 72 pentads (five-day cycles). Shorthand for this transition was the term setsugetsuka (snow, moon, flowers). Words depicting nature's changing hues in grass and trees sound dainty and weak, but that very frailty is what allows the perfect infiltration into the innermost recesses of human sensibility. A thin needle threaded with the supremely delicate thread of a color's name flawlessly stitches one subtle portion of our perceptions to another. What overwhelms me, no doubt, is the pleasant sensation or empathy that is sensed only when the intended target within is shot straight through. It is a sensation that involves some melancholy, as I simultaneously recognize the reality that this sort of delicate sensitivity has deteriorated under the circumstances of modern life.

Just as an entire limestone cave is formed only upon an inconceivable repetition of dripping droplets, the names of colors have come into being through mental imagery, generated little by little through our accumulated interactions with nature's twinkle and the world's many transformations. While some of them have ceased to exist, and others have undergone a metamorphosis, these images have developed, unnoticed, into the great system of consciousness called color. I suppose that the number of color systems called "traditional colors" equals the number of the world's languages and cultures. Japan's is only one of them. The white I will write about here is one of these traditional Japanese colors, but a strikingly distinctive one.

In ancient Japan, the term kizen was used to describe a situation of implicit action, the moment before something happens, and thus becomes explicit in the real world, in response to a particular impulse. White, the very possibility of the introduction of color, is then the color of kizen.

## Escaping Color

If you mix together all the colors of light, they become white. If you discard all the colors in paint or ink, they also become white. White is a synthesis of all colors and, at the same time, the lack of color, achromatic. As a color that escapes color, it is a special one. Put another way, color is no more than a single aspect of white. Insofar as it avoids color, and thus more strongly awakens physicality, it is a materiality; like empty space or a margin, it is pregnant with time and space. It even entails abstract concepts like absence and absolute zero.

It goes without saying that the white to which we refer does not have the attributes of ordinary trendy colors, colors that are consumed, nor does it serve as an object of color theory. Neither can its features be elucidated according to the genealogy of traditional colors. White has always been a concept and a resource underpinning aesthetics, which is an invariable object of human consciousness.

## Original Forms of Information and Life

The world is a chaos of jumbled colors. The invigorating freshness of trees, the glitter of the surface of a lake, the color that conveys the epitomized presence of a piece of fruit, the color of a crackling fire: I love each and every one of these. However, just as we say that everything returns to the earth, an infinite number of activities and inspiring experiences mingle in the accumulation of time, eventually swirling into brown, seen from a macroscopic perspective of time. From this viewpoint, the earth appears in camouflage. Mix it even more and the colors connoting life change into a chaos of gray. But chaos is not death. The energy of the dazzling colors will quicken, and from within the chaos, a completely new color will be born again.

Let's superimpose white onto this image of regeneration and transmigration. In an extremely striking image white occupies a special position, standing out as it does, from the chaos. Running counter to the negative principle of mixture, white becomes explicit by breaking through the gravity of degeneration that tends towards a return to gray. White comes into existence as the ultimate of idiosyncrasy. It is neither a mixture, nor even a color.

There is a concept called "entropy." Referring to the degrees of chaos, it is described in the second law of thermodynamics. This law states that all energy is eventually equalized. The coffee in the cup in my hand is now steaming hot, but over time, it will cool, eventually reaching the temperature of its surroundings. Cold coffee in a similar situation will neither boil nor freeze. Thanks to the activity of the earth, like that of a living organism the temperatures in Tokyo, Siberia and the Congo basin differ, but from a macroscopic temporal perspective, these differences tend to equalize over time. The temperature of the earth as well, interrelated with that of the surrounding universe, will eventually and infinitely approach a mean value.

An increase in entropy means a decrease in idiosyncrasy, tending to a maximum of equalization. Just as all colors merge into gray, there is a complex world of great amounts of energy as the result of the increase in entropy.

![](images/9b9662d165b39dd60b72e91995c3915f8e88469fc5a1a38f15de297e22a3aad7.jpg)  
CHAMBER of CURIOSITIES

from the Collection of The University of Tokyo

Pictured are the leg bone of a large mammal and the skull of a small monkey. The Chinese character for "white" is said to have developed from the hieroglyph for "skull." In ancient societies, the objects most naturally engraved as "white" in the consciousness of our ancestors were the bones of animals and fellow humans.

All thermal energy, whether it is the temperature of a coffee cup or of Tokyo or of the earth, is preserved as one huge average. Yet this chaos is neither death nor nothingness. Perhaps the energy that has become no particular entity but at the same time is potential itself—the potential to become anything, the thing that juts out of this immense chaos as it decreases entropy—is both life and information. These are the taking of flight by shaking off the gravitational field of entropy.

White has risen from chaos and is the original form of life and information. White is the extremity of negative entropy, driven to cleanly escape from every sort of chaos. Life glitters as color, but white is the inclination to reach the pole opposite from chaos, and in purity, escape even from color. Life is born into the world wrapped in white, but from the moment real, exteriorized life sets foot on the soil, it takes on color, just as life in the form of a yellow chick begins with the hatching of an egg. White is something that cannot be realized in an actual world. We may feel as if we have seen or touched white, but this is an illusion. White in the real world is categorically soiled; it is merely the trace of the aspiration towards white. White is delicate and easily damaged. Even at the moment of its birth, it is not a perfect white. It is already soiled to a degree that we cannot even sense it when we touch it. And yet this gives white all the more prominence in our consciousness.

According to Doctor Shizuka Shirakawa, one of the leading scholars in the field of ancient writing symbols, the Chinese character for white is the ancient symbol for skull. In the early days of writing, the image of white that captured the imaginations of the people was a skull abandoned in a field, weather-beaten and exposed to the sun. If they walked through the desert, they would have found the bones of beasts, and if along the seashore, the bones of sea animals, or shells, would have dotted the sand, so these skulls, bones and shells gave the impression of white as a trace of life.

White exists on the periphery of life. First, milk is white. Giving suck, for animals, is a vital activity, one that passes the life of a parent to its offspring. The whiteness of breast milk is a shared trait among human beings

2003   
11

Traces on a white surface, as if it has been scraped with a spoon or something. White, with materiality, thickness and depth, gives us the impression of tangibility.

and the other mammals. Milk carries an abundance of nourishment for life itself, so the white we call "milk white" is one that in our minds is cloudy with organic matter. The taste of breast milk is likewise the taste of "milk white" and of organic matter. It is fascinating that the food of life, that which drips from the nipple, is white.

Many eggs are also white. It's not that white birds lay white eggs; blue birds, black birds, even alligators and snakes lay eggs of pale white. Within this white dwells a real life, and when it comes into the world, breaking the shell of the egg, the membrane between that world and this, it is no longer white, but the color of a living creature. Could this mean that all animals, born into this world as living entities, have already begun their journey towards chaos?

White lies on the brim of the image of life, that is, of information, which has soared up out of great chaos. Chaos is like the world and white is like a map, or a figurative representation. Mapping the world, or generating figurative representations, is graphic design. White is the original form of life. I see the original form of my own work as the imagining of white rising to majestic stature from chaotic gray.

![](images/bf1a401f88544d2673554d179318ebf8be77cc3497c28c1d218160f2e698d294.jpg)  
Cover designs for the magazine "A Book."

![](images/52e9eb66e81bfad553187ec442ea7ef253b8fa23015f4db1842fccaac47cae52.jpg)

![](images/4ed496d392f9e02f12ebb5ea0f1e662023c1e3f026db274e6691b7d3e6d5ea7d.jpg)

5

Nothing, Yet Everything

![](images/e742bedfe179f62d1bd48e0731a6e8140a30da3fae3122751e55405431b40558.jpg)

# MUJI Nothing, Yet Everything

Visualizing the MUJI Concept

Since the 2002 advertisement campaign, I've been both MUJI's art director and a member of its advisory board, helping to create its vision. These two jobs differ slightly in content and in my position, but coincide in the visualization of the MUJI concept. Born into the Japanese market in the 1980s, the original product concept was rather naive. I believe that it is my role to communicate in such a way as to empower that concept to meet today's global context, in which a wiser way of dealing with manufacturing, resources and the environment has begun to arise. No longer a localized experiment, MUJI is now a practical project operating within the global consumer culture.

![](images/efed57dbcdf64ef117e48797c362a88f2cf060312f973e6d6433820cc8ce81dc.jpg)  
MUJI advertisement, 2003, "Horizon" poster, Uyuni Salt Lake.

MUJI's perspective is unique; in the MUJI concept, design intervenes in the making of things.

This counters the rest of the world, which runs on the fuel of capital and appetite. Japan, looking upon the world from its detached location at the eastern end of Asia, has built an aesthetic that is infinitely attractive to human rationality, not within luxury and extravagance, but simplicity. Doesn't affluence lie within understatement? This is what MUJI asks the world.

![](images/3f39b92d12c8e1aaa720879b609070efcbef9b3043e4c7259598f1bcaf94547f.jpg)

## What Ikko Tanaka Passed on to Me

In August of 2001, Ikko Tanaka, MUJI's first art director, asked me to take over the art direction of the company and join its board. With the flow of time, the role of founding members also changes. The deliberation and decision that Mr. Tanaka, although he still held sway, wished to pass his role on to a new generation came as a surprise.

For the rest of the day, I ruminated on what kind of vision would come to me. As I was considering the future of this brand through a panoramic global perspective, I became oddly elated. It wasn't expanding the number of overseas shops. What came to me instead was posing the MUJI concept to the great talents of the world.

The next day I let Mr. Tanaka know that I would happily accept his offer. I also proposed that one more designer of my generation join the board. This was Naoto Fukasawa, a product designer who had done superb work for

![](images/a3d27fae81ba9a8e0801267f9f6162dfd82b49149c757db51a4b596acbd38861.jpg)

MUJI for several years. I intuitively felt that his participation would be vital to restructuring MUJI's product quality. It was 8 January 2002 when I brought Mr. Fukasawa to Mr. Tanaka's office to introduce them. Over Japanese tea and buns, we talked of many things, including the prospect of Mr. Fukasawa's taking care of product design matter.

This work kept me up at night, it was so fascinating." These were the words of Ikko Tanaka, one of Japan's most illustrious designers. Our conversation took place just three days before his sudden death. So transpired the eleventh hour passing of the MUJI baton from the senior generation to ours.

## MUJI's Origin, MUJI's Challenge

The concept of MUJI is the offspring of the good rapport between the aesthetics of the graphic designer Ikko Tanaka and the vision of Seiji Tsutsumi, an undisputed leader of Japan's distribution industry. The company came into

![](images/4ed9f7c28cb31844d75408a9a113ae984e22340d9946724f866478950d453947.jpg)

# MUJI

MUJI magazine advertisement, 2002.

existence in the fall of 1980. Its basic concept was the creation of a group of extremely straightforward, low-cost items by thoroughly simplifying the production process.

MUJI's initial slogan, "Lower Priced for a Reason," was the work of Kazuko Koike, who helped prepare MUJI's debut. Ms. Koike, a well-known curator of contemporary modern art, also acted as a MUJI slogan copywriter. MUJI's management is unique in that creators and managers carry equal weight in supporting the company's vision. Rationalizing the production process is a common idea today, but MUJI's simplification has never resulted in cheapening. Instead, it has led to the manifestation of aesthetics. MUJI, established as a private brand of the Seiyu supermarket, has grown into a

![](images/4354bf12075695c431483ebd08a58bde1248db976f39dca477c2943b1b3489b4.jpg)

# MUJI

solid corporate entity with consumer support. In 1983 MUJI went independent, opening its first street storefront in Tokyo's Aoyama neighborhood. Interior designer Takashi Sugimoto participated.

MUJI's fresh, pure products were the result of product development in the service of quality and function, as well as simple packaging and the use of unbleached paper. Take, for example, the unlikely hit product "Broken Dried Shiitake Mushrooms." This product rewrote the book on the dried shiitake market, in which only those with perfect caps were considered saleable merchandise. The MUJI product is manufactured on a commercial basis using only broken shiitake or shiitake in poor shape, those that were once excluded. In fact, shiitake are usually finely minced for cooking, so the appearance has nothing to do with flavor. It was simply a shifting of priorities that led to a low-priced dried shiitake product. The same can be said of paper. If the bleaching process is skipped, the final color is pale beige. MUJI uses this paper for packaging, labels, and other materials. The thorough effort of simplifying the production process has led to the emergence of a whole list of products with unparalleled aesthetics. The clear-cut contrast of these products with the conventional, excessively produced merchandise of the era affected not only Japan, but the rest of the world. MUJI products earned the support of consumers, who are highly conscious of their living environment, and of opinion leaders, with their sophisticated perception. There are more than 270 MUJI branches in Japan and more than 7,000 products.

![](images/8774dab36e9852d614bf271bbbc188301729556d459561b535c53fb5b52574a6.jpg)

![](images/f79b7befc207c21c4074d8706a737da079eb7b0e846ceb88b18e6e3f1b4b9206.jpg)  
Concept book for MUJI Milano Salone, 2003, contrasting the Earth/the earth and MUJI products.

MUJI also has overseas branches in Europe and Asia that have attracted a tremendous global response.

MUJI also has several problems to resolve. In the beginning, the rationalized production process led to an overwhelming price advantage. But as today's industries have founded their production base in countries with low labor costs, we find it difficult to replicate the price advantage of the early days. We could compete on product cost by following the same methods, but the MUJI concept isn't based on being "cheap." We can't lose our precious spirit in a frantic effort to lower costs. Furthermore, the whole idea of producing in a country with low labor costs and selling them in countries with high labor costs is not sustainable. MUJI should be based on the ultimate rationale of adequate quality that makes sense and travels to the farthest reaches of the world. So now, we must appeal to consumers not with a relentless pursuit of the cheapest things, but with the very most compatible price range.

![](images/d48f79eee29ff365e25e060c01241a73a0109b51b43c3ad5a92121d19fab4b50.jpg)

Even in the case of unbleached paper, because of the high volumes of paper being bleached, using unbleached pulp doesn't always mean lower costs. In some cases, singling something out for special treatment results in the contradiction of higher costs. Baby powder, for example, is more expensive if unbleached. We are living in an era in which it costs more to use raw, pure materials.

Production cost isn't the only problem. Omitting production processes doesn't automatically lead to good products. In the cases of notebooks and shitake mushrooms, it does, but what about chairs? Simplifying the production process doesn't always make a good chair. A chair requires experientially enhanced insight, design and skills. Essentially, the same is true of clothing, daily commodities, other furniture, electric household appliances and processed foods. With a list of 7,000 products, a substantial living environment ought to be possible through a combination of these items. If we list only those products based on omission, the group is bound to lose some of its flavor, and the user will certainly feel that something is missing. Omission-based production is also easily copied. The MUJI ideal is a product so enlightening that by simply touching it, we will gain a new consciousness of life and living. Ideally, specific subtraction or omission leads to the exposure of the object's very essence, through the search for the optimal shape and optimal material, screening out the egoism of the creator and the designer. The product then would not be due to omission but to ultimate design. In the beginning "no design" was the MUJI slogan. Gradually, it has become obvious that if we are to firmly realize the MUJI concept, a very high level of design is indispensable.

![](images/b01645a007388f19b67ba0ef2b5630f815f8a3560256697cf34aa129c1cd5898.jpg)

## Acceptance, not Appetite

By the way, just what is the level to which MUJI products aspire, to what extent are our customers satisfied with the products? As a brand, MUJI has neither striking idiosyncrasies nor specific aesthetics. We don't want to be the thing that kindles or incites intense appetite, causing outbursts like, "This is what I really want," or "I simply must have this." If most brands are after that, MUJI should be after its opposite. We want to give customers the kind of satisfaction that comes out as "this will do," not "this is what I want." It's not appetite, but acceptance. Even within acceptance, however, there is an appropriate level. Our goal is to elevate it as high as is possible.

The remark "I must have this" is characterized by a candid attitude that makes clear one's will. When you are asked what you would like for lunch, it feels better to reply, "Spaghetti is what I really want," not, "Spaghetti will do." Besides feeling better, the latter extends a certain courtesy to the dish. The same applies to tastes in fashion or music, lifestyle, and so on. Like the value of individuality, the attitude of clearly expressing likes and dislikes has been unnecessarily esteemed. Desire and freedom may carry similar values. Now that I think about it, in the modern era, we have constructed a society in which every single person has been able to express his or her desire. Once, only kings and aristocrats could voice their appetites, but now everyone enjoys articulating his likes and dislikes; I like Paris; I love Tokyo; I want to visit Buenos Aires. I want to be a baker, a businessperson, a musician. Which is to say that the modern era is a society that can decide everything, based completely on individual subjectivity: domicile, occupation, nutrition. At the risk of being misunderstood, I believe it can be said that the management of desire is democracy, and the competition of desire, the free market economy. For some time now, the world has evolved following this sense of value.

Still, I would like to recognize the fact that desire sometimes involves obsession, causes egoism, or strikes a sour note. I wonder if humankind, having rushed after desire, has finally reached an impasse. Both the consumer society and individual cultures, chasing after desire and driven by appetite, are hitting a wall. In this sense, today we should value the qualities at work in acceptance: moderation, concession, and detached reason. Might acceptance be a form with one more level of freedom? Acceptance might involve resignation and slight dissatisfaction, but raising the level of acceptance thoroughly eliminates both. To generate "this will do," by creating this very dimension of acceptance, one that is clearly self-confident and also truly competitive in a free economic society: this is MUJI's vision. The sense of value that MUJI holds now can be an extremely useful concept for the entire world in the days ahead. We could call it "global rational value," a philosophy that advocates the use of resources and objects according to an exceedingly rational perspective.

As many have noted concerning the environmental problems that overshadow the future of the earth and mankind, we have already passed the point of any revolution in consciousness or enlightenment; it's time to take the most effective practical measures to counter the situation in our daily lives. One of our world's grave problems today, the clash of civilizations, reveals two truths. First, the quest for profit, once guaranteed by the free market economy, is at the beginning of the end. Second, the stubborn insistence on cultural individuality probably acts as an obstacle to global harmony and mutual benefit. What will become indispensable to our future generations is not attaching the highest importance to the monopolization of profit or the values of individual cultures. No, it will be the rational mind, which controls the egotistical one by working from a truly global perspective.

If I say, "a critical spirit and conscientious actions," it may sound too ethical, but in order to balance the world, we definitely need a flexible rationality, and unless such a sense of value serves as the world's driving force, it will cease to revolve in harmony. I believe that in the hearts of all people, the concern and the prudence to counteract this situation have already begun to act. From the company's inception, this ultimate efficiency, or rationality, has been incorporated within the MUJI concept.

Right now, the products that envelop our living environments seem to fall into two categories, idiosyncratic + expensive and ordinary + cheap.

Objects in the first group compete through distinctions, using innovative materials, startling shapes, and scarcity, raising the value of the brand, and expanding a fan base that honors high prices. The second group, made up of products that are as inexpensive as possible, come into existence through mass manufacture, with the most simplified production processes possible, in countries with the least expensive labor costs.

MUJI belongs to neither of these groups. Searching for the most compatible materials, production methods and forms, MUJI gives birth to a new sense of value or aesthetics from within "simplicity." Furthermore, although we omit any production process that we find unnecessary, after detailed investigation, we also introduce rich and varied materials and processing technologies. MUJI works to bring into existence not the lowest-priced products but instead the naturally low cost of affluence and the inexpensive range of good judgment.

The MUJI concept is to point out the "basics" and "universality" of everyday life. This sense of value will be important to the world in the time to come. I would like to call this "global rational value."

## World MUJI

Through MUJI, I want to consider life culture and economic culture on a global scale. And with a global perspective, I want to help create products that most people will accept, saying "this will do." Fortunately, we have learned that there are many immensely talented individuals around the world who empathize with MUJI's way of thinking. For instance, most savvy designers with flexible sensibilities know MUJI. They appreciate MUJI and are happy to work with MUJI. MUJI has always operated on the basis of anonymity, but in order to personify the MUJI concept a little more intentionally, it may be necessary to have talent from around the world participate in product definition. It's time that we stopped thinking of MUJI within the narrow confines of Japan. We have reached the point of actively incorporating talent and ideas from around the world.

Imagine what kind of products and shops would have come into existence if the MUJI concept had originated in Germany or Italy? Or in today's China, where consciousness about daily modern life continues to mature?

How and what kind of product groups would come about? This kind of imagination is important today. MUJI's big vision may be the concept of collecting the universality found in locales around the globe and the acceptance (the "this will do") born from the world's myriad cultures, and then creating from them, and with the most rational processes and most penetrating design, products that we can offer to the entire world. Embracing this image, I hope to move MUJI forward. I would like to help realize this vision.

## Emptiness

The MUJI vision: On the one hand, it considers product development. On the other, it must at the same time continue to communicate this vision to society. This is my specialty. So, first I'd like to present MUJI's thinking on advertising communication.

In a word, the concept I propose for MUJI's advertising is emptiness. That is, advertising does not present a lucid message, but in effect, offers an empty vessel to the audience. Communication is not dispatching information from one entity or person to another, but facilitating the mutual exchange of information. Advertising communication is generally thought to be the process of clarifying the point that you want your audience to comprehend, turning it into an easily understood message, choosing the appropriate media, and circulating the message. But not all advertising has to follow this method. In some cases, communication happens when the recipient, offered not a message but an empty vessel, supplies the meaning himself.

Let's take the example of Japan's national flag. The red circle in the center carries no meaning. It's just a geometric figure. People supply the meaning. Because the flag was once used as a symbol of militaristic Japan, there are many who still abhor it. On the other hand, there are also those who insist that the flag has now become the symbol of a peaceful nation. As I myself grew up in the peaceful days following World War II, the flag looks rather pacific to me. But when I made this remark at a university in China, the audience stirred. Although most of them were young students, they seemed not to share my view of the meaning of the Japanese flag. And then there are those who say the red circle symbolizes the sun, which is a shintai, or Shinto object of worship. Others say it represents red blood, or spirit. Others laugh, saying it is the basic element of a Japanese boxed lunch: a red pickled plum on a bed of white rice. All of these interpretations are equally valid. The interpretation depends on the interpreter. Because it is not aligned with any one interpretation, this national flag functions well, accepting all. The simple, red circle is an empty vessel. Being empty, it can accommodate the impression of every individual. This is the essence of a symbol. The symbol's function is as large as the meaning that can be attributed to it. At the Olympic Games and other such occasions, the slowly raised flag flies peacefully above the crowd, but produces tremendously powerful centripetal force, for it is nearly overflowing with the multitude of meanings, both positive and negative, attributed to it by the masses of spectators.

![](images/cad0cf147765a62c6fd8b98f437202dc02879fc239577a6fd7213416df063839.jpg)

Another good example is the offering box at Shinto shrines in Japan, into which visitors cast coins to the local deity in the year's first worship. This ceremony represents an exchange between a deity and a worshipper. One way to stimulate this divine interchange would be for the shrine to give the worshipper a tangible gift, like an amulet or rice cake. But the shrine does no such thing, only quietly places the offering box in front of the main hall. Into the box the worshipper puts his money and his thoughts for the deity, and, believing in divine favor, sets off again.

These are Japanese-oriented examples, but upon close inspection, we see that superb brand advertisements reveal effectiveness based on similar principles. The ad's nucleus is the centripetal force that allows for diverse interpretations. People who like it pile into it their varied expectations and wishes. The MUJI advertising concept is the recognition of this fact, developed into a methodology. That is, communication becomes effective only when an advertisement is offered as an empty vessel and viewers freely deposit into it their ideas and wishes.

MUJI has many admirers, but their reasons vary. Some think of MUJI products as ecologically sound. Others like MUJI's urban aesthetic, and some respond to MUJI products because they're inexpensive. Yet others take pleasure in MUJI's simple design. Then there are those who neither like nor dislike the products but use them habitually because they do the job. Our advertising message should not represent any single one of these reasons; our ideal is to create a capacious vessel that will accommodate them all.

And so MUJI's ads have no copy. In a sense, the MUJI logo is the best copy and brand mark we could possibly have. By virtue of the passage of time and the accumulation of significance, the four Chinese characters of the MUJI logo reflect meaning to just the right extent. In keeping with this concept, our product advertising has a definite, simple style in which the featured product is placed in the center of an image in which the MUJI logo also appears somewhere.

## Placing the Logo on the Horizon

Our ad campaigns basically follow the same concept. The 2003 campaign uses photographs of the horizon as an empty vessel of epic proportions. The image of the horizon represents nothing in particular; on the contrary, it can accommodate everything. This is because it gives us an unobstructed view of everything in heaven and earth at once. It is the ultimate image, capturing man and the earth. Can't this be seen as illustrating the "global rational value" I described earlier, the sense of value that all inhabitants of the earth should share? No, more than an illustration, it's a symbolic image that allows for and nurtures that ideal. That's how I thought of it.

It was the photographer Tamotsu Fujii who suggested the horizon when we were discussing what kind of image could accommodate peoples' many perceptions of MUJI. Mr. Fujii's work aims to extract the essence of an object from within an extremely simple image. Although he had already done quite a few of these kinds of large-scale landscape photos, which didn't strike me as very refreshing at first, I decided to accept his proposal. I had a hunch that we would not be shooting an outright landscape photo, but a horizon that wouldn't even be approachable without the concept of emptiness, functioning as a vessel that people could fill with their own ideas.

![](images/e60f7af1d384647787b5748cef1ea04f8649964df766900bf84643057420d750.jpg)

This is no coincidental horizon. This is a perfect horizon line, impeccably separating the picture plane into upper and lower segments. Where would we be certain to be able to find a line like this? We consulted a globe. The sea horizon is visible from upon the sea, but the perfect horizon between earth and sky was difficult to locate, despite our extensive search. After investigating a variety of information, we finally chose two locations: the salt lake at Uyuni, Bolivia, an area of 10,580 square kilometers and the prairies of Mongolia. We visited both on location. The images here are only posters, but they function as both the vessel that can accommodate MUJI's future and the vision of that future.

![](images/ee6bfb46cd57ef60dfecbe61f5764418c6e2d6e7d7fefded5624c21b607ab370.jpg)  
MUJI advertisement, 2003, "Horizon"poster, Mongolia.

Location—In Search of the Horizon

In the quest for a perfect horizon, we visited the Bolivian town of Uyuni. The n  0s    Te their peaks at 5,000 and 6,000 meters, stretch out around the area.

Leaving Tokyo, we flew first to Buenos Aires in Argentina, Bolivia's neighbor, and then to Jujuy, near the Bolivian border. If we'd gone any further by plane, we would have succumbed to altitude sickness. So we drove to the Andes in a parade of four-wheel drive vehicles. We approached Uyuni over a couple of days, lodging in small towns along the way and gradually adapting to the altitude. Even so, we all experienced the slight headaches symptomati oaltitude sickness. Our destination was certainly physically r away, but the amount of effort needed to reach it further intensified the sense of complete isolation.

Why would we shoot the perfect horizon in Uyuni? Because of its pihe a l ake  e woThe erl k  lte flat and sparkling white. The 360-degree view reveals only the white earth and the sky thehorizon fills the e.

The salty earth was unyielding. Our vehicles hardly made a rut. A streaky pattern, like the coat of a muskmelon, overlaid the whole area. This gigantic white melon-like epidermis, partitioned by polygons the size o handkerchiefs, went on forever.

Not the entire lake is completely dry, though. Because a river flows through it, in certain seasons you need boots to walk on it. And even though it isindeed water, it's thick, sticky salt water that never streams and looks as i it's stuck fast to the earth. It might be due to its high specific gravity that the water doesn't ruffle like the surface of a lake or the sea and reflects the sky as in a flawless mirror. In this place, the sky is divided in two; the horizon is the boundary line. A vast expanse of the sky extends as far as the eye can see. haWe clouds that formed a symmetrical line, bordered by the horizon. There were two suns. There were two moons. Because the moon happened to be full when we shot, at dusk the doubled moon and sun lined up, facing one another from east and west. It was more than unusual scenery; I slipped into the illusion that we had traveled to another planet entirely.

![](images/5dca69c5270bd4e6b72cdaf531947cfb9773149ebbdce96713630718df4b25cd.jpg)

![](images/294e5153ab66bc06011ccad12eddb6c4c2ba98981e64a518b6eae22ce8e744e4.jpg)

![](images/fc14a4c785cb996eee533dd1fd6e6cdb07d708de96de55ccfc2234ef836dc83a.jpg)

![](images/f650a91674d84c57dbf4f4a4ac2c4ed2f66af2168184d1f564f4006fbcd8d909.jpg)

Why on earth did I need such immense horizons? Because I wanted to use them for a certain project, which would help people speculate on the universality of nature's providence, the prerequisite for the wellbeing of the earth and mankind. I planned to shoot pictures in which the solitary figure of a person stood like a dot on the horizon. It's simple, but it's also the ultimate composition of earth and human being. There was nothing, yet everything. That was the kind of photo I thought we could take. A young woman from

The photographer Tamotsu Fujii wanted to shoot the scene from the height of about four meters. I was skeptical about the difference that fourmeters' elevation would make on the scale of the landscape, but the staff worked through the night welding iron pipes they'd gotten somewhere and lW he derstood; an enlarged view of the earth's surface came into sight. The elevation had amplified the depth in perspective between where-we stood and the horizon, clarifying the grand scale of the landscape.

The shoot lasted five days. The mirroring of the sky in the water was impressive, but it was so impossibly beautiful, dreamlike, that it lacked the earth's realiyAn o we chose instead to take pictures  the clouless blue sky and the white earth. We left Uyuni satisfied.

To travel from the center of the lake to its edge took an hour by cr.From there it was another half hour to the town. There was a restaurant in town called Cactus, where every night we ate llama steak, not knowing what to do with the afterimage of the colors of that horizon, engraved on our mind's eye.

![](images/b624766107a0b8b6039309d30c97357262397c8d1d0f6ca14b92b6eac1ebf321.jpg)

![](images/2339085f63b00e55d4798b5a50107efd6bc12a7cb4fdcce7aed0adf94abe496e.jpg)  
MUJI advertisement, 2003, "Horizon" poster, Mongolia.

![](images/98a381060839892b6872c2c34fa9420d08238745ff8dd535f89db27647a19013.jpg)  
MUJI's 2004 advertising campaign was launched with the keyword and concept, "house." We used the word as the copy for the ad, but in keeping with the MUJI concept, it didn't convey a particular or clear message. It was simply a vehicle for emptiness. It functioned as a focal point for thought. When I think about it, I realize that we Japanese have managed for a very long time without any particular criteria or standards for creating our own style of housing. More than 100 years have passed since, in pursuit of Westernization and modernization, we turned a cold shoulder to traditional Japanese housing. But we still have no specific form of living space that we can boast of to the rest of the world. What today's Japan can do, having reached the point of no return vis-a-vis the past, is challenge this ambiguous status quo by asking, How should we live in the postmodern era?" Although we made a grievous error by interrupting the ecological cycle of nature, we have been concentrating our efforts to restore it: once-polluted rivers have survived and salmon have returned to spawn. It's been a long time since we realized that there was a girdle of contradictions around cities that can be described as beautiful. Japan is not greatly blessed with natural resources. And so we have been able to become even more conscious, and at an earlier stage, of the limitations of the earth's resources, and have practiced moderation and contrived technological methods to help sustain the environment. In this country, under these circumstances, what kind of lifestyle or living conditions should we aim for? At the height of Japan's economic boom, our urban housing met with harsh criticism: our living spaces were called rabbit hutches. But we may have access to a particular rationality precisely because of the difficulties we face. In these circumstances, we can freely plan our own housing style, not talking about dreams, but as dictated by our actual situations. Affluence may be the discovery of rational housing styles that are perfectly compatible with our individual lifestyles.

![](images/cd41e613d98babe061191976855efaeeb35a500e0b43b7d3ec576b112ebbd0e6.jpg)  
MUJI magazine advertisement, 2004, "House." This year MUJI began to sell homes.

![](images/fcb1d9c0c9585d74f0cad44297491bb4fd2b92535141990d5d46ef3fbd99eaac.jpg)  
MUJI magazine advertisement, 2004, "House."

![](images/644251a95dfd40f9e8e266c5ce2f0f4757cc705d66039bb501a75d8a527b86be.jpg)

![](images/ced0f1032ebc19e09d6c1705b944c249ebbc54a15d198f4c5c8d3e6a64e33cd4.jpg)  
MUJI magazine advertisement, 2004, "House."

The guiding principle of the housing style that MUJI proposes is "editing." A living space isn't designed for the sake of convenient construction. The natural conception of an ideal style of housing is nurtured over time, simply through living. Some want to enjoy a vivacious, dynamic life, with a beautiful kitchen at the center of the home, where they can entertain guests. Others want the joy of sitting at a grand piano and tickling the ivories at any time of the day or night. Others want a large bath to which to retreat in rest and relaxation. Other people dream of spending their days reading, in rooms whose walls are lined with custom-made bookshelves. One of our ideals is to allow each and every one of these people to independently "edit" his housing environment in search of the one most compatible with his personal lifestyle.

![](images/01652f5ac7bb5ea9383f39538db418bbaf3fb2abeafb1f08585c95b99e57465b.jpg)

None of MUJI's 7,000 products stands alone. They all share a common and clear-cut design, and so are not simply a hodgepodge of products, but the elements of "lifestyle" created by our customers' conscious and free selection and editing. The MUJI plate is related not only to our spoons and forks, but also to our refrigerator, sofa and storage solutions. Through various combinations a harmonious living space can be freely created. It is not simply that MUJI's products maintain module compatibility. Their simplicities reverberate, engendering a new sophistication in the user's consciousness. This invisible system, the backdrop of life, is what MUJI is.

Our secondary concept for living space is "infill." In this case we call the structure the skeleton, and the interior, meeting the customer's objectives, "infill" For Japan today, which faces a growing number of dilapidated apartment blocks and vacant spaces in office buildings, relying on infill renovation will become quite important. MUJI has begun by redesigning a floor, walls, ceilings, waterworks, storage, etc., not building a structure from scratch, but exploiting the potential of existing buildings in as flexible a manner as possible. The interior designer Tokujin Yoshioka proposed the first infill space, a very spacious single room, made by removing as many partitions as possible and devising a rational, effective storage method. This conceptual space, using accordion-fold plywood walls concealing a storage area, is a model of extremity; it can be freely re-designed to correspond with changes in the residents' lifestyles. This space also fully presents the possibilities of our modular product group.

![](images/b54c69b858e062e1ab161c0d87289647c8b14e55e256fb4d101ea0ae4d1acc3d.jpg)

![](images/08c6800408540a4be935d44559344d2ba390c7e61ba0a166123cc13d29a30562.jpg)

![](images/c59f376ead9b4aaed329e20df7114c58ad89184ca58c00c5254f900af8508c43.jpg)

![](images/4b9f94c315f08d61581bd0444ff6d4e3c0bc78bf50eb57ab514d5a17b6a6fcd8.jpg)  
MUJI advertisement, 2004, "House" poster (Renovation). Storage proposals for MUJI's single-module products. The photos show extreme cases, yet imply the possibility of rational storage management.

Further developing this idea in practice, we designed the "wooden house" as a tangible product, with a vaulted ceiling from the first floor through the second. The idea of a "concrete house" was also entertained. Although they're both actual houses, it's better to think of them not as a largescale product (house) but as a lifestyle produced by editing the entire range of MUJI products, from flooring to walls to doorknobs, lighting appliances, spigots and storage tools. Just as ingeniously created daily goods nurture health in our lives, a congregation of MUJI products will produce a certain form of living style. With this goal, we have to reconsider our entire family of products. When we're finished, I believe that we will have arrived at a form for our lifestyles, a "house" that's congenial to Japan today.

![](images/bdfdf3daf7bd9c76e64c783ddbc287a4c2db191a91c35665ba1a09b745abc3ef.jpg)

The house concept featured in the 2004 campaign functions as both a message and food for MUJI's thought. It acts as a message for the outside and as a guide to reconfirming MUJI from within.

The photographs for this poster were taken in Cameroon and Morocco, in Africa. The photographer was again Tamotsu Fujii, who shot the Horizon ad campaign in the previous year. We searched for powerful scenes that would say, "People live alive." We wanted to find the reality of houses not designed by architects. We felt that if we considered the concept of house surrounded only by familiarity, our ideas might deteriorate. This scene, with the impressive rock formation, graces a small village called Deli in Cameroon's mountainous north. This may indeed have been "the most beautiful village in the world" praised by the French novelist André Gide (1869-1951), who once traveled around this region.

![](images/c2acf22409ecd193843aab5ae047680d2d059a1b4ca28bfbc4155ee2d391c425.jpg)  
MUJI advertisement, 2004, "House" poster, Cameroon.

The residents have no electricity or water system, let alone television. Through the middle of the village flows a streamlet that looks as if it could be crossed in a single stride. In the rich green, we glimpsed here and there mud-walled houses with thatched roofs. Planted around the houses is just the right amount to feed the family. At mealtime, puffs of white smoke rise from each house. Before such honest scenes of people so actively living their lives, a word like "simple" seems completely empty. Rushing around Africa on this location hunt for house photographs was not a delightful experience; on the contrary, it was a bitter time for us, observing again and again these strikingly hard lives. The copy accompanying the image is the single word "house," because this photo can be described by no further words.

![](images/eaae61efb58b4886338d842f16c1e585b70180adffc854237814577b60b4ddef.jpg)

In complete contrast, products star in our magazine ads. Behind each product, the MUJI-proposed lifestyle can be glimpsed, if obscurely. The photographer is Asuka Katagiri (see pages 256-259). The two visions are equally indispensable: one that backs up to display the global scale, and one that steps forward to focus on a single toothbrush.

![](images/1775a7d2583b1030da2f50d8a06386ea35ad920ad4c130fe33d110e51cdab7be.jpg)  
MUJI advertisement, 2004, "House" poster, Cameroon.

![](images/3ffed310bd642f1e077353a8d7b4cf9ae75a353f713c3fc86f49b3be45a513db.jpg)

$$
