![](images/a135f793320fa59972f7fe64bee62a759b6aed5f45cb3b0bf04755bc2ea736f1.jpg)

## What Is Quality in Simplicity?

What is the simplicity that acts as the backdrop for the MUJI concept? What's good about simplicity? How is simplicity related to the quality of a product? The 2005 ad campaign touched on these themes. At the same time, they helped delve into the origin of Japanese aesthetics, which look for beauty within simplicity.

In the photo is shown a national treasure, Togudo's Dojinsai, at Kyoto's Jisho-ji (temple). Dojinsai is the origin of both the teahouse and the space today considered a traditional Japanese-style room. Jisho-ji, known also as Ginkaku-ji (The Silver Pavilion), was built as a villa for the Shogun Yoshimasa Ashikaga at the end of the 15th century. Disgusted with the long-running civil war known as Onin-no-ran(1467-1477), Yoshimasa transferred the role of Shogun to his son and pursued a quiet life at the east end of Kyoto, cultivating his taste for calligraphy and the tea ceremony. One of the country's greatest wars to date, Onin-no-ran marked a turning point in Japan's history. During the decade of hostilities, many of the cultural assets that had been accumulated in Kyoto burned, and taking root in the "eastern" culture that Yoshimasa began to develop, Japanese culture started anew, with innovation in all aspects of its conventional traditions.

![](images/072742ccbe9d0bb38144711d379fa876a465020bfa685dfe8e0d9b791cd40a5d.jpg)  
MUJI advertisement, 2005, "Tea House" poster.  
"Dojinsai" study at Kyoto's Jisho-ji(temple) and the Ginsadan garden in front of the main hall.

Yoshimasa spent a lot of time in Dojinsai, a study of sorts. In this Shoinzukuri-style room, there is a writing platform in front of the shoji (paper sliding doors), which act as an aperture for natural light. When these doors are opened, the garden scenery evolves like an open picture scroll. The deep eaves that grace Togudo Hall with subtle shade, the light streaming through the shoji, the shoji latticework and the edging of the tatami mats: all effect a simple composition displaying one of the origins of Japanese space. This is why Dojinsai is a national treasure. In this room, Yoshimasa sipped tea and relaxed in tranquility. The founder of the tea ceremony, Murata Shuko, who was Yoshimasa's aesthetic advisor and communed with him through tea, probably visited him here.

![](images/9ecb655defad34a76df3806c136aaef692a816517f7dc3609870e9b78b04100d.jpg)

The tea ceremony came into being during the period from the middle of the 15th century to the 16th century. It was an attempt to escape the influence of Continental (Chinese) culture and bring to light unique Japanese values within wabi (austere beauty or elegant rusticity) and simplicity. Shuko distanced himself from the contemporary tendency towards splendor and things that originated on the continent and discovered an aesthetic within wabi. Joou Takeno, a philosophical successor to Shuko, advanced the quest for a uniquely Japanese perception, in which a simple form projects the complexities of human inner nature. For example, in contrast to the richly ornamented ivory Continental chashaku (tea powder scoop), a simple scoop carved from a piece of bamboo delighted Takeno. Only the nodule in the chashaku's approximate center lends a slight accent to its form. One could enjoy the human spirit reflected in such simple elegance. Here we see the further cultivation of the aesthetics of simplicity in the tea ceremony. Later, led by Sen-no-Rikyu (1522-1591), the space as well as the utensils and manners of the tea ceremony were pushed to an extreme: profound simplicity and silence. This is a simplicity that induces an image. The communication method of expressing ultimate simplicity and calling into it an image or a meaning is the basis of the concept of emptiness that I described in the horizon section. Exchanging images via the medium of emptiness is a characteristic common to all art forms of the time, from the religious thought of Zen to the lyrical Noh drama established around the time of the tea ceremony, but in the birth of the tea ceremony this trait is particularly striking.

![](images/a322aea00afee6b906ba94c3aeb798cd3440061d58ad6dc76f43fda4a2eaf39e.jpg)  
MUJI advertisement, 2005, "Tea House" poster.  
Above: "Kasumidokoseki" teahouse at Gyokurin-in, Kyoto's Daitoku-ji (temple Next page: "Saan" teahouse at Gyokurin-in, Kyoto's Daitoku-ji (temple)

![](images/c58ba7e28a1fc0ef2a29976c1e4f3c4080f60e4430060e831d5ec1a8ef402ed1.jpg)

![](images/bbb6b4563aee9cecd2dda8603131cc203e07c82f75216bbac2d612437e2b7000.jpg)

A tearoom is an empty space.   
Here the master entertains his guest. Minimal preparation can stimulate the guest's imagination.   
For instance, the space may be prepared to make him feel as if he were under a cherry tree in full blossom.

![](images/95f7ead0b6c9d0b2f2f9c09406c57a6101f23ec5ce19d3c7f792680d4e2cd4ad.jpg)

![](images/c27da247cf9ec7438150872af8e219bfd2fb524e45d1fb8c5334b006ff92e322.jpg)

The tea utensils Rikyu used and the teahouses he designed seem astonishingly simple to our eyes. The tearoom in particular is extremely small, with no theatrical or dramatic ornamentation. Rikyu favored the kind of space that looked as if it had been constructed hastily, in a natural spot, to welcome his guest. The space is big enough only for the master and his guest to sit face to face. A tearoom is, after all, a small theater. In it there are no pretentious fixtures. A mere picture scroll is hung, flowers arranged. There, the master boils water and serves tea to his guest. They drink. This is all. Precisely because it is the smallest cosmos, a whisper of production generates the greatest image. For instance, if the master displays a flat vase full of water and spreads cherry blossoms petals there, he can thus place the guest under a cherry tree

![](images/6f93cb99881e2b128fd8338c074033a6696518f403d55d909437e9809232e910.jpg)

#

L42088620

144144242+4+x-04 -8

6241##64 2=-2/65

W-03=41423144<0880260

3<-

in full bloom. With a slight change, the same space becomes the seashore with the waves rolling in. Because a tearoom is a simple space unfixed to any particular location, the consciousness of those who share it for a time becomes so receptive that the smallest bit of ingenuity will engender in their minds the richest image. Here is the origin of the principle of "communication through simplicity" that Rikyu achieved. In this space he created superb performances invoking dazzling images in his guests' minds, performances that attracted the warlords and other powerful characters of the time. Communication executed through the medium of the tea ceremony grew into a well-defined artistic milieu against the backdrop of an era of war.

We can say that this is part of the MUJI concept. MUJI's products, simple, yet not simply minimal in form, are consciously designed to be used in any circumstances. The MUJI table is simple but never geometrically plain. It's important that both the 18-year-old starting in on the single life and the couple in their 60s think "this is nice" in the same way. We are not designing

Annually, MUJI addresses its vision with a plethora of words through newspapers and its website.

MUJI newspaper advertisement, 2005.

a simple living room table for the youngster and a table that can be used in the older couple's bedroom. By making a table with the simplest design possible, we create a table that will adjust to a variety of living environments and will be suitable to any stage of life. This is "quality" as MUJI sees it.

The white porcelain bowl featured in the tearoom poster series is one of MUJI's rice bowls. These vessels are produced for MUJI in the small city of Hasami in Nagasaki Prefecture, Japan's traditional porcelain production center. They are strikingly simple, but it is the kind of simplicity that embodies scrutiny of not only today's dietary culture, but also the consideration of all sorts of dining situations. These photos feature a combination of a national treasure and a rice bowl, but are hardly an advertising gimmick. They illustrate collaboration between two creations of different eras that share the same aesthetics. The series photographer Yoshihiko Ueda is an expert in firmly depicting the beauty hidden within an object.

![](images/8867803df0123179b8e03d539edd9a2cf02a75294e1eaa11ff587af61944ba96.jpg)

![](images/e5b1b26494fbc904a78d6b105c9c68a6e4b426d824084a2ccc77cf11060bc692.jpg)

## The Future of Design

I sometimes say to myself, "Look at the big picture and work on the task at hand." We want to look at things from the vantage point that allows us to see not the near future, a half-step ahead of where we are today, but the whole of time, reaching from the past to the present and then on a bit, to the midfuture. The future lies ahead and at the same time we have an immense accumulation of culture reserved in the past, which is another unknown resource. Even if I can manage that vantage point, what I actually need to do right now might be making tomorrow's presentation successful, or organizing a planning paper for the presentation, or arranging my desk so I feel better doing it, or, the most important of all, washing out the coffee cup on my desk. In fact, what we do is just an aggregate of practical, trivial things. But to get to that place where we are satisfied, while we're occupied taking care of all the little things, we need to maintain in our consciousness some sort of guidance device to help us navigate there. So here I'd like to move on to the big picture: where should design go next?

Naturally, MUJI contends with the world economy. Design, too, is definitely linked to the world economy. Design is a driving force of the economy as well as a vital resource of corporate management. The cool, accurate application of design dramatically improves the competitive power of products and the effectiveness of a corporation's communication. Once we understand that design has this kind of power, we compete for the most sophisticated methods and thus the most effective design. But what are we really after? I'd like to ask what the end result of our work is, without focusing on how the products do in the marketplace or the power of the messages.

A person is looking at the horizon" is rhetoric. A refreshing im-

![](images/ba3a9138830bda3080845e8198ced55d6b8f235c3eb2769ceaa1251ded2e12a2.jpg)  
age stretches out before you, but as I noted in the previous chapter, the image is an empty vessel. The vessel can receive people's wishes and ideas but the vessel itself is not a vision. When I think about this, the phrase "education of desire" comes to mind. I'd like to take a little metaphorical walk around this phrase.

## Education of Desire

Design is like the fruit of a tree. In product design vehicles and refrigerators are the fruit. Design functions from the perspective of how to produce good fruit. If you look at the fruit from some distance, you see next the tree that bears the fruit and then the soil in which the tree stands. Important to the whole process of creating good fruit is the condition of the soil. If we're after good fruit, we must cultivate the soil, though that may seem a roundabout path to the fruit. In our metaphor, the soil corresponds to the market and the "level of desire" of the individuals who make up the market controls the quality of the soil. What matters is the quality of their appetite: what kind of appetite do they have for living? The market is the parent of the product, and the quality of the desire in that market determines how competitive the product will be in the global market. We have to delve deeper than ordinary marketing does if we're to recognize this fact.

Right now there are four big markets in the world: the European Union, the United States, Japan and China. If the target were the U.S. market, even a Japanese manufacturer would fully investigate the market before launching production. But as long as the corporate seedbed is basically the Japanese market, the product will remain appropriate to the Japanese market.

![](images/92ff1659a4de5b2ced2b8ea20efeb0e594790363172c28c41d28735872a66c72.jpg)

We often hear Japanese cars criticized as lacking aesthetically and philosophically in comparison to those from other countries. True, some European cars are powerful in terms of self-expression. When the product is a car, you can sense that it's infused with the will of the manufacturer, but not in Japanese cars. Manufactured to suit Japanese desires, they have no ego at all, but are quite mild and obedient. They are economical, perform superbly, and develop fewer problems.

Japanese cars look gentle because they reflect perfectly a precise scan of the desires of the Japanese when it comes to cars. Therefore, whether it's a good thing or a bad thing, Japanese cars exhibit the Japanese "level of desire" for a vehicle. When the marketing is conducted precisely, the products monitor the consciousness of the market where the franchise is located, thus revealing the level or direction of desire in the market.

![](images/dce712011423585817d9b7811a079af494545919b45d9aa8faeb97aa4a08ca28.jpg)  
MUJI catalog, 2006, a semiannual clothing catalog.

The product's character in the global context is affected by which market's desire is targeted when the product is first defined. This is probably much like the origin of films; as long as the premise is that the film will be released in Japan, it inevitably follows the Japanese style. Hollywood movies, though premised on the global market from the beginning, still impart a tint of their mother culture. Movies from France or Italy convey a certain Latin humanity. And that's perfectly natural.

Italy's idiosyncratic sports cars don't target the predilections of non-Italians. In fact, they may ignore them altogether. This is why they seem odd to us, but also exotic. What we should focus on is the fact that a product that is rationally targeted at a local market's level of desire will have advantageous influence in the global market.

![](images/4af6cc03e7a2e022869b2c1e39645d7788f1e5855b345af14f48296241bf2380.jpg)

If marketing is conducted very precisely in an unsophisticated country, the products are also unsophisticated, and sell well in the local market. The opposite is also true, but causes no problems unless the products are circulated on a global scale. Now, if the sophisticated products flow into the market of an unsophisticated country, consumers are likely to be inspired, nurturing a desire to get products from other sophisticated countries. The opposite of this scenario never occurs. The sophistication I speak of here is the power, compared to products that don't have it, not only to educate, but also to oust all comers. I think that here is a handle on seeing the big picture.

![](images/1eaad425f7b16aa632e04a11ed1c68f6ef0ff7dfc98e19199598daafc707c30a.jpg)  
MUJI exhibition, Milano Salone, 2003. The following year MUJI opened its Milan branch.

The problem is not how precisely we can conduct marketing in the global market. What's important is the quality of desire in the local market where the corporation lays its foundation. In other words, cultivating the soil. A bird's eye view of the situation shows the global context, which is connected to the advantages of the company's products. A brand isn't created in a vacuum. It reflects the level of the culture and the country where it is based.

The Chinese food in Hong Kong is great, but that in Tokyo is not so great. If the difference depended on the chef's skills, we could invite a superb chef from Hong Kong or China, and surely this has been done. But this doesn't

![](images/7b3c56cb717cc49cb360d085022c2189de6e9e780b69b2e0930ca1f93154e12a.jpg)

Ep

8654p p+erx3363-

Rxops#f"Euepiovd P8p3o540Oc3092p vOn't # fo Jp2ae#210436=n ARtiNPt"R-dARSPR aueetn #6rpan064 43559nt68eorpt

eliminate the gap. That's because the problem doesn't come from the chef, but the customers. If you compare the number of people in Tokyo and Hong Kong who know and demand good Chinese food, there's no contest. But more and more Tokyoites have developed discerning palates for Chinese food. This isn't due to some shortsighted marketing, some survey of Japanese taste, to which Chinese chefs have catered. What's happened is that the excellence of a cuisine that has long been cultivated on the Chinese mainland has worked its enlightening power and developed the appetite for Chinese food among the Japanese.

The same can be said of French or Italian food, and sushi as well as kaiseki tea ceremony dishes. Any cuisine, matured in its parental cultural sphere, can educate the appetite of consumers from other markets. Now we find that all national cuisines, if served in a world metropolis, reach a very high level. With the homeland culture as its prop root, every food reciprocally educates those in other markets, enlivening dietary scenes across the world. It's a rich competition.

## Fertilizing the Soil

One thing that can be said for the economy in the days ahead is that competition on a cultural level will join the competition in production technology and production cost on a cultural level. No, I think I should call it a competitive performance instead. The competitive performance of cultures supported by locality makes the world richer. It is a competition to create products or ideas that are based in one's own culture or market but can inspire other markets. In this way, the education of desire will compete for global influence. Predicting such a thing, I'm putting my mind to the possibilities of design that can inspire consumers through products. Once someone gets hold of a product

![](images/c78f649a38bb78f58a57899b304e5d250a5ce220860c9e2b33d1f94e2928f71a.jpg)

E

86#P\* a . .

with whose concept he genuinely empathizes, he sees the world a little differently. I think that MUJI can develop into a project that can engage in this very stimulating education of desire.

I've used the word Japan many times so far, but my remarks indicate neither intolerance nor petty nationalism. In fact, precisely because of a global perspective, we must face the world and boldly assert our localism. We don't need to lose our individuality to become global. In the global context, distinction is valuable. As long as we know the world and remain our original selves, we're fine. The basic market for marketing is the "field" at our feet. In MUJI's case, the field is Japan. Even in our collaborations with talents around the world, this point remains unchanged. I need to clarify though that this is not the Japan of the past alone, but the Japan where the MUJI of today is based.

I would like to conclude this chapter by introducing the MUJI 2006 ad campaign. This year we featured products designed by Naoto Fukasawa, Jasper Morrison, Masahiro Mori and others. Once a year MUJI launches a single spread newspaper ad, otherwise keeping silent. On this occasion, we make a point of addressing our vision with a considerable amount of text. This lengthy campaign statement is a message for MUJI's customers and for its domestic staff, and, for that matter, a message questioning MUJI itself.

![](images/0dbf67e38b30f89222adb4c10fdb532d6d313e1e35e94b4bfc0988c1400eb6d9.jpg)  
MU JI: What Happens Naturally Text from Newspaper Advertisement, 2006

In the photo are the MUJI bed and chair. Because the backrests are designed to have the same angle, when the pieces are viewed from the side, the backs align perfectly. Our mission was to create a reasonable, genuinely modest form. This is what happened naturally.

Picture, if you will, a bedroom. Beside the bed stands a chair. There is nothing special about the image. In fact, the scene is quite ordinary. Furniture tapers off at the head. The inconspicuous fusion of the reclining lines creates a spot of comforting harmony. MUJI ponders scenes like these, moments of everyday life.

The MUJI bed and the MUJI chair are both made of tamo, a variation of Manchurian ash. Used for baseball bats and tennis rackets, tamo is solid and pliable. The unchanging and sober color of the wood, along with its

![](images/07b9fc67daa0dba8951c82ea6b59594908b00f43f4db5ce531fe37baf1a3be91.jpg)  
What happens naturally

MUJI advertisement, 2006,

MUJI bed and chair made of tamo, a type of Manchurian ash.

straight-growing grain, burled only slightly, diffuses a sense of tenderness and a feeling of security. Looking for the perfect wood for our furniture, we came upon tamo naturally.

Consider form. MUJI products do not make individualistic statements through their shapes. Objects that are made simply may at first appear monotonous. However, in their accumulation of calmly determined design choices are discovered the invisible comforts of daily life. This is a wisdom that, within the context of history or environment, is transformed into tools. Once we called this "non-design." But today MUJI recognizes that this is in fact the essence of design.

MUJI's design is internationally recognized and highly accredited around the world. At the 2005 iF International Forum Design competition, we won five golds in product design: for our DVD player, shredder, telephone, CD player with radio, and square paper tube rack system. Winning awards for basic products like these gave us tremendous confidence and courage in regard to making things. Now we proudly call this "design," and in the interest of improving the quality of our products are collaborating with designers around the world.

![](images/fff1f21ad0b5c6a70e7408b5de57d5a550adc47200f3e945753e6b45aba4661d.jpg)

The wellspring of MUJI design has nothing to do with fashion or the mood of the day. Our target is neither youth nor age. We don't pay any more attention than necessary to leading technology. The ethos of MUJI is interest in people. Our concern is for those who work and rest, sharing the planet of today: people who create their living spaces with realistic expectations, have fun with their attire, eat safe food, sleep, go on a trip now and then, face the usual ups and downs, laughter and tears—ordinary people. MUJI's role, accomplished through more than 7,000 MUJI products, is to continue to help people have a life that's a little happier each day. Our originality comes from the fact that in our work, the logic of capitalism is surpassed slightly by that of humanity.

![](images/65f1b0b3bb139c35cc4291040820c3325a6f0539108e24f6f887b0575426d261.jpg)  
What happens naturally  
MUJI advertisement, 2006, What Happens Naturally" poster. MUJI's molded sofa with plenty of give.

Coolly observing people among people and life. Integrating optimal materials and technologies...Investigating low cost while maintaining high quality... Considering nature and the environment... Listening to the voices of our customers...Working with the world's designers... This is what happens naturally.

![](images/f22420be4f21a66496cac3c65fa78971db65cd51cd115e4239d8fda2e4d40018.jpg)

and the photography(above) for the advertisement, 2006,

![](images/53c9e064aa84d3c56adc2edcb06d5d38ac5244a5302f46a1aec96a5089695609.jpg)

6 VIEWING THE WORLD FROM THE TIP OF ASIA

# VIEWING THE WORLD FROM THE TIP OF ASIA

Where All Cultures Are Accepted

The other day I wrote down the names of all the cities and towns abroad that I've visited and found they number nearly a hundred. Several have left an indelible impression on my mind and I always find a new place fresh and stimulating. Travel revitalizes me and foreign countries are a perpetual source of inspiration. But I've never thought of living in any of them. I regard Tokyo as my place. New York is creative, but to respect it, it seems I have to look at it from a distance. I'm attracted to Beijing and Berlin, overflowin g with signs of transfiguration into new metropolitan identities, but I can't find any reason to settle there. For me it feels natural to make up my mind to face the world from that tip of Asia, Tokyo.

Tokyo is a city full of curiosity. Tokyo is more passionate than any other city in the world when it comes to collecting information about other cultures. And it is a city that carefully assimilates that information and exercises its industrious intellect to really understand what's happening in the world. It seems to me that behind this there is a consciousness at work that says that where we stand is not the center of the world, and furthermore, that there is no such thing as the center of the world. This is why Tokyoites don't try to figure everything out based on their own sense of value, but try instead to understand other countries' cultures by reflecting on them within their own contexts. I think the reason for their diligence is due in great part to the bitter circumstances of the modernization of Japan as a country.

Within the past 60 some years, Japan has experienced defeat in war and, of all things, the suffering of nuclear bombardment twice. In due course, it also learned the meaning both of rapid economic growth and the money that makes the world go round; and from rapid industrial progress, Japan has caused environmental pollution. Then, by overcoming that, became convinced of the importance of nature and our environment. What's more, the collapse of the "bubble economy" enlightened us about the frailty of an economy that rises and falls at the mercy of speculation. If we shift our attention to the nottoo-distant past, about a hundred years before I was born, we come to the Edo period (1603-1867). This was a period in which polish was applied to the originality of a culture accumulated over a period of more than 1000 years, and then isolated for another 300. Today, we have absolutely no means available that would allow us to realistically imagine the extent of the brutality of the cultural revolution called the Meiji Restoration (1867), which managed to transform completely the purely Japanese "Edo Culture" into a Westernized one. However, people in those days probably expended a stupendous amount of energy on information gathering and study about the West. And no doubt they were also pained by the discrepancies between their traditional culture and Western culture.

To look back on Japan's modern history as a designer is to imagine the disruption of both a culture and a sensitivity. If I'd been a designer working for the Edo Shogunate, confronted by the Meiji Restoration, I might have committed seppuku (ritual suicide). Of course, given our post-defeat fusion with American culture, today more than ever, with the circulation of people and things, the accelerating economy, and our close contact with the diversity of the world, there are bound to be various cultural conflicts. Viewed from a cultural perspective, Japan's modern history is nothing but scars and bruises.

And yet, we've been able to attain a certain awareness precisely because we've suffered such culture-splitting predicaments and pain. The Japanese people see themselves as eternally unrefined country bumpkins, always situated at the edge of the world. But I think we needn't devalue this nature of ours. Isn't it O.K. to think of oneself as seated in a modest position, without considering oneself the center of the universe? In fact, the global perspective only becomes possible when one orients oneself on the edge, not, like the United States, at the center of the universe. With an attitude that observes the world based on a full knowledge of all the facts, not just some of them, we will think globally based on a lucid awareness of both our bad and good points. In the coming world, that kind of attitude will surely be necessary.

When you think about it, Japan is in a special place even in terms of geographical position: on the eastern tip of Asia. The journalist Hajime Takano introduces an interesting point of view concerning Japan's position in his book "How to Read the World Atlas" (Nippon Jitsugyo Publishing Co., Ltd., 1982). If you take Eurasia and tilt it 90 degrees until it is on top, like the cabinet of a "pachinko" game (Japanese upright pinball), the Japanese archipelago is on the bottom, right where the ball catch would be. In pachinko, when you shoot a small steel pinball (about ten millimeters in diameter) toward the top of the machine, it descends in a complicated path down the back wall, negotiating a far greater number of obstacles than are in a Western pinball machine. If the ball finds its way to one of the holes in the wall before it falls into the catch at the bottom, you win. Let's suppose that the top of our Eurasian pachinko game is Rome in the days of the Romans. Japan is situated precisely in the ball catch, where all the balls that missed the holes accumulate. Looking at the map this way, the paths of the transmission of culture and influence are liberated from uniform thought. The Silk Road, starting in Rome and extending through Persia to Dun Huang, traversing China and the Korean Peninsula as well, is thought of as the course culture took. And it may have been the primary course. But when the map is viewed from our new angle, imagination takes flight; there must have been an innumerable

![](images/4f3bc2450c00ddd705d07c4d4aa63cc22a46014a010f868c69195ee84ba633d1.jpg)

Viewing the world from the tip of Asia.

If you take Eurasia and tilt it 90 degrees to see it as the platform of   
a pachinko game (Japanese upright pinball), all the balls pop up around Rome, for example, travel through various places in the world and accumulate in the catch at the bottom, where the Japanese archipelago is situated.   
Below Japan is the boundless Pacific Ocean. It is conceivable that Japan has thus been influenced by cultures from around the globe.   
Illustration based on "How to Read the World Atlas" by Hajime Takano   
(Nippon Jitsugyo Publishing Co., Ltd.)

number of paths for the falling balls to take; there must have been maritime and drifting routes from Oceania or Polynesia, and of course the oceanic silk road flowed from the coasts of India via Southeast Asia. Even balls traveling through Sakhalin Island via the cultural area of Tunguska and Siberia in the Russian North must have fallen into the catch. Some must have fallen straight in through the Mongolian Prairie. Nothing exists below Japan; it is situated on the abyss that is the Pacific Ocean, where it catches all the arriving cultures and institutions. Japan has long existed here. Call it a frontier; surely it is, but aren't there very few places that maintain such a self-possessed posture towards the world? This unique geographical point of view opened my eyes.

Even in Asia, the intentional simplicity of the Japanese culture and the tension generated by an object placed all alone in an empty space are unique. Any example of ornamentation or decoration from another Asian region will reveal dense, elaborate details. On the opposite end of the scale is the Japanese concept of contentment with simplicity and emptiness. Whence comes our sensibility for such things as suki (aesthetic appeal), sabi (elegant simplicity) and ma (negative space)? Where is their origin? For a long time, I couldn't find an answer that satisfied me. But I feel the issue is dispelled by a look at this tilted map. Japan, catching such a great variety of diverse cultures via multiple routes, was probably a very tangled cultural locus. Accepting all and continually shouldering the chaos, quite the reverse of what we might expect, created an extreme hybrid that amalgamated them all in one breath. That is to say, perhaps our ancestors came up with the idea of stopping them all in their tracks, negating them with the utmost simplicity: zero. They must have arrived at the sensibility of balancing everything with nothingness. Looking at the map of the Eurasian continent, on which Japan occupies the lowermost spot, this explanation satisfies me.

The aesthetic of Japan is something that was nurtured as a kind of wisdom to balance the world from our place on the frontier. The Japan of today exists thanks to the combination of three components, namely, a position on the edge of Asia, the special cultural sensitivity nurtured there, and a stance capable of calmly facing the world, encouraged by our grave experiences with the process of modernization. Fortunate to have been born in such a place, I think I'll live here and bend my ear to the rest of the world. Putting down here the roots of my sensibilities, I would like to see their infinitesimal threads grow densely into this soil.

![](images/6768889d226a872b76c7545c34913df8b5dd7d5298798fbcd243555edb26e33c.jpg)  
Interior details, hotel Mukayu

## Tradition and Universality

Recently, two of my friends recommended I reread the book "In Praise of Shadows," by Junichiro Tanizaki.One of them was the writer Munenori Harada, and the other the product designer Naoto Fukasawa. Fukasawa commented on the book for a design magazine and Harada was kind enough to present me with a pocket edition. Junichiro Tanizaki was active more than a half century ago as a novelist well versed in Japanese aesthetics. What impressed both of my friends enough to tell me about this book were the lines depicting yokan (a reddish-brown sweet bean paste formed into rectangular blocks). In this section, Tanizaki describes yokan as a sweet meant to be eaten in the dark. He claims that yokan is black because it is eaten in a traditional Japanese house, whose rooms are always rather dim inside. It tastes as sweet as anything when you have in your mouth a small lump of this stuff, whose very shape dims, dissolved into the interior darkness. Tanizaki says this sensation is the essence of yokan. It seems that both of my friends were struck with this point of his observation. Since I had read Tanizaki's book as a student, at first I only vaguely recalled that he had written about yokan. But in rereading, I realized something. While this is a superb insight into the Japanese sensibility, to me, a designer living and working in the present, it seems to be a concept book of Japanese design, which reached the light of day only through the struggles of Westernization. Here's my idea: if Japan had not modernized towards Westernization, but had been able to impregnate its own traditional culture with modern science and developed naturally, surely it would have produced a unique design culture that could have competed with the west. This design culture would have been completely different from that developed by the Japan that endured the Meiji Restoration. "In Praise of Shadows" attempts to disclose to the world of the possibility of Japanese-oriented design that would have evolved, not under the far-too-intense lighting of Western modernity, but within our own dark and shadows. The book was written 70 years ago but good ideas don't age. I believe Tanizaki's hypothesis is valid even today. If we read this as a design book, we should be able to bring into bloom, in a place still far beyond traditional Japanese culture, a modernity that is neither known nor has ever been experienced before.

I am not writing from a standpoint of anti-globalism. It's been a long, long time since the onset of active international exchange; it would be nonsense today to insist on the idiosyncrasies of individual cultures. I simply believe it must be meaningful to be conscious of the part of Japan that's capable of contributing to universal values. As a designer who was born in Japan, I would like to concentrate on quietly delving the ground beneath my feet. It may be that I want to know my roots a little better. Every time I step out into the world, I find my affection for Japan grows, and yet I also feel frustrated with the fact that I am not more acutely embodying that culture. I don't know if this feeling has reached some other realm, but lately I've had more and more opportunities to encounter locales and cultural aspects that convey Japan's individuality to the present, and to meet fascinating individuals who carry this legacy. In this chapter, I would like to introduce some things that I've come upon in my focused attempt to review Japan. Afterward, I would like to briefly mention a few examples of my work that also convey Japan's individuality to this present time.

## Recreating a Mature Culture

From now on, and for some time, Japan will be standing right beside, and observing, a bustling China in the verge of an economic boom. It's like looking upon a gigantic shopping center newly built in the neighboring town.

The hustle and bustle of the market is annoying, but it's also the economic world's grave new standard. For present-day Japan, which is stagnating, carrying its once-upon-a-time prosperity in the form of its citizens' miniscule savings, this stimulus is too powerful.

However, this is no reason for Japan to lose confidence. Here in our unflappable position on the eastern edge of Asia, in full knowledge of our confidence, we must make a reasonable appearance. And that quietly and introspectively. And under no circumstances should we act hastily to bring unto our own nation the kind of prosperity currently enjoyed by China. We mustn't try to keep step with a country with four thousand years' worth of cultural resources, where one billion, three hundred six million people have begun to undertake a forceful acceleration of their national economy. High economic growth is like adolescence, when fatigue is unknown. Japan has been there already and is in a place to develop into both economic and cultural maturity. Living here, we should be completely aware that human happiness lies not only in the hustle and bustle of a soaring economy. We must evaluate the factors that have fueled the worlds of economy, technology and foreign cultures, as well as the virtues and idiosyncrasies of our own culture. And we must clearly recognize the significance of producing the elegance associated with a mature culture. Otherwise, Japan may be obliterated from the memory of the rest of the world's people, forgotten as a shallow place, a nation that, without recognizing their proper use, abandoned not only its own economy but also its cultural resources: a place not worth even visiting.

How can we recreate the identity of a mature culture? Probably what this country needs is to start afresh with a vision. From this perspective, I sought and found several topics that seem like they would point us in the right direction. I would like to deal with neither abstract stories nor massive development projects, but to introduce small-scale practical examples that are on the road to being realized through the power of individuals.

![](images/a90c69cf44881daa195f6f45b0e84245c68276ef5c6e14c8b55d728160019420.jpg)  
Gajyoen Hotel, exterior.

Waiting for What Nature Brings: Gajyoen and Tenku-no-Mori

First is "Tenku-no-Mori" (Forest in the Sky), conceived by Takeo Tajima, manager of the hotel "Gajyoen Hotel," which is at the southwest tip of the island of Kyushu and accessible from Kagoshima Airport. Gajyoen is quite different from the conventional hot-spring hotels around the rest of the country, which are well built, sterile and generally without character. Here, where thatched roofs line the sky and chickens dart about, you can sense a tension in the air, the force of a subtle grip on the very essence of things, far from narrow constraints. On the brushwood fence in front of one of the cottages, pieces of konnyaku potato are drying in the sun. Elsewhere, a tiered stone tank fills silently with water, cooling submerged freshly picked vegetables. On the way to one of the open-air hot springs, an arbor partially obscures a sunken hearth, where, at the proper hour, bamboo cups are filled with the Japanese spirit called shochu, which is mixed with warm water; the guests help themselves. At the table guests use chopsticks made of bamboo that was cut that day. Here they don't use extravagant, high-priced ingredients; every dish nearly bursts with the vigor of nature. To put it simply, there is no artifice here. To associate with nature is to wait, and waiting, before we know it we are swathed in the abundance of nature. Tajima knows how to pour the gift of nature into his space. This is the overwhelming inimitability that distinguishes Gajyoen and the reason for its unflagging popularity. And yet, popular though it may be, Gajyoen is not bustling with guests. The spirit of visitors who love its elegance quietly pervades the whole space, lending an atmosphere of quality.

Mr. Tajima bought a mountain. At first glance, it seemed impossible that this mountain of 79 acres of bamboo thickets could be made use of. But the location was good. The peak overlooks a wonderful panorama facing the

![](images/297d8a59c2a3a2f97a4f97934617e4ac6e075eeeac474c0b5bd7bdc58b8dae6b.jpg)  
Wooden terrace, Tenku-no-Mori resort.

Kirishima Range and is secluded and secure from prying eyes. With his own hands and the aid of a few staff members, Tajima tackled the nearly hopeless task of clearing the thick bamboo growth. In seven years, the mountain was transformed from a bamboo grove to a breezy hardwood paradise. Now it is a first-class resort destination in every way. However, Tajima has no intention of building any large-scale accommodations. Apparently he plans to have only five rooms built, scattered here and there around the place. Five rooms on 79 acres! This far exceeds the average sense of scale, but if the goal is to create a utopia where human beings can experience happiness in the bosom of nature, this is what you'd come up with, I think. This is not hotel management. It's forest management. This man wants to manage a place where time passes at nature's rhythm, isolated from the artificial, not exerting effort and care to make something, but waiting for what nature brings. One day we'll have places where people can feel like Adam and Eve, and these forest spaces will gently destroy the whole "resort" concept we've entertained thus far. Right now, near the mountaintop, is a spacious wooden terrace facing the distant Kirishima Range and a trial open-air hot spring with a fantastic view.

## Reclaiming Japan's Quality in the World's Eyes: Obuse-do Corporation

Next is Obuse-do, a business situated in Nagano Prefecture in central Japan and run by one of the descendants of a wealthy merchant who acted as a patron of Hokusai Katsushika, the painter known for his landscape pictures in the Edo period. The central townscape is dotted with Obuse-do's concerns: the company's principal businesses are traditional shops selling chestnut sweets and the factories making them; additionally, Obuse-do runs a sake brewery, Japanese- and Western-style restaurants and bars and the Hokusai-kan art gallery, and maintains a Japanese garden. The C.E.O. Tsugio Ichimura is the central figure in gathering people with an eye for good culture. Among them is a U.S. citizen, Sara Marie Cummings, whose 1993 trip to Japan to help prepare for the '98 Winter Olympics in Nagano led to a visit to the city of Obuse. Today she holds the unique role of "producer" for the company. Cummings proposed renovating the storehouse of the sake brewery Masuichi Ichimura as a sake shop and Japanese-style restaurant. She was aided by architect John Morford, whose interior design for the Park Hyatt Tokyo suggested an ability to design Japanese-style spaces. Once under the guidance of Frank Lloyd Wright (1867-1959), Morford opened an office in bustling Hong Kong because he was attracted to Asian culture. It suggests something that these two people involved in redeveloping the Obuse brewery are both from the United States. Fairly evaluating Japanese culture in a global context may not be something we Japanese can accomplish. Morford designed the restaurant as an open kitchen, but in a corner of this liberated kitchen squats a huge traditional kamado, a ceramic stove with two holes meant to accommodate large iron pots. Thus is the Japanese atmosphere accomplished. No detail is left unexamined or unattended. The male cooks and servers are attired in bappi (livery jackets) designed exclusively for the brewery. They receive Cummings' strict instructions on all things. For example, they are to carry the restaurant's lacquerware and ceramics, whose finer-than-average arabesque karakusa pattern was specified by Cummings, not using two hands for one dish but rather with one dish in each hand, and these dishes must be large, communal plates. Above and beyond managing the restaurant, Cummings became a sake sommelier and revived one of the brewery's varieties called "Hakkin." The Masuichi brewers accept Cummings'vision. With the clear insight that no vision but hers can motivate the peculiarity of Japan, blonde-haired Sarah influences not only the brewers but the city's government officials as well. This serial movement has created a large cultural impetus in the area. A seminar she hosts on the day of each month corresponding to the number of the month (1/1, 2/2, etc.) to which Cummings invites guest speakers from around the country, is one aspect that has attracted particular public attention.

![](images/f8000ef559a78e0b2f64b3c7540d78f9704b62c8d7fb1cee302d5303fef2595e.jpg)  
Interior details, Obuse-do.

![](images/8f1eec0d95a6bc945465991c4dee60b146025ecee8c1b40f73c547f4cb1cbd7c.jpg)  
Interior details, hotel Mukayu.

Unearthing the Meaning of Nothingness: Mukayu

Let me relate one more hot-spring hotel story. In the photo is pictured a hotel located in Kaga, Ishikawa Prefecture, on the Sea of Japan. Originally named Beniya, the hotel gained an annex, Mukayu, and is now called Beniya-Mukayu. Its architect, Sey Takeyama, named the annex. As coined by the Chinese philosopher Chuang-tzu, mukayu means nothingness or idleness. But therein lies a complete upending of common values; it comprises a vision in which that which seems useless at first glance is all the more abundant. A vessel that's empty has the possibility, precisely because it is empty, to hold things inside. In the same way, abundance lies in possibility—the possibility that exists before anything occurs. This philosophy, in which the potential of possibility is seen as power, has been common to China and Japan since ancient times. Takeyama designed this annex with the concept of evoking the power of the potentiality of "nothingness" and so named it. Mukayu will certainly vitalize both the concept and the space, as the hoteliers Kazunari and Sachiko Nakamichi have been working hard to lend a face to both the concept and the space of Mukayu and have fortunately been very successful bringing in more people.

One of the hotel's distinctive features is its garden, located in the center of the site, which looks like a large thicket of assorted hardwoods. It bears no resemblance to any of the elaborate, artistic gardens found in the temples of Kyoto. Here, maples, pines, camellias, Chinese quinces and other trees grow freely and thrive. Flourishing natural formations sometimes permeate nature that's been left relatively untouched. In the fresh green season, new maple leaves inundate the garden. The windows of every guest room are opened wide onto the garden. And so the flood of young leaves, transformed into currents of dappled light, spills into each room. The rooms as Takeyama has planned them are in the Japanese style, but this is as spaces that, like an empty vessel, accept the garden scenes that pour in. He has provided guests with an empty vessel, a receptacle both for the garden spectacle and for the time spent there; this is Mukayu. The master and mistress of the inn both tend to this quality of "emptiness." Their arrangement of wildflowers in the alcove illustrates only that the space is an empty space that is tended with care; the flower arrangement does not perform as an addition, or a decorative agent. The concept of nothingness as value is also accomplished with simple furnishings, which accentuate this place where the only ambient sound is the wind rustling the trees. Then, next to the sitting room of each suite there is the Japanese bath, always full of hot water. This cypress tub, filled to the brim for the purpose of savoring the garden from the water, reflects the trees as in a mirror. As the body slips in, wavering trees and water flow together over the rim.

![](images/c231a2ec26f35f328ed14c8a3398ccaa0425b2fa4682f1b9bd254336e4fea339.jpg)  
Interior details, hotel Mukayu.

Mukayu has no noise-emitting entertainment facilities. Instead, it has a library that is absolutely perfect for reading. Because the library also faces the garden, its light is from the green of the garden. All other light filters into the room through shoji (paper screens). When I stay here, I don't go out at all; I take pleasure in the quality of the empty time and space. It seems as if there must be other lodgings where we can do this, but in fact, there aren't many.

![](images/0b61d41c03d4428fa5eb174a1dea485717da30a23f57cf05e4c06141c596822e.jpg)  
Water bottle for hotel Mukayu, 2003.

![](images/c46e83af5d5aad36ca119a16b4a72c1408dffcebe5cb70b3a30d7208094f9de2.jpg)

![](images/e7a4e619dce04429222807c3b32d0fc768fae248b26f42e1536dc70f869e76c1.jpg)

"Washbasin" placed at spa, Mukayu, 2006.

This washbasin is a modern interpretation of the traditional installation for washing the hands and purifying the spirit. Water droplets swiftly roll along the water-repellent plate and are conducted toward the large, shallow funnel-shaped plate, eventually spiraling into the square hole in its center at a leisurely pace. The installation is designed to help viewers appreciate the purity of water and attain peace of mind.

![](images/bd3f7e545603d6467be549e4e3894ddd4aff7570bbdbfe59501cefd9846f31da.jpg)

![](images/8e9bc3e57fba1bfc852e9072161a31500252b8764f09f55ddeac6afcccb0414d.jpg)  
VIEWING THE WORLD FROM THE TIP OF ASIA

![](images/ff2cf416be37cb16ad8d8a3db1c2098386256da87bf85c4f37ac06d02a97161c.jpg)

Packaging for Japanese sake "Hakkin" for Masuichi Brewery, Obuse, 2000.

## Identity Breeds Attraction

None of these supremely enchanting examples can help but inspire people. The concept of Tenku-no-Mori is intent on maintaining not popularity, but tranquility. This secluded forest, isolated and serene, commanding its superb view, is dotted with shelters spaced at generous distances. This is the most sumptuous situation. There must be people everywhere who are searching for this sort of majesty; if by chance they happen to be deep in the mountains of Kagoshima, they'll certainly discover this sumptuousness. This is something that answers a demand that exists around the world, so perhaps a tranquil culture could become a kind of model for the rest of the world.

As for Obuse-do, its directors grasped the primary advantageous aspects of Japan within the context of the world's cultures and then focused on boldly modernizing and utilizing those characteristics. What's more, the owner had the insight and resolution to entrust the historically important area of Obuse to an outsider with a vision. Certainly, the greater China's clamor becomes, the more radiant Obuse will appear.

As for Mukayu, here the people are well aware of the power of an empty vessel and make good use of that. They recognize the value, and the challenge, of fully activating this power. Undertaking steady, consecutive remodeling, this inn is drawing nearer to the ideal of "nothingness," but in fact, this honest and unflagging attitude may well be what's stimulating the power inherent in emptiness. Pretty soon, I think those involved in matters concerning any vision of the future should just drop the idea of planning for popularity. At one time, the term machiokoshi, literally, "town-waking," was heard a lot, referring to a national trend of concerted economic development of small towns, usually based on some amalgamation of their characteristics. But towns that were thus "awakened" are now lifeless, defunct. A town isn't something you wake up; its only appeal is in its identity. You don't wake it up. In fact, you can tackle its maturity and tranquility head on, and even after these have fermented to perfection, you don't have to advertise it to the public, just leave it in its isolation, in the depths of the forest, beyond the wisps of rising mist. Anything that is superb will be discovered, without fail. Identity is that sort of power, and should certainly become a great source of communication.

As a designer, I provided a little help on Obuse-do and Mukayu. I was introduced by acquaintances to people at both establishments. Moved by their determination and work ethic, I lent a hand, really in hopes of gaining a share in these sensibilities. For Obuse, I designed several things, including the brewery's sign; the shop's noren (traditional divided curtain in the entryway, which serves as the door during operating hours); and the bottle and labeling for their newly revived brand of sake, Hakkin. The bottle, made of stainless steel, is like a mirror. For Mukayu, I designed the logotype and asked the photographer Tamotsu Fujii to capture the atmosphere of the inn. These were not explanatory photos, but a series of pictures through which people who have stayed here can ruminate on the trailing memory of that time. I designed the hotel brochure using these pictures. Now Tamotsu Fujii has become a true admirer of the place; apparently he visits now and then, to enjoy a hot bath and take pictures. These are by-products of the inn's charm, and it's my covert role to use the photos that have accumulated as a result in things like Mukayu's postcards and coffee packaging.

As for Tenku-no-Mori and Gajyoen, I just visit. I've never done anything for them as a designer. There is neither room nor reason for it. Once, in the small bar in Gajyoen I happened to see a bottle—for a brandy called Alambic—that I'd designed long ago. I was with the architect Kengo Kuma at the time, and I showed it off to him. I felt quite happy to know that a bottle I'd designed with my own hand was displayed in a place like that.

![](images/75e66b1ec16809e894e04931c71dd9bc4533523eb6540d344bd7e730b8505452.jpg)

Package for Koshihikari, 1999.

Aspecially cultivated variety ricefrom Iwafune in Nigata Prefecture, one of the nation's greatest rice-producing districts.

![](images/0c6a32182d7eeed43f71a3470e38d2a124b38ee809b52cdb58e29b3c30268a66.jpg)

Package for dandelion wine, 1999.

This wine is produced in Mukawa on the northernmost main island of Hokkaido, famed for its fields of dandelions. Each year the flower on the label gains another seed.

![](images/84c15e1ca7332c7e872c59e87f3dd383cba35b337184378812430be565346406.jpg)  
Bottle, "Grace Koshu" for Grace Winery Co. Ltd., 1999. Package for white Japanese wine made from Koshu grapes.

# The Expo That Might Have Been

The Original Idea and "Nature's Wisdom"

As art director, I was in charge of the early promotion stages of Expo 2005. First came the Japanese presentations to the Bureau of International Expositions (BIE), from 1996 to 1997. The opening plan was described in a thick sheaf of materials, but we formed a team to present it with complete clarity. This Theme Conception Committee comprised the religious scholar Shinichi Nakazawa, who expressed the idea in writing; the architects Norihiko Dan, Kengo Kuma and Sey Takeyama, who conceptualized the site plan; and myself. I took charge of visualization.

The official presentation was held in Monaco in June of 1997. The BIE member countries voted, and Japan's Aichi Prefecture, which had vied with Canada's Calgary, won the right to host the World Exposition. The contents of the presentation and the subsequent projects we had planned are still clearly engraved in my mind. But the Aichi Exposition as it was translated into reality was utterly different from the initial plan presented at the BIE venue. How did this happen? There's no need to debate here the propriety of this outcome, but I would like people to understand what kind of initial conception we had for the Aichi Expo, because I think it would provide a hint for thinking about several things: the view of nature within individual cultures, the situation of ecology today, and the meaning and possibilities of the international event called an Exposition.

The theme for this Expo was "Nature's Wisdom." What I'm going to relate here is my understanding of the contents and the intent of the theme in general, based on communication with those involved in planning it.

Since ancient times, the Japanese have believed that wisdom resides in nature and human beings live by basking in the wisdom of nature. This differs from the Western conceptual climate, which posits wisdom on the part of human beings and states that nature, an undomesticated wilderness, should be controlled by human intellect. This Western idea, which puts humans at the center, functions to express the will and responsibility of this living nucleus, the human being. This concept has been very persuasive for a long time. Certainly, it can be said that modern civilization has been built on the human-centered view. But do we call this controlling or managing nature, what today's civilization does? No, we've done nothing but destroy it, wounding both nature and our own living environment. On the other hand, the more science (a symbol of human intellect) progresses in various fields, the more it is exposed to the wondrous subtleties inherent in nature and life, and the more it is enchanted by this truth. These incessant encounters with a wisdom that transcends human wisdom naturally lead us to the modest concept that humans are also a part of nature. What's happened is that the sensibility at the very forefront of science has drawn near to the view of nature that sees wisdom as residing in nature.

The words of the Exposition theme, "Nature's Wisdom," are meant to illustrate this thesis. In order to convey its idea more precisely, I would like to offer a composition entitled "Beyond Development: Rediscovering Nature's Wisdom," by Shinichi Nakazawa. As the piece is not too long, I include it in its entirety.

"Nature has given its bounty freely. Nature, the source of all life, has willingly bestowed wisdom and enabled humankind to develop the technologies needed to utilize all manner of energy and other resources. Yet humanity has not repaid or even duly acknowledged this vast debt, and now nature is beginning to lose patience. The overriding imperative for humanity in the 21st century is thus that of rediscovering nature's wisdom to once more resonate in harmony with the rest of life and nature. Technology was not given so humankind could ride roughshod over nature and wreak irreparable changes. Rather, technology is to draw out the hidden essence of all nature so that it can sparkle with new purpose. Technology is not to enslave and command life but to draw forth the infinite possibilities locked within all life and to open new realms of significance. Thus it is that we need to heed the message of life and nature and to create new interfaces infused with nature's wisdom. Thus it is that we need to breathe the forgotten wisdom into our technology-dominated civilization, to allow modesty and humility back into our hearts, and to restore the precarious relationships between humanity and the rest of nature and among people themselves. This is the great experiment being begun in Japan's semi-domesticated woodlands. These woodlands lack imposing wilds but have everything that humankind needs for the 21st century. The essential experiment underway here is a bold attempt to confront the many issues facing humankind head on and to deal wisely with them. Integrating the best of human technology, art, and culture, this experiment pushes the very limits of nature's wisdom to bring forth a new dimension of human development for the new century. This is the focus we propose for EXPO 2005."

## The Practical Power of Ecology

Today, one cannot lightly make one's theme the earth and the natural environment; our whole assertion will collapse unless we are ready to sort out as well as solve, through practical measures, issues like the effective use of energy and resources and creating the technology necessary for a society based on recycling. And yet Expo Aichi was ready to boldly accept this challenging theme. Backing it were not only philosophical, but also technological justifications. In its rush towards industrialization, Japan committed the grave error of spoiling the environment. And yet, countering this painful experience of ecocide, Japan has developed technology to restore nature. That is, as far as environmental technology goes, we have gathered the power to contribute specifically to the urgent environmental issues facing the world. When we were working on visualizing the presentation materials for the BIE , the official from the Ministry of International Trade and Industry in charge at

![](images/66ec53e5bc107a3c695e548149e44d913050e29c35f56da25dc61786e3fbcaae.jpg)

![](images/2159c443c160225926d5080ca6bc90fdf340d7155a7eef1bc9c5b12fa4e3b180.jpg)

Presentation kits summarizing Japan's proposal   
for the 2005 World Exposition, 1997.   
The plan illustrates the idea of erecting an "ecocity" in the forest.

the time would regularly drop by our offices on nights when we were still beavering away, and eagerly explain details of various situations and measures available for meeting all the energy and environmental issues. Thanks to his visits, I gradually became well versed in this kind of knowledge. By taking measures to counter environmental pollution, Japan is developing a variety of technologies that reduce the environmental load.

In 1997, when we were in the midst of planning, a high-efficiency turbine that generates power by burning waste safely had been developed, revealing a practical path toward waste-to-power energy generation. In Japan, the efforts industry has made to deal with emission controls or vehicular energy consumption (like hybrid cars from Toyota and Honda) are a step ahead of the rest of the world. The plan for the eco-community of the near future that we envisioned in the Expo forest included equipping the forest with technologies like photovoltaic generation, fuel-cell technology, methane fermentation to produce bio-gas, electric power storage and high-performance heat pumps, as well as an efficient control system for all of the above. As a result, by reducing energy consumption to 50 percent of that in an average urban area, and consumption of fossil fuels by 50 percent, we expected to be able to reduce carbon dioxide emission by 25 percent. We proposed that a significantly practical plan be carried out that addressed ecology. We thought of the expo as a model of recycling-based energy and resources, covering not only power generation, but also local composting and gray-water recycling using an intermediate water supply. This was a proposal to test the kind of technology you would need to create the clean infrastructure that would be necessary in an urban area expected to make noteworthy progress on the environmental front. In a world in which we even find it difficult to abide by the Kyoto Protocol, which was enacted as an agreement on the lowest carbon dioxide emission numbers needed to just barely keep global warming from advancing any further, this proposal was brimming with opportunities to make it into a completely rational plan.

## Our Forest Vision

The most important point of this plan was that the Exposition be held in the forest. The supporting idea for the theme, "Nature's Wisdom," was that the more technology evolves, the closer it comes to nature; we were advocating not the confrontation of technology and nature, but the assimilation of technology into nature. To carry this out in the forest would be to symbolically put the theme into practice. Would technology be able to unite with nature in reality, not just end up as pie in the sky? We were going to get an answer from the forest.

The proposed site was in a forest intricately linked with the history of the porcelain of Seto, a city with a thriving ceramics industry. To understand this forest, you must know that 90 percent of Japan is covered with mountains, so the boundary zone between nature and human habitation, called a satoyama, (literally "village mountain"), referring to the base of a mountain near a town, village or city, is a vital area with uses specific to the occupations of the people who live nearby. This particular satoyama has long been where clay for porcelain is extracted and firewood gathered, what's sometimes called a "firewood forest"; therefore it's been repeatedly denuded, and every time, reforested. In other words, it is like so many other satoyama in our country, which combine man-made and natural woodlands and are therefore rich in biota.

The concept of nature preservation often deifies "untouched" wilderness. But human beings are an intrinsic part of nature and forests that develop in association with humans are richer natural areas for it. In fact, it has been reported that as the use of "firewood forests" has declined, forests whose floors are no longer managed or tended in this way will be devastated. Forest floor management means tending the forests' healthy growth by scything and thinning the lower growth. A richer diversity of flora and fauna is observed in these satoyama, where the artificial and the natural interact to just the right extent, than in complete wilderness. The recycle-based urban site model envisioned for the Exposition was based on the concept of the satoyama. Our goal was to realize, in this model city, an ecologically friendly community born of the interaction between nature and the man-made.

Our original proposal even included an intention to revolutionize the rules of the Exposition itself; we planned to change the rule that calls for the host country to clear a large expanse of flat land for the site, partition it, and erect an array of pavilions there. The Great Exhibition held in London in 1851 was very meaningful in that it allowed for the real (and rare) "exhibition" of a collection of cultural and industrial objects from around the world. And thus the enormous iron-and-glass "Crystal Palace" was significant —in terms of providing the space. Since the Great Exhibition in London, there have been so very many spectacular World Expositions in so many locations. However, with the increase of human exchange and commodity circulation, the original significance and role of a World Exposition has become obscure. Even so, the Expositions up until about Montreal ('67) and Osaka ('70) did good work; they played the role of spreading maturing technologies to a mass audience. But when transportation and media have developed as far as they have today, the very meaning of the exhibition and display that are the root of the Exposition is fading. Today, if you want to see something, you can look it up on the Internet. If you want to touch it, you can go to the trouble of visiting in person. It's just a twelve-hour flight from Japan to Europe. In addition, these days, when architecture itself has begun to involve impermanence, and examples of experimental architecture are constructed one right after another, the architectural appeal of Expo pavilions is pretty feeble. What's more, it's clearly a waste of resources and money; it lasts for so short a time.

If the Exposition has any current significance, it is as a way to point out the salient themes of the near future, to nurture the seeds of the next generation of technology and thought, and to share them with the world. One more rationale is the creation of information that cannot be experienced any other way than by physically visiting the Exposition site.

## Assembling the Trees of Wisdom

## The Twelve Faces of the Forest

EXPO 200S

![](images/f235355b2bf02a04fa1a888f6b8378358da735f0876ed863450b075b8406995e.jpg)

![](images/39bed0e83e4e300d8a53d84d124245a49b264d89c0d151731045098c873c108b.jpg)

EXPO 2005 AICHI concept book, 2000. For the exposition venue, twelve forests were envisioned; the Exposition in the forest exemplified the ideal of "Nature's Wisdom."

The vision for an eco-community was a new experiment in technology, but this was not an experiment involving the destruction of nature through large-scale land creation, but rather an experiment in assimilating technology with nature gently and subtly. Because the proposed site was in the woods, we were going to concentrate the high-density urban functions in one location, and planned to construct the facilities very simply, following the terrain and leaving a light footprint. The vision for the "Exposition of the Forest" included a mechanism to make sure we didn't end up with a forest of pavilions.

As for the experiences people would like to have by going to the trouble of visiting the Expo, several epoch-making ideas were under consideration. One was the idea of regarding the forest itself as a "living exhibition resource." If there were an anthill in the woods, cutting-edge camera and projection technology would allow visitors to experience the confined interior environment with magnificent reality.You could idle about the nest convinced of the reality that you yourself were an ant. You might even be able to enter a plant cell from the hair-like roots of a clump of woodland grass. The mantra among the planning staff was, "Experience the forest with the eyes of a bird or an insect," but if you were to bring in the latest technology, you could also explore the intricacy of the forest on the level of micro-organisms and even of genes. In short, we would turn nature as it's found on the Exposition site into endless exhibition resources, without trucking in great numbers and massive volumes of exhibits from the world outside the forest.

Wisdom resides in nature. The basis of this proposal was to try to access the workings of nature's core. There's no need for the relics of the last century, the pavilions and the mega-scale projected images. Simply by putting on our "high-tech glasses," the forest could have become a Crystal Palace in its own way. And technology, far from being a concept opposed to nature, should have been repositioned as a continuation of nature's elegance.

## Forest of Resources and the Environment

## EXPO 2005 AICHI concept book, 2000.

The idea of finding a way to make technology keep abreast of nature in a sensitive manner is an attempt to resolve the conflict between the man-made and nature, and amalgamate them.

## Technology: Closer to Nature with Each Evolution

In 1998, we gradually set the ball rolling on promoting the Expo vision through slow media like pamphlets, calendars and posters. We still had seven years to go until the Expo opened and we wanted the essence of the event to permeate society little by little. An Exposition speaks to the future, but the future conjured for this one was not something that sparkles like a CD, a glow with rainbow color. The image we were after was that of chic high technology so deeply fused with nature that, as it evolves, becomes indistinguishable from nature. The motifs I used to represent this image were from an Edo-period series of illustrated books of natural history called the Honzo Zusetsu (series).

What is the Honzo Zusetsu? According to experts, honzo was the pharmacology transmitted from China to Japan long ago, which consequently developed into the study of all fields of natural-science, including zoology and botany about 300 years ago. Due to the encouragement of the Edo Shogunate and a long period of peace, there occurred a kind of natural science boom. Many encyclopedia-type books featuring detailed illustrations of animals, plants, aquatic life and minerals were compiled. Among them was the Honzo Zusetsu, authored by Shunzan Takagi (?-1852) and consisting of 195 Japanese-bound volumes. As all the pictures were hand-painted, the edition consists of only the original 195 books. The strokes of Takagi's brush vividly convey the exertion of a man engrossed in portraying, with due reverence, the physical forms of nature. These days, we can just take a picture, but in his time there was only painting. Takagi worked hard to scrupulously copy nature by assiduously training his eyes and hands. His work has a look of sincerity that reveres the creativity of nature. The look I'm talking about here conveys not the human-centered science pursued by Western modernity, but a sense of nature that venerates all animals, plants and humans as object creatures in which the deity resides; they are all considered to stand aligned on level ground. These are the eyes of pre-Meiji Era Japanese, before the forward progress of civilization began.

![](images/4bbff51a2d33e5818790cdb9b1e190596507d52b05ae1fab9f09c8194730cf8d.jpg)

![](images/b3b538e0eba74f22a3ac923f9266994778b123fb45d6d116ad4fc90c3ef05236.jpg)  
EXPO 2005 AICHI calendar, 1999.  
The motifs in the calendar are from the Honzo Zusetsu, a series of illustrated natural history books authored by Shunzan Takagi about two centuries ago. The viewpoint is not scientific and analytical, but celebratory of the awe due to nature. This viewpoint is in accordance with the original concept of Expo 2005.

I intuitively felt that these illustrations were a perfect fit for the imagery to convey the concept of the coming Expo. Some may wonder about the use of antiquities for a future-oriented event. But I found it fresh to discover, in something old, the sense of value so vitally necessary today and to use it as

![](images/8d806955e5e48d82406ea99f050f1745fac4edd8f716d3a7896f781c55957091.jpg)

The calendar numbers are written in Chinese and Arabic numerals. The design stresses an Asian identity and not a cheap, glittering   
futuristic vision, but one in which the past and the future, the man-made and nature, interact.

a message to narrate the future. With it, we could well express a panoramic perspective penetrating the future from the distant past.

I designed several communication tools using his illustrations. This is a collaboration with a painter who worked well about two centuries years ago.

## Familiar Nature and Life Forms, as Characters

Here's one more story—about the epoch-making idea of Takuya Onuki for promoting the Exposition. Onuki, a member of the design committee, won the nomination for the Expo symbol and as such was expected to play an active role in the series of design projects for the event. The symbol he designed is a heavy green dotted line in the form of a circle, whose shape seems common at first glance, but is superbly designed. It's a shape that says "attention," or rather, "caution." Broadly put, it is a symbol calling the attention of mankind. Dotted over a picture of the forest, they would generate an image promoting awareness of—or pleading caution about—the myriad lives and happenings of the forest. Encircling the Earth, as it floats in the space of our universe, the symbol would visually issue an alert on the global environment. It would work pretty well as a cursor on computer screens, too.

Around the time I was planning promotion for the initial stage, Onuki was planning the Expo's promotion ad campaign. Whenever we happened to run into one other, we would talk about our respective campaigns. Once the symbol had been determined and the first posters presented, the design committee started working on the mascot character. Both Onuki and I started to think that it would be best if this weren't the kind of mascot that would become a plush toy. A mascot character not only functions as a communication resource, but also as a profit-maker when developed into consumer goods. Naturally, the host committee wants a tool like this. However, if we could infuse this kind of tool with an epoch-making idea, this too would function as a message of the new-format Exposition.

![](images/1a11fcbdf54304d032d33522f039fbd2580c6d307bfbea67575d77267a8e008f.jpg)
