Onuki's vision follows. It was the idea of making into Expo characters all of the life forms and plants familiar to us all and found in every satoyama: the stag beetle and grasshopper, dragonfly nymph, ant lion, the buttercup and the delicate flower of the birdseye speedwell, among others. Children, even children today, love flora and fauna, and insects top the list. That's because they're truly interesting. In the curiosity based in children's instinctive love for nature, Onuki recognized the groundwork for attracting them (and adults) to the theme of the Expo: the wisdom of nature. This idea accords with the concept of searching for the wisdom of nature in the forest.

Of the overseas programs on the Discovery Channel, there are some that rivet our attention and, before we know it, completely engross us. When the workings of nature are ingeniously represented using high technology, the results are sometimes superb. The more familiar to them is the flora, fauna or other life form, the more the accessible it seems, and the more empathy should be engendered. Computer graphics can create truly realistic images of the ecology of insects, so kids are sure to become engrossed in the life cycle of a stag beetle as illustrated through computer graphics. Onuki must have imagined the effect as such: living creatures become the star characters in kids' lives, and information about them flies through the streets like Pokemon cards. He planned to launch an ad campaign that would build on this childlike interest in and appreciation of living things, and spontaneously direct that interest towards "Nature's Wisdom." A fancy plush toy character would be useless in this case. When the subsequent "nature observation" boom took hold, more television shows would be related to the wisdom of nature and would lead to a rising interest in the Expo. In bookstores, illustrated books of fauna and flora or Expo-recommended books wrapped in partial dust jackets of green would be part of some of the most popular displays. This attentiongetting symbol would certainly have had a lively time of the whole situation.

![](images/2b4d58f08d7672e85cf2ef7375dd967bccd9f4494659f2e77ef1ccdd1f14ff9c.jpg)  
EXPO 2005 AICHI symbol mark by Takuya Onuki. The heavy dotted line in the form of a circle draws the attention of viewers.

## The Self-propagating/Multiplying/Breeding Media

The last project I worked on for the Exposition was a series of illustrated gummed tape created as "communication goods." For a promotion campaign, so-called novelty goods are expedient, but I would be ashamed if, in promoting an event endorsing the future and the global environment, we were to carry out a massive distribution of ballpoint pens and Expo coffee cups. I suggested that, as long as we were going to do something, we should create an entire category (communication goods) particular to the event and put together an annual design plan that would make a clear statement. At the time, I was working on the RE-DESIGN project, for which the architect Shigeru Ban had devised a square toilet paper roll. (The project and Ban's solution are presented in Chapter Two.) This led me to the idea of delivering the Expo's message in the guise of daily commodities, which would be used up, and fade away to nothing in the end.

The gummed tape was the first of the communication goods. Used as motifs on the color-printed tape were flora and fauna familiar to us in Japan: Reeve's turtle, the goldfish, and our common grasses. In order to avoid a misunderstanding I would like to explain that the tape was not some fashionable design trinket, but meant to function based on the idea of gummed tape as a medium. Ordinary communication tools function only upon their distribution, and then as mementos, while the tape spreads the Expo message continually and more effectively the more widely it is used. When cardboard boxes are taped, they themselves metamorphose into the Expo message. In the circulation process, a variety of people would glance at the tape's message. We're in the Internet era. The more e-commerce thrives, the more physical objects are delivered. Right now, physical distribution is growing with great strides; creating this series of gummed tape is based on the idea of making use of those delivered packages as messages. One roll of gummed tape can seal two hundred manila envelopes. That is, a single tool achieves its goal two hundred times. And once they're used up, they are reduced to atoms. And so, to the very end, each is converted into our message.

![](images/8cbb9188c457cb439804c57346d17ce3ed699733be115c133ee9933d2a8e4cb1.jpg)

![](images/882943d4910187aebdbbcdef1204a098e2c94f9913ba871915f420932bf3494f.jpg)

We planned to recruit talent from across the country to make one example after another of this kind of communication goods. If it had gone well, a retail shop just for these might have existed by the time the Expo opened.

## The Never-ending Project

To our disappointment, the Exposition was not to be held in the forest. Because public opinion already firmly equated an Exposition with the destruction of nature, an outcry against allowing the event to occur in the forest took shape and flourished. Furthermore, the woods planned for the event site were publicized as a sort of mysterious, undeveloped forest, with a wellknown anchor on one news program phrasing a question this way: "Because the Kaisho-no-Mori (forest) is situated here, the area is wonderful. Do you agree that the area should be used for the Exposition?"

Then, because the existence of a goshawk nest in the forest was confirmed, the movement to make it impossible to use the woods intensified; goshawks, at the top of the food chain, symbolize the rare ecological system there, it was said. This movement, focusing on a rule that is beyond argument, was to leap to a conclusion allowing for no dialogue. In any case the discussion points brought up by the mass media were consistent with those of the movement. The problem was never discussed from a levelheaded perspective graced by essentials; no, such a possibility was mocked by the argument that, leaving the old polar opposites of nature and human as is, holding an Expo in a forest was disgraceful. Without discussing the essence of the matter, the public apparently changed its vision, based on its emotions. This, too, exemplifies Japan.

As a result, the Exposition was held in a location at some distance from the forest. Transferred to a site that made ample allowance for largescale development, it was then rewritten as a plan fully loaded with a forest of pavilions, gigantic-scale image projection, a mega-scale Ferris wheel, and shows galore. Most of the brains as well as the talent that had been involved from the beginning didn't participate in the project in its final form.

These are not problems that can be easily solved here. Behind the cancellation of the initial project also stands firm a mode of thinking that views an Exposition as a public works project and makes the economic effect primary. The event we envisioned, with its lesser impact on the surroundings, might not have answered the expectations of the local economic world, which anticipated public works or grand-scale civil engineering and construction. In our publicity planning, we might have underestimated the influence of a public whose image of an Expo is limited to pavilions. Furthermore, we had not yet accurately infused society with our initial vision for the event. As the director of promotion, I hold this as a regrettable point.

However, even after experiencing such a setback, I have come to think anew that as a designer, I would like to take part in conscious projects with clear intentions, even if they involve aspects that don't go so well. I am not interested in creating messages that stand against something, like messages against nuclear development, or anti-war messages; design functions as part of a planning process. Be it the problem of the environment or of globalism, how can we move towards improvement? What can I do to budge it even one step in the right direction? I want to make design function persistently in this positive, practical dimension. In this sense, my Exposition has not come to an end.

# The Beijing Summer Olympic Games Symbol Design Competition

The Pulse of Asia

The Beijing Olympics is going to be held in 2008. Taking this event as an opportunity, China is expected to undergo drastic change. In 2003, nominated by the Beijing Olympics Organizing Committee, I entered the symbol design competition. The design introduced here received an honorable mention but was not selected as the final symbol. I am happy with how I made it and how I understood its function, and I would like readers to appreciate it as a way of thinking about Asia's design.

Design in China, diametrically opposed to that in Japan, appeals through details. I thought it would be fascinating if the symbol were to impart a complex, serene aura that would shake up the conventional modern symbol, which is oriented toward simplicity.

Every portion of this symbol is comprised of pictograms (ancient writing symbols) representing a variety of sports. As you may know, written Chinese with its units called "characters," uses ancient writing symbols. I produced pictograms that employ this Chinese tradition. The aggregate of symbols indicating the various athletics motifs forms a complex, centripetal symbol.

A modernist tendency is design through systematic and geometric organization. Japanese designers who studied design in Europe created these kinds of pictograms for the Tokyo Summer Olympics in 1964. For the Munich Summer Olympics in 1972, Otl Aicher (1922-1991) designed pictograms in which all the forms were situated at respectively unified angles, like 90° or 45°. It seemed to me that with this work of Aicher's, systematic graphic design had nearly reached its conclusion.

This symbol does not have the severe structure of systematic design, but exhibits a more generous hieroglyphic nature. All of these pictograms are designed based on curved lines drawn freehand. The athletes move dynamically around the central "" that is the symbol of the sun. Thus I expressed an image like the pulsing earth, as a whole.

On the one hand, the individual pictograms function as a motion graphic. Because the structure is designed as a simple wire frame, they can execute charming movement with extremely minimal data. I'd like you to imagine them dancing in the corner of your TV screen.

As the symbol is made up of pictograms, there is a smooth continuity between it and the signage. It's fun to imagine the individual pictograms, isolated from the symbol, moving as motion graphics.

This symbol also functions as a Chinese seal. There are two styles, white," or white on red, and "red," or red on white: a sort of Ying and Yang. Making these two types seemed to allow for convenient classification. And because the seals may be used at smaller sizes, three densities are available, insuring a stable impression at various sizes. If the symbol were actually carved into stone as a seal, it would probably be an interesting tool. Someday I would really like to have it carved by a Chinese craftsman. It will be my private memento of the Beijing Olympics. The photos feature simulated images of the admission ticket to the opening, the athletic games, and the shopping bag.

Design in Asia is about to bloom. There will come a day when the East will maintain a position relative to the West, and the two will associate with one another in a true sense, and together influence world culture.

Asia is blessed with the resources of dazzling histories and cultures. Yet it gives the impression of having been buried by modernization or Westernization. But no, Asia's histories and cultures may be embedded, but not in the ground. They are buried in the depths of our sensory perceptions or in the abyss of our memories. By unearthing them little by little, we will see the subsequent birth of completely new design. From the tip of Asia, I hope to continue to communicate their vital pulse.

This integration of the hieroglyphic motifs of sports-playing people represents our globe, which pulsates with the joy and excitement of opening the festival of peace that is the Olympic Games in Beijing. Reminding us of the traditional seals of China, this figure also symbolizes strength of another kind: 5,000 years of history, the culture of China and the spirit of the East.

![](images/8a6c9832a9da28b9b8afd3da6b355848bb66ee8e7b052af13a3c8d45c1b8c55f.jpg)

EXFORMATION

A New Information Format

![](images/d274eeca75c86b58ab951d0e3b907e14aee95082f73e89052d09f50104bb0550.jpg)

# EXFORMATION

A New Information Format

Graduation projects at Musashino Art University, Department of Science of Design

Making the World Unknown

What constantly invigorates the human mind is the unknown; we aren't animated by what we already know, but we're eager to make the world known. In today's context, in which media continues to evolve, every event and phenomenon processed into information circulates with high density at high speed. By constantly keeping in touch with those pieces of information, people continue to replace the unknown world with identified events and phenomena. To know something is to impregnate the senses with an inspirational, vital, exciting experience. Yet, because the information supply has exceeded critical mass, at some point unbeknownst to us, knowledge ceased functioning as a medium to activate thought and piles of information, like seeds not germinated, were reduced to an ambiguous condition; we can't tell whether they're dead or alive.

![](images/e79e3289215e348bd6d4e1164597c5e57d7ca2c177f8b864632b8aee1933166e.jpg)  
This is a composite of a full-scale asphalt road and the surface of a river. Through this simulation, we experience the terrain as well as the movement of the river with a reality beyond our expectations.

So then, shouldn't we make the world unknown? I decided to pose the question to my university students. I coined the term "exformation" as half of a conceptual pair, the other half being "information." Exformation doesn't mean "making known," but "understanding how little we know."

The project featured in this chapter covers research I conducted with students at the Musashino Art University in Tokyo. The practice concentrated on the theme of communication while turning on the idea of exformation. I have long thought that raising questions is more important than giving answers. Creativity is to discover a question that has never been asked. If one brings up an idiosyncratic question, the answer he gives will necessarily be unique as well. In this sense, I will be happy if this project helps my readers question anew the meaning of knowing as well as understanding, and the essential identity of communication.

## Putting a Full Stop to Thinking

Modern people frequently remark, "I know, I know." I don't know why, but it's always said twice. When the topic is the architect Le Corbusier (1887- 1965), they'll say, "I know, I know," When the white tiger at the Delhi Zoo comes up in conversation, they'll say, "I know, I know." Whether the subject is the design of the restroom at the new bar at Piccadilly Circus, the spa service at the new hotel in Chiang Mai, or the glistening hologram sticker on Japan's recently issued currency, I'm haunted by this feeling that as soon as I even start to mention it, I'm going to be answered, "I know, I know." Are people today blessed with some great store of knowledge? Well, we do spend every day in contact with loads of information. With the development of the media and its vigorous news coverage, it's like the surface of every occurrence and phenomenon in the world is mowed into tiny particles of information, which fly about piecemeal, like cut grass, in media space. Willingly or not, our brains are obliged to keep in constant touch with these pieces of information. As a result, innumerable portions of segmented knowledge have stuck to our brains. This accretion would be all right if it incited further interest, as a kind of intelligence index, but objective observation shows that we only talk about the fact that we've brushed against the information; we seldom get any more involved in the topic. The remark "I know, I know" puts an end to our conversation, like ringing down the final curtain on thought.

## Acquiring Knowledge Is not the Goal

"I've heard the new China Central Television building in Beijing will be designed by the Dutch architect Rem Koolhaas." "I know, I know. Jacques Herzog is working on the construction project of the Olympic stadium, isn't he?" He's from the architects' group Herzog & de Meuron, isn't he? They built the Prada building in Tokyo." "I know, I know." "The Prada shop in SoHo was designed by Rem Koolhaas." "I know, I know." "As I recall, next month a gallery in Ginza is going to hold an exhibition featuring the works of Antony Gormley." "He's a sculptor who uses the human body as the basis for metal casts, isn't he?" "Olafur Eliasson's work, which I saw at the Hara Museum of Contemporary Art, was just wonderful, too." "Ah, I've seen his rainbowlike installation." "That's an early work, entitled 'Beauty'." "Its motifs are natural phenomena like light and wind." "Fantastic. I'm impressed by your knowledge. You are well informed about sweets, too." "Oh, you're talking about the Geppei moon cake from Shanghai, right? That was delicious, wasn't it?" I know. I know it was from Shanghai. There was an article about it in a magazine and I bragged to my friend that I had eaten the sweet featured there."

As a conversation, it seems to be proceeding quite pleasantly. The topics are varied and rich in knowledge. However, because the "I know" remark is expressed with a tinge of accomplishment, it functions as a prompt for the conversation partner to move on, or a way to introduce knowledge on another topic, or the end of one topic and an invitation to switch to another. The person providing the topics only introduces facts. So in the conversation, in which each side transmits "I know" information in turn, the conversationalists never actually intersect. The example I used is a bit extreme, but we're all plenty familiar with this kind of dialogue.

Basically, knowledge is no more than an entrance to thought. To converse is to exercise one another's thoughts by beginning with a trifling piece of knowledge and speaking about it together. By ingeniously mingling fragments of knowledge with dialogue and speculation, we are able to come up with heretofore-unknown images and ideas. To know something is not a goal, but a starting point for our imagination. Even so, as is symbolized by the habitual "I know" conversation, somewhere along the line we've run off the rails that lead knowledge to imagination, and have completely halted the train of thought. The information-dispatch side is engrossed only with throwing broken pieces of information at the recipient, and the recipient has begun to consider catching information as the goal. Evading the troublesome work of inquiring into it, we have become devoted to a game of "information catch." I wonder if this is where the problem of stagnating creativity in communication lurks.

## Making an Entrance for Curiosity

Here's another thing. Let's try imagining not a conversation, but something in our immediate surroundings: information design. Take the example of a guidebook, something from which we gain necessary information for travel. Let's call it "The Smart Guide to Exploring New York." The tag line is, "With this, you'll be a New Yorker by tomorrow." In this guidebook targeting experienced travelers who plan to travel to New York alone, a mass of information is rationally edited and combined for handy reference.

The first pages describe the options for air service to New York from your nearest international airport. These are followed by the introduction of two airports, La Guardia Airport and John F. Kennedy International Airport, as well as access to midtown, etc. Details are provided on the use of and fares for transportation—taxis, buses and subways. After the currency exchange rate comes information on accommodations, and a graph of average temperatures and basic tipping conventions. Accommodations are classified by the number of stars, ranging from ultraluxury hotels to budget lodging. A number of pages are assigned to dining information, delineating even the interiors, as well as detailed price lists, extending from famous restaurants where they serve seafood or steak, to recommended shops and restaurants in Chinatown and Little Italy, and includes restaurants featuring the cuisine of Scandinavia, Japan, India, Mexico and France, as well as fast-food joints, popular delis and bagel shops, and restaurant store-fronts, where you can even order take-out soup. You can find information on shopping by area and street. Of course, the map section is succinctly compiled and designed for quick comprehension. Furthermore, information on cultural facilities like art galleries and museums has all the bases covered, including musicals and concerts currently in performance, nightspots and even recommended spas. There is also a report on recent SoHo news or the activities at new Midtown galleries, in response to readers' expectations for the latest information. In addition, the appendix includes reader-recommended routes for strolling and suggested jogging courses in Central Park.

It was the information architect Richard Saul Wurman who said, The goal of information design is to empower the user." I basically agree with his view and am sure that an efficiently compiled guidebook must serve as a precious navigation tool for sightseers in New York. They would visit alternative spaces in Midtown with this guidebook in hand. They would get to know New York by eating a bucket of mussels at the South Street Seaport, picking up a few things at some museum shop, roaming around SoHo. Home again, before long they would bounce through a conversation centered on questions about their experiences in New York.

"Did you buy spices at Dean and Deluca?" "Did you see the amazingly gorgeous kitchens and baths at the Boffi showroom? Did you try a hot stone massage?" "Did you see Karim Rashid's furniture in that Midtown interiors shop?" "Did you go to the new MoMA?"

Probably you too can imagine how happily their conversation swings along through its unlimited supply of I knows. They toss questions to one another, each to be answered by "I know, I know." Behind their conversation is a perceptible satisfaction in the understanding that they have successfully replaced the pre-travel guidebook lessons with actual experience. It goes without saying that this kind of conversation has to be enjoyable in a way.

To these people, traveling is "sightseeing," period. Their desire is to see expected scenes. We cannot say that a serious documentary approach is of higher quality than the approach of these people in just looking for what they expect.

However, would it be possible, by bluntly disregarding the cycle of exchanging already known information, the "I know" cycle, to create a guidebook that would act as an "entrance for curiosity," one that would help readers sense a fresher, unknown New York? I suppose it would be something that in the end does not make New York known, but rather awakens us quietly, yet thoroughly, to the truth of how little we know about the metropolis of New York.

## The Process of Making Things Unknown

Is it possible to communicate not by "making known," but by "making understood how little we know"? If we can recognize that we know so little, a method for finding out how little we know will become clear as well. As the Greek philosopher Socrates remarked, "The only true wisdom is in knowing you know nothing." There are an infinite number of methods to attain knowledge; finding the right method is up to the individual. This single idea throws conventional communication methods into reverse. I call this method "exformation," as a counterpart concept to "information." "In" is to "ex" as "inform" is to "exform." In other words, I want to speculate on the form as well as the function of information, not for making things known, but for making things unknown.

The "in" of "information" is an affix. Attached to the beginning of a word, sometimes it adds a negative implication. But in most cases, it intensifies the root meaning, or adds meanings like "directed within, on and toward." This is the case with "inform." The word "form" means to shape, organize, or arrange in order, but implies the movement vector involved in taking a definite shape. Accordingly,"inform," with the background meaning, "giving a certain form," carries meanings like, "to make known," "to tell,"

to imbue (with a feeling)." Then, in noun form, "information," it takes on meanings such as communication, knowledge, information and scholarship, and further refers to the service of giving information, as in "information booth." In the word "exformation," the prefix is changed from "in" to "ex," reversing the meaning of "in." The meanings of "ex" include "not," "out of," "outside," "eliminated," "prior" and others. This is the source of the concept of converting the known to the unknown. Notice that "exterior" is already widely used as the counterpart to "interior," so my coined term, "exformation," may well make sense to the general public.

## Exformation—1 THE SHIMANTO RIVER

The Object of the Process

After I demonstrated the idea to the students, we looked for a theme through which to practice exformation. Any theme will do, be it the Orient Express, the Champs-Elysées, water, a refrigerator, or a honeymoon; anything we make light of with preconceptions, thinking to ourselves, "Yeah, it's probably like this..." is a potential theme. We talked over a lot of possibilities and settled on the famous Shimanto River, known as one of Japan's clearest and most beautiful waterways. Because the Shimanto River is acknowledged throughout our country but is not world famous, I would like to explain a little about it. Japan is a country more than 90% of whose landmass is covered in mountains and at the same time, a country full of sharply inclined and fast-flowing rivers.

If you look at a map in which only the rivers are drawn in detail, the land of Japan looms so clearly that the rivers appear to cover the country like capillaries. Protecting these rivers from environmental pollution has been an issue for a long time. Under these circumstances, the Shimanto River has attracted nationwide attention as an uncontaminated and precious limpid waterway. The river is special for the number of fish found there, and many people in the surrounding areas depend on fishing for a living. In Japan today, our consciousness about environmental preservation is heightened, so this location has garnered sincere attention. It's situated on the frontier, on the island of Shikoku, so not that many people take the trouble to visit, but a popular TV documentary, "Japan's Last Clear River," helped impress the audience with the area's untouched wilderness. So we could easily imagine that when people hear "Shimanto River," a vague image engendering a vague comment of preconception, "Ah, I know what kind of place it must be," springs to mind.

I had been to the Amazon, the Sahara, and even to the Iguazu Falls on the Argentina-Brazil border, but had never visited our own Shimanto River. Nor had any of my students been there, although they could all conjure up a preconceived image of it; so we had our theme. Probably every country has a river or two that meet the same criteria. If you were to imagine a familiar clear river in your country, you wouldn't find much of a difference between it and the one we were going to visit. But, being only 200 km long, it's not as large in scale as the Amazon, the Mekong or the Rhein.

## Field Research

Our project may be supported by abstract thought, but realization demanded awfully practical processes. To conduct field research in the Shimanto River basin, we gathered in a small village in the western region of Kochi Prefecture at the end of August. We were careful to avoid the holiday festivities, waiting till our target quieted down a bit. We rented an old house to stay in and one of our local collaborators also provided his workshop for us to lodge in.

We divided into several teams and began our research, but the weather forced us to return to Tokyo for a time.

This year Japan was hit by twice as many typhoons as usual. Most of them made land in Kochi Prefecture, which functions like a kind of welcoming gateway for typhoons headed to Japan. Particularly at the end of August, Kochi is like a typhoon intersection; as soon as one passes, another one hits. Over the Shimanto River, there are 21 "sinking bridges" (chinka-bashi), so named because when the river rises during various disasters, including typhoons, these bridges are completely submerged.

The edges of the bridges are rounded, like the wings of airplanes, and they are designed to stand up to the water's flow, interrupting the velocity of the current below the surface. They are equipped with neither handrails nor parapets. The people of this region might well have built taller bridges that would never be submerged, but that would hardly be attuned with the scenery, and would appear rather dreary. So even though it means putting up with some inconvenience, they have chosen to maintain the sinking bridges, which beautifully partition the entire basin, from the upper reaches to the lower.

We placed some of our research bases on the bridges, so when the river rose and the bridges sank below the surface, we were forced to abandon our research for a time. We came to the project with some understanding that these sinking bridges apparently are submerged now and then, but we didn't foresee how frequently they would sink. They certainly went under easily!

It was in the beginning of October that we returned to the river after having experienced several typhoons since our first visit. My students had polished their plans for this second visit and so did a fine job carrying out the fieldwork on their own.

## THE PROJECTS

Here's how the first year of the exformation project took shape as the "Exformation Shimanto River."

Simulations -If the River Were a Road

Produced by Shinsaku Inaba, Sousuke Matsushita and Hirofumi Mori

This team worked on image simulations. Their final plan concentrated on creating composites of asphalt roads on the water's surface by taking pictures of various scenes up and down the river. Our memory of the size and texture of a familiar object functions as a measuring stick by which we can infer the size or shape of something new or unknown to us. The photograph of a dinosaur fossil next to a cigarette pack sets up a standard for the fossil's dimensions. The same is true of our memories of asphalt roads. Without realizing that we're committing anything to memory, by virtue of repeated expe-

![](images/09d19dff393f5a2ef8035d9614e916902e7b219e572d9d6cf5adf9411d1633ff.jpg)

riences of moving down the road in a car, we recall the textures of road surfaces, the width and length of the white line, and the margin between the lanes. These memories seems ambiguous yet are more precise than we expect. The white lines on the road are not only a boundary but also act as a scale of perception, helping the driver sense his own speed and

the distance between his car and the car in front of him, from the speed and changing pitch of the lines moving along in his field of vision. This team concentrated on the asphalt road as an object with which to measure the environment and used it as material for a visual rhetoric with which to illustrate the reality of the Shimanto River.

At its head, the river's current has the width of a white line on the road, soon growing to that of a one-lane road. As soon as it comes to the middle reaches, it suddenly begins to meander. Because the river is expressed

![](images/8db14c5a99e8a96f0da236f5a282dc30a4f0649d0eecd1c210c4b995cfcd2c76.jpg)

![](images/9f50632e40539dff2f197079fbf33b425c9de6d1e62adf46b1d0e6a583fe0e4f.jpg)

![](images/be22fef0f5bf6ea6c9395ec9aad89f9d042f96d9205a00a20a1c6ec6c3ceb781.jpg)

These composites are laid upon the surface of the water. The top photo depicts the headwaters. The width of the white line shows the scale of the water. The images of the asphalt roads and the white lines serve as yardsticks to measure the dimensions and the terrain of this environment.

![](images/de8988f586fa9efa99d86bb59cb24991d6682720f7811558ec3b87271afd4761.jpg)

![](images/a8ded92fc98b1c26c6180932964cdb0f988ff89215b5ba36bd339c4f2a7e159e.jpg)

![](images/25660e569607730a72d3643749499a5a1a53235f58266d9677730c72490d2d89.jpg)

![](images/7a2794a6e385c2def3ca2addce5a0ee847e34d60ed7e64cdfbebc6c9a2d20a26.jpg)

![](images/37b2eb4c19055f100ea92421284c102df387e563b2efa85d21497bce9bb543b8.jpg)

as a road, this winding makes us feel as if we were turning a steering wheel of a car. The number of lanes increases in the middle reaches and the dam across the river looks like a parking lot. Finally, near the mouth of the river emerge 120 lanes; this is an astounding sight.

Thanks to the illustrative rhetoric, we experience the terrain as well as the dimensions of the river with a reality exceeding our expectations. Here is generated a reality so fresh and vivid that it awakens us to the fact that we have never seen the river in quite this way. That is, the incompatibility between these aggressive images, in which man-made objects (roads) are placed within nature, attracts our attention and helps fix the river firmly in our memory. Because of the nature of the images, though, we don't so much remember the river's shape as have it etched into our brains. The color photos shown here are those taken before the superimposition of the road's surface on the water's. They leave only the impression of a beautiful river. I would like you to compare the impressions you get from these pictures and from the simulations.

![](images/cd416564af015b52e0306befd3de493ef4f12cd92c79d38357a24c390d3321b7.jpg)

![](images/1519df079909d8e845c688299063b206a073f230a602389df9dc43e29c6c0889.jpg)

![](images/af03fd98771f7eac011d7043ad6141b144981c66215cb85a739baa4ae103a2b7.jpg)

![](images/eaba4519fc1b119aade090ac8ebe54748f34daa1a0049b1c85985f229604b056.jpg)  
EXFORMATION

![](images/ffe56e60d0ac32c01b489111a16b0d4a7e9abe2f59a8a9ee8576bea76381712a.jpg)

Footprint Landscape- -Stepping on the Shimanto River Produced by Kyoko Nakamura, Kazuko Nomoto and Kaori Hashimoto

This team documented the Shimanto River viewed through footprint-shaped windows. They photographed the ground-level Shimanto River landscape through a full-scale footprint-shaped mask of black acrylic. By reconfiguring the photographs of a tremendous number of footprint-shaped ground surfaces, they present the full range of sensory experiences available to us at the riverside.

The ground is cut into the shape of a footprint because, although the visual representation is done with photographs, this team's approach is to stimulate, through the sense of sight, the tactile perception one senses from the ground when walking barefoot. When we see an image of a fish exhibited in an ordinary square-framed photograph, we concentrate only on the fish itself. Put the fish in a footprint-framed photograph and we become very conscious of the fish as an object to be stepped on. When an object is shown with a suggestion of the passage to be sensed (unlike a photo showing the ground in its entirety and implicating no definite intention), we reflexively simulate this suggested act within our consciousness. The fish presented as an object to be stepped on will arouse a conglomerate of whirling senses, including our tactile perception. If this were instead a fish on a plate, other sensory perceptions would be aroused. I've mentioned this interrelation of the senses in the Haptic" chapter. In short, this team's unique perspective is that it tried to present the river as an object to be stepped on.

![](images/90e8821b6403bb7ceffdbd7ad2e5297ef0ec7462b76c6f9fa59b650074d8c8b8.jpg)

I think a method for realistically portraying the sense of touch will evolve hand in hand with the sophistication of technology, but the research done by this team artlessly foretells the future of haptic interaction.

![](images/4bf23802b8079a99a7f1adc6972437c445a160335ee73010c9d67f6a186151a3.jpg)

Picking Up

Produced by Akari Ohno and Asako Tadano

Litter, or fragments of the man-made, is scattered in every quarter of the world. Whether we're on the summit of Chomolungma (called Mount Everest in the West) or at the bottom of the Mariana Trench, there's no escaping the spread of the man-made. And so a point of view that looks not for the existence of litter, but for the type of litter, and how the litter is left, is a viable means of becoming acquainted with our target location.

This team worked to depict the Shimanto River by patiently gathering the man-made objects discarded on its banks and coolly analyzing them like a team of scientific criminal investigators. They gathered samples in the neighborhoods of the 21 sinking bridges. First, they photographically chronicled each littered spot and then gathered the photos. Then they objectively rearranged the data, including the types of man-made objects, the amount of time that had lapsed since they were discarded, the degree of change due to wear and tear, and the conditions of the objects' contact with natural objects like stones and sand.

To avoid any misunderstanding, I would like to add that this approach has nothing to do with the concept of pointing out that this beautiful, clean river is contaminated with garbage. Just as I pointed out in the road simulation story, without even realizing it, we become conscious of the details of the litter in our immediate environment and remember them. That is, without even being conscious of the process, we become familiar with the location and other information about litter on the streets in our neighborhood or along our local riverbank. Thus, by closely examining its litter afresh, we can paint a portrait of the river's individuality. This team approached the river by analogy, eliminating both its shape and riverscape, and deciphered it through the sole aspect of its litter.

![](images/4f7bb8bb8f7006808fe862aa77188b7e8e046f7cd398b393259e2d5e60b0a0e8.jpg)

Those of us who live in the city see the litter of the Shimanto River as somehow less vivid and something that has already half returned to nature. Even though man-made, these articles of trash are physical objects destined to return to nature within a larger cycle, and so they really speak of the elements of the interrelation between the man-made and nature. In one sense, a river is a mechanism that decomposes garbage and returns it to nature. I would like you to concentrate on the objects these students have gathered without seeing the man-made as in opposition to nature.

![](images/4c2ec8006b88d9ce222494170cd555e5158f6cfd153b97842b1e0c62d2e5716f.jpg)

## The Shimanto River Cut into a Cube Produced by Aiko Yoshihara

Just as in the litter-gathering study, observations were done from the bridges; Aiko Yoshihara took photos from each one, and then turned the Shimanto River into information by recomposing the photos into a regular hexahedron, a cube. More precisely, she placed the camera on three points of each bridge (both sides and center) and photographed the scenery in six directions (forward, backward, right, left, up and down), with forward referring to the lower reaches of the river. These photos were affixed to the six facets of a cube. The idea is to let us experience the river through handling the cubes; we can arrange them as a reproduction of the scenery from the bridges where they were created, in 21 rows of three cubes each, or play with them like building blocks, placing them in random groups.

When expressed as the lineup from upper to lower reaches, the blocks entail a perfect mixture of the consistency of images and the abstract quality of scenery reorganized into a cube, and become a mysterious, semiabstract, semi-tangible object. However, once we understand the rules of the photography, we can maintain a perspective on the river that gives us a consciousness of both consistency and inconsistency that differs from conventional photographic expression.

The expression as random aggregates of cubes that is created when we actually play with them like building blocks allows us a heretofore un-

![](images/9ae76f320d8f1bc1bc3971c83cc8f9f6cc9d477aa40d31a2d682db51674d5607.jpg)

![](images/1096b8eb2de767a3baa9c9fb7846461b7b1032e5b283bc7c7db073e1d83f778a.jpg)

Translating the cubes and photos affixed to the six facets of each cube into movement, the blocks are piled and arranged. The image of the existing river is slowly broken up and reassembled.

known experience: physical contact with the images of the river as well as "chance," a context completely different than that of looking at a book of photos. This quiet abstraction and dissimilation of the Shimanto River, with whose image we thought ourselves familiar, is one example of exformation. As a by-product of the project, a volume called "Chinka-bashi no Hon" (The Book of Sinking Bridges)" was also produced, featuring images of the 21 bridges photographed and edited according to a set of fixed rules.

Six Days Alone: The Document Produced by Kouichirou Uno

This project was an attempt to express the Shimanto River by objectively recording the experience of staying there. The approach was to portray the river through the medium of a flesh-and-blood human being who is not familiar with it.

To do this, Uno erected a tent in one location and stayed there for six days. He planned to obtain his sustenance (except drinking water) by fishing the river. It was his first contact with this river and he had no prior experience with either camping by a river or fishing in one. He was going to catch fish with nothing but a fishing kit he bought in Tokyo for about 1,000 yen (eight US dollars or six euros). Thanks to the cooperation of locals living near his tent site, he learned—and put into practice—both fishing methods and the fundamentals of associating with the river.

![](images/cfba40967596a744fdcb58636c99e1ce4e35c3134422f349388446cc558c7a01.jpg)

Surpassing his expectations, Uno was able to increase his daily catch, and he was able to secure all of his food from the river. In his documentary record, he expresses the number and type of fish caught as a graph. The report includes objective facts such as the recipes he used to cook them and the change in his own weight. He narrates his daily experiences as a monologue. Reading through this account, we recognize the atmosphere and feel as if it is we who have begun a life on the banks of the Shimanto River. The existence called "an individual" is certainly an idiosyncratic being, with various experiences, tastes, and strong and weak points, but for the reason that this single human being is a human, I wonder if he is able to act as a deputy sensor, having, on the behalf of others, the experience of many people. Speaking from this perspective, we can consider this document an attempt to gauge the Shimanto River with the measure known as "a person." The intervention of the experience of one individual relates the Shimanto River's existence in an extremely lifelike manner.

In addition to these examples, the project included three other attempts to "exform" the Shimanto River: making a book of continuous, successive photos of the water's surface; expressing the river through composite layered visuals, as if viewed through the compound eyes of an insect; and portraying the river by interviewing the people living nearby.

Here I conclude my presentation of the Shimanto River Exformation project. The students' research was compiled in a book, which demonstrates a particular kind of editing that is in a completely different league from that of conventional guidebooks or monographs.

# Exformation—2 RESORT

After Clothing, Food and Shelter

After a discussion with the new students, we came up with "resort" as the theme for the second year of exformation. In contrast to the very practical theme of the Shimanto River, this one is a bit abstract.

Today people are assiduous in their work, but they seem to put equal passion into their time off. We say that the basic needs for living are food, clothing and shelter, but "repose" may be next on the list. Looking around, we see the burgeoning "resortification" of places that provide the perfect climate, cultural conditions, and easy-access environment for relaxation. All over the world, facilities that seem capable of mesmerizing flesh and soul alike are being built one after another. What do people do when they visit these places?

They draw a deep breath and relax, "Phew!" Sunbathe to their hearts' content. Bask in the silence of the forest. Eat fine food and drink good sake and wine. Enjoy leisurely conversation with family or friends. Read any book, at any pace. Have a massage or a healing treatment. Sleep as much as they like. Relax poolside. Do sports all day long. Forget social restraints. Appreciate an exotic foreign culture. Spend elegant hours surrounded by sophisticated interiors, and so on.

I've heard that "resort" means a place of frequent, habitual visiting. I guess that basically any place we tend to visit with the aim of doing the things listed above would be a resort.

Resorts are nothing new, but I feel that people are paying more attention to them now than they did before, which may be a reaction to the slightly more hectic world we live in today, made busier by technological progress and the resulting increase in the intensity of economic competition. Marshall McLuhan (1943-1962) said, "The message is the massage," but the massage we need isn't one from the media; it's a physical massage. I might have written something like this somewhere in this book, but the truth is, walking in any city in the world, we see many, many signs for aromatherapy, reflexology, Thai massage and shiatsu (acupressure). It seems that our desire for relaxation increases in proportion to the increase in the world's speed.

But what is relaxation, or "resort"? We seem to have a clear understanding of it, but seldom speculate on its essence. "Resort" might not be an ideal theme for analysis and earnest, humorless research, but this is why it impressed me as an interesting topic for exformation.

However, while proceeding with our discussion, I began to notice that there was a subtle gap between my views of "resort" and those of my students. As a middle-aged guy who has had the resort experience just out of the necessity of taking breaks from his work, I imagined "resort" along those lines. I need only spread out a map of the Atlantic Ocean and see the islands dotted over the vast waters and in my mind I can't help but feel the pleasant breeze blowing and imagine a peaceful breakfast surrounded by the scent of lovely flowers.

Below I've reproduced the keywords I still have from our class.

The positive images for "resort": a Heaven-like serenity/doing nothing in particular/going with the flow/pleasant, comfortable/soaking away tension/secluded and extraordinary accommodations/tasting exoticism/returning to Mother Nature/schedule-free/staying the way you are/loose fit/a sense of belonging in harmony/in touch with great nature/limpid and transparent/ neat and clean/bright and lively/in touch with divine, venerable perception.

The negative images: extravagant and blasphemous/affected and offensive/lazy and distracted/unethical and immoral/decaying and decadent.

The images for scenes and situations: rich natural environments like seas and mountains/benign climates/tranquility/luxuriant flora/sunshine filtering through foliage and rustling trees/a thick growth of palm trees/ strange topography and foreign countries/animals, birds and fish/sophisticated spaces/marble and fieldstone/natural wood and botanical material/ expert service/delicious, plentiful food/fully-matured local cultures/perfect hot springs, spas and pools/water spectacles/plant spectacles/massages and other treatments /shade /wind/water/plants/stones/sights/gardens/strolling paths/benches.

The sensory impressions: the soles of the feet/the tips of the fingers/ the touch to the fingers/the touch to the palate/textures/a senseof fitting/a sense of relaxation/moist to the touch /velvety to the touch/slick to the touch/dry and smooth to the touch/sweet scents/wafting scents/faint scents/tranquility/ the sounds of the natural environment/music/cool/lukewarm/warm/hot.

## Relaxation Time that Makes Sense to Everyone

As it turned out, my students' response to these keywords was less than enthusiastic. It seemed that they didn't sense much realism from the image of the picture-postcard resort. That is, there was a difference between the images of a conventional resort and what the students had in mind. What really set us on a beautifully straight course were the words of the students. One, exploring her own consciousness for an accurate sense of resort, came up with this:

To me, resort is peeling a mandarin orange so that all the inner membranes come off nicely and then finding no seeds at all... When I finally take off my shoes at home after having walked in pumps all day... When I finally take off my shoes at home after having walked in pumps all day.When my end of the seesaw is going up!"

What these words capture are not the dramatic resorts where we stretch out our arms and draw a long, long exaggerated breath, but rather those small instances when we release a small sigh of relief in our souls. People do have those moments of repose that occur without flying all the way to some tropical seaside thick with palm trees, or finding a comfortable lounge on which to stretch out poolside. Those moments could be called the "seeds" of resort. And they seem to include more of its essence as well.

One student looked at the example of the hot spring resort town of Atami i Shizuoka Prefecturen central Japan, a place that became quite popular and prosperous during Japan's economic growth, but is now in decline. He pointed out that perhaps the essence of resort might be found there, in the unaffected, rather relaxed air around this particular resort town. He focused on the possibility of finding resort in the intention of skillfully evading sophistication, something like the scent of dissolution that clings to blues music. Even the essence of pleasure manifests itself not in the roaring fire but in the remaining embers. And from the cluttered table following a banquet, we can observe both the bright and dark sides of human behavior. These meanings too were included in this student's suggestion. The discussion with the students awakened me to the fact that before I realized what was happening, my own sense of resort had been hijacked by the stereotyped pursuit-of-pleasure image that mirrors the sense of "resort" or relaxation that has been developed by Western society within the cultures of its colonies. At that moment I was sure that resort was a very viable theme for exformation.

We also began to realize that when we make something, we bring to that process far more rationality and order than necessary. In the practice of making things, modernism tends to compel us towards refinement and inflexibility. Bauhaus-style purity, or achieving perfection through the refinement of some object, does have a certain valuable benefit, but we would feel claustrophobic if the entire world were enveloped in this kind of rationality and sophistication. The world maintains itself with just the right balance between creation and depletion. Design is the intelligence that is conscious not only of the generative direction, but at the same time, of the degenerative direction. The world is like the water's edge, where creation and depletion intersect.

Human existence is the process not only of creating something but also of simultaneously causing the depletion of something. Depletion exists within creation and creation within depletion. "Resort" may be the ideal theme through which to comprehend this.

In a gentle image of decline there is something that cheers people up. This project awakened the consciousness of wanting to capture the bud of a certain sympathy, a sympathy that would make anyone nod in agreement, and almost laugh without realizing it. Here is the list of research topics: Vinyl/ Stripes; Sleeping Outdoors; Soft-serve Ice Cream Machine; Loose Typography; Resort in Everyday Life; Resort Switch; Hiragana (one of two Japanese phonetic scripts); Hyakucha (a hundred kinds of tea); Atami (the hot spring resort mentioned earlier). These nine topics will help open the door of the "resort" that lies within our everyday senses. Below, I'll explain a few of them.

![](images/6caef36632911376c65a66f792c1ae573bc1db83b2deac27fdc6f95fb8f9615f.jpg)

![](images/782d48d8f6890165c4216044e5eb272e6a7d692ecb20e0d599033928dea36dc8.jpg)

VARIOUS RESORTS

Vinyl / Stripes: Turning Tokyo into Vinyl and Stripes Produced by Emiko Ai

This was an experiment to discover the essence of "resort" in the color and texture of vinyl and stripes and apply it to real street scenes. As a first step,

![](images/4f32d1b3887b6ca18e850b4ea7cec89dc5fa0bdd479575156a0a3784a1e65b4b.jpg)  
The road signs turn into vinyl. The design overlays even onto negative signs a heartwarming atmosphere.

Ai observed the situation of vinyl and stripes through photos. Looking at the line of photos, we all felt a deep surge of empathy. At a glance, we understood that colorful vinyl does possess a strange charm, creating a liberating feeling that emancipates the senses. Featured in the photos are a variety of colored sandals, brilliant plastic cups, a blue and white parasol, bright striped tropical fish, and so on. Sometimes the persuasive power of the visual surpasses the eloquence of logic. This presentation was extremely simple and very much to the point. Ai's research went on to simulate images to project the "resort" hidden in vinyl and stripes onto actual scenes of daily life. The series, entitled Turning Tokyo into Vinyl and Stripes," included such unique objects as a road sign, a shop sign, a motorcycle license plate and a rooftop water tank, all of which were transformed into the textures and colors of vinyl inner tubes or pillows, complete with plastic nozzles. Ai laid fresh blue and white stripes on the exterior wall of a housing complex and a pedestrian crossing. Obviously, this is an attempt to visually expose the nature of "resort," rather than a practical design proposal.

![](images/74175782ad752c96870586312a5d74b90d788ff5c991c66149ae829a94e2cfdd.jpg)

Coming in contact with these simulations, we can enjoy imagining the metamorphosis of our immediate surroundings into something filled with an air of repose through the media of vinyl and stripes. When they make us smile, I wonder if we are coming in contact with an essence of "resort" that we never noticed before.

![](images/89aeebe7b8662c6e8df1c696f2043867ccd61663dc5c23afb0f8aa25422fe66a.jpg)

Sleeping Outside

Produced by Makiko Orihara, Yukari Kimura,

Satoko Takahashi and Hiroko Mori

This team's research topic was sleeping in the daytime and outdoors. This kind of rest is different from nighttime sleep. It feels great to enjoy fine food and/or a soak in a hot bath, but there are few things better than doing nothing. This is where this team discovered the "bud of resort." This is to take for granted doing nothing while the sun is high in the sky. Furthermore, they found more value in doing it outdoors. "Sleeping outside." This unpretentious title summarizes the essence of relaxation for a human being.

The team started their research by observing people lying on the grass in the park. They recorded the number of people as the hours passed and the temperature changed. The report shows that the number peaked when the weather was best. When the sun went down and the temperature fell, the number decreased. Few outdoor sleepers were alone. Which is to say that the important factors of the charm of sleeping outside are those that enhance sleeping itself, like the right amount of sunshine and wind; companions or friends; the pleasant touch of the grass and a beautiful green landscape.

![](images/e1b48a161dfd850a67e0453749bfacdf684b0bdc6b131726efe8fbd7bbc99658.jpg)

Once aware of these facts, the team collaborated in creating a portable napping tool they named "NAP." This was designed not for lodging or camping, but for a short-term outdoor activity—afternoon napping. After making their NAPs, they went out to nap, on the moor of Hokkaido ( Japan's northernmost main island); in a meadow, on a ship, on an urban rooftop; on a very large scale pier and on a university campus. Every nap is chronicled in photographs. There is no evidence that required recording, but this was a kind of "resort hunting." The resort they were looking for, and found, is expressed in their recumbent forms, snoozing contentedly.

![](images/50a4da5fde35ae237ca6562d60a20fb4d3b4bd51530dc6ed6ea7f786beaa5266.jpg)

Soft-serve Ice Cream Machine Produced by Shino Ito, Aya Kazama and Mona Tanaka

Soft-serve ice cream IS resort. This team began its research with this simple statement of intuition. Truly, as soon as the cone is in our hands, we forget the hustle and bustle of reality; we are instantly transported into "soft-serve ice cream time." It might be the transient nature of soft-serve—that delectable treat that is more ephemeral than ice cream—that attracts us. The moment of tasting a cone of cold soft-serve ice cream is sweetness itself. It's short-lived, but there are very few things that can transport us to the very doorstep of the "resort" of everyday life so dexterously as this one does.

Previously in the seminar, we had touched on the theme of sunglasses. The moment we put on sunglasses, the whole world turns into a resort. We thought sunglasses were the perfect resort tool. This project has a similar vision. The photos of the security guard, the corporate receptionist, or the salaried worker on the footbridge, each holding a soft-serve cone, precisely reflect the reality in which "resort" becomes incarnate in the ordinary.

![](images/d331a6bc27676d2a2a2aa4b64c8b885d80dc4875594999b420b575fb61d69da0.jpg)

![](images/c5033dbfdbd331e55b374ce655381f36cc7589bb92c32f1335210fac087c6710.jpg)

This team's research first pursued the question of how to design the creamy, semisolid substance known as soft ice cream. In analyzing the production method, the group discovered the mechanism that creates the ice cream's shape. They focused on molding the sweet treat based on different nozzle forms and different motions of the hand holding the cone. With a variety of forms and hand motions (both intentional revolving and random movement), they came up with completely innovative soft-cream designs. The ultimate solution was a cone of ice cream in which the shape of the cone on the bottom exactly replicates that of the cream on top. (The convoluted cone is repeated upside down.) It's a design of superb metaphor, leading the ordinary to an ephemeral "resort."

## Loose Typography Produced by Kazu Yanagisawa

Loose typography is the target of Yanagisawa's research. He attempted to glimpse the congenial appearance of letters that seem to cozy up to us, preparing us for relaxation. Modern typography, represented by Swiss typography, has been very successful because it is clearly measurable, and in the chaotic world of letter usage, this is important. However, typography is also part of a less ordered world; it always interacts with the sensitive human mind and is in continuous contact with naïve reality, which can't be decoded by rules and systems. One more essential aspect of typography might be its groping for a fresher expression and wriggling to escape from order.

Typography operates in close contact with human emotions and psychology. It might even be called a kind of research in which we gather the subtleties of communication that we would experience by delving into the absolute truth of what we really want to say, or, in some circumstances, stepping into a world full of unsophisticated images. It's loose but not corrupt. It would be great if you could sense this nuance from the embroidered clock.

Resort Switch

Produced by Makoto Tomita

Starting with the idea of adding a "resort" mode to the switches of an instrument, Tomita went on to examine the relationship between human beings and machines. In other words, from imagining the reactions of various devices when their "resort" switch is activated, Tomita tried to discover clues to the interaction of humans and instruments.

Tomita's idea originated in the "yuragi (fluctuating breeze)" mode of an electric fan. The yuragi switch makes the regular motions and speed of the fan irregular and unpredictable. This changeable breeze makes us feel comfortable, as if we were in nature's breeze. In the fan's case, the yuragi function came first; the technology led to the development and existence of the instrument. The theme of the "resort switch" came from Tomita's question: Is "resort" the affirmative acceptance of irregularity?

The yuragi button is a resort switch. When it's flipped, the stiff relationship between human and machine becomes supple. Unlike in the case of the fan, in which the yuragi function came first, the device second, Tomita's instruments are first given only an imaginary "resort" switch, superimposed photos, and then Tomita infers its function.

For instance, pressing the resort switch on the TV begins a process in which the images are gently snuffed out and the screen emits a plain white light. In time, the screen slowly turns dark, and eventually turns off. What Tomita has done is imagine the idea of directing a wavering moment of gentle, sleep-inducing light, lessening the lonely silence that falls so suddenly when a television is turned off in the conventional way.

The resort switch on the telephone provides harmonious background music for your conversation. If the person on the other end of the line is someone with whom you can speak candidly, the music makes for a nice mood. This is pure speculation, but if an interface could be devised, product development could follow.

![](images/70c9a6e4b0ca33a7fe919496e5de34273b922c578a0ec316fae23b95f0f26623.jpg)

The exformation project themes I have not explained here are the following: an oral collection of "resort seeds" discovered in everyday life; research into creating a harmonious, mirth-invoking image by writing in the simpler, more rounded phonetic characters known as hiragana words that are normally written in complex, formal kanji (Chinese characters) and in foreign languages; gathering 100 scenes involving tea from all over the country, based on the idea that "resort" can be found in tea; research on the hot spring resort town of Atami, which has been on the decline, but whose identity as an orthodox resort has been motivated.

Exformation taught us that the elements that liberate our senses are innumerable, and they are not to be found in extraordinary extravagance, but in everyday life, where the essence of repose is concealed. People subconsciously desire this essence. We also found out that we had been unaware of this truth.

## Exformation Continues to Evolve

As of 2006, the theme of the third year of exformation is "creases," which are not fresh, plump and full of life, but the opposite. Normally creases have a negative image, but upon reflection, we realized that there's a way to think about things that will allow us to discover their positive value. For example, the best jeans of all are not new, but worn-out jeans, either bleached and weathered by machine washing, or simply vintage jeans.

We expect to be able to exform something by concentrating on the concept and the phenomenon of creases, and the project continues to make headway.

## 8 WHAT IS DESIGN?

# WHAT IS DESIGN?

Hearing the Outcry

What is "design"? This is the fundamental question for my profession, and I spend my days as a designer trying to find the answer somehow. We've entered the 21st century, the entire world is engulfed in a vortex of great transformation, accelerated by technological progress, and our sense of values concerning both the making of things and communication is in flux. When technology changes the structure of our world, the aesthetic values that have accumulated in our environment are often victimized. The world, armed with economy and technology, pushes ahead, but the long-nurtured aesthetic of our daily life, overpowered by the intensity of the transformation, lets out a continual scream. In a situation like this, might it be more important to listen to the cries and face the delicate values that are about to be dissipated in the whirling change, than to look for the next big thing on the horizon? Lately I can't help feeling this way, and the thought grows stronger daily.

Constantly pushing the era forward isn't always progress. We stand between the future and the past. I wonder if we could discover a key to our creativity not in that far-off target at which all of society stares so intently, but rather in the extension of a vision that looks right through society from the past. The future lies ahead of us, but behind us there is also a great accumulation of history—a resource for imagination and creativity. I think we call "creative" that dynamism of intellectual conception that flows back and forth between the future and the past.

Now design is not based solely on Western thought. Because the Industrial Revolution occurred in England, people of non-Western societies have long thought they had to learn the standards of modern civilization from the West. But both the effete civilizations of the day and struggles among civilizations were caused by the global spread of the values of Western modernity. Both human wisdom and design are found in the bosom of individual cultures around the world. We have to become aware of the wisdom and insight that is being threatened with extinction, soon to be swallowed up by the torrent of globalism. We have learned myriad things from modern thought in the West. We give this truth its due respect and each unique culture digests the fruit of Western modern thought, but still, the world has begun to move towards new design wisdom.

Design is the energetic acknowledgement of our own living world through the making of things and through communication. Outstanding perceptions and discoveries should make us happy and proud as living human beings. New things are not born of nothingness, and they are not taken from without, but from our attempts to boldly awaken our everyday existences, which seem ordinary and mundane. Design is the provocation of the senses and a way of making us discern the world afresh. Some of the design projects I've introduced in this book are my attempts to access this sort of speculation in my own way.

If I talk about my own experiences, it's not going to turn into any beautiful story of design theory. Still, verbalizing design is one thing a designer does.

So far, I've told you several design stories, but I'd like to take a brief moment to review the period from the origin of the concept of design through today, in terms of various epochs. This is because I want to confirm one more perspective within the tide of history, one that looks out on both design and my own life. Of course, I don't intend to trace history precisely, but to summon my courage to create a rough portrait, like a croquis.

Two Origins

![](images/552945b8ae25f74f430bc32224134b1faa52e6fc0543b301ce13371052722618.jpg)

Design began at the very moment man started to use tools. When was that moment?

In the film, "2001: A Space Odyssey" (1968), there's a famous scene of that very spectacle. Two groups of anthropoids are fighting one another. One of the anthropoids finds a cylindrical object like an animal bone and picks it up. Using it as a weapon would give his group the advantage. The group with the power of the blow ousts the other. But the club is thrown up into the air and, rotating slowly, turns fluidly into a huge spaceship.

It's thought that tools originated when anthropoids started walking erect, picked up stick-like objects, and hit things with them or used them as weapons. From the instant they took the sticks in their hands, they started changing the world around them through intelligence; the working of their intellect began with the construction of their own environment, and that led to the spaceship. One particular scene in the film symbolically illustrates this evolution with dazzling imagination.

If design is the transforming of the world based on understanding, which forms our environment, the beginning of human wisdom may have been the beginning of design.

By the way, was the club the only resource for tools? To me it seems there was one more. When our ancestors began to walk erect, for the first time both of their hands were free. Putting these free hands together would make a vessel. I wonder if our ancestors drank water from the vessel of their lightly folded palms. I bet they did. Just as we ladle water with our hands from a mountain stream, so did they. When the palms are lightly folded together, the space within is so small that a butterfly can barely flap its wings. Here, in this empty vessel, ready to hold something, is the origin of one more tool, a vessel.

A stick and a vessel—just as life itself has both female and male, so do tools. Didn't our ancestors first obtain these two kinds of tools at about the same time? Imagine the beginning of design right there. What meaning is there in this vanishing point of these two images, where they nearly converge in our distant past? I can't clearly express it in words. However, if we place the wellspring of design there, on this vanishing point, our imagination concerning design will become dramatically more flexible. In particular, I believe that by concentrating on the original form of a vessel, which functions precisely because it holds "emptiness" or nothingness within, we may be able to come up with a new critical discernment towards civilizations that have stressed the club and processed the environment to excess.

The club amplifies physical strength and has evolved into a tool that can process and change the world. A sharpened stone axe developed into tools or weapons for hunting animals: the sword, the spear, the bow and arrow. At the same time, it evolved into the plow and the hoe to cultivate the land; an oar to propel boats; a propeller to paddle the air and processing tools such as the saw, the hammer and the knife. In the dazzling, brilliant history of human beings, the axe has evolved slowly and steadily. After the invention of motive energy, it developed into an enormous power. Power shovels, cranes, tanks and missiles that actualized an even more mammoth increase in power have swollen to such a scale as to metamorphose the nurturing environment of our very existence. And it is not only large-scale things, but also infinitesimal things and microengineering aimed at nanotechnology that are extensions of the club as a means of extending our physical functions.

Of course the vessel developed into various kinds of containers, but it also evolved into all kinds of tools, like clothing and shelter, that hold emptiness within and also hold or preserve things. Likewise, languages, which act as a tool for emotions and speculation; letters and characters, which preserve languages; or books, which house letters and characters. All these are vessels too. Extending from the vessel are also receptacles of intellect, such as the hard drive, which archives all data, including sound and image.

Mankind has constructed civilization by considering the operations of acceptance and preservation as antithetical to those of manufacture and change. Within their evolution, club-descended tools and vessel-descended tools have sometimes united, giving birth to innovative tools like the spaceship and computer, which are neither sticks nor vessels, but comprise both; they are both stick-like vessels and vessel-like sticks. With these innovative tools, what kind of wisdom will mankind cultivate now? The present is the first step in this new situation.

## Decoration and Power

Is design decoration? The concept of modernism is to speculate on the shapes and colors of objects with a rational mind, eliminating all patterned decoration, or "frills." But in terms of human history, mankind's grasp of the concept of simplicity is comparatively recent. In fact, with no fear of misunderstanding, I can say that throughout most of mankind's long history, design has been a metaphor for affluence and the decoration that celebrates the traces of the man-made.

For instance, what was the purpose of designing the volute patterns that are densely overlaid on the bronzeware created in China's Bronze Age? Why aren't the patterns plain and simple? When you think about it, the sensibility that can see value in simplicity depends on a special aesthetic. Human beings are naturally awed by things that are intricate and complex. Our attention is more aroused by bronzeware whose entire surface is covered with intricate patterns than by a plain one with no decoration. This is because within the intricately patterned object is concentrated the mastery of difficult skills and the accumulation of artifice spanning a vast period of time. So we feel a special aura is expressed in the complexity of patterns. Bronzeware was the high technology of the time and was closely related to the authority that then ruled. That is, elaborate decorations were employed, as were breadth of scale and symbolism, to exert centripetal force to maintain unity among countries or clans.

The more the number of human beings on the planet increased, the more dynamically active were the various countries and clans, and the more mutual contact and interference ensued. Refined patterns functioned in the surroundings of all great powers with especially stalwart armed forces and intimidating auras. The volute pattern led to the creation of the dragon pattern. In the neighboring Islamic world, the authority of the religion and of the nations was expressed with intricate geometric patterns as well. The same is true of Europe. The authoritative power of the kings and their countries was closely connected with delicate, elaborate patterns. The congeries of magnificent, complicated skills found in the churches has the same meaning in terms of the demonstration of authority and power.

The same root applies to the reasons for the creation of numerous astounding decorations: the walls of the Taj Mahal in India, with their amazing arabesque designs using a variety of colorful stones from all corners of the globe; the dragon patterns carved everywhere in the Forbidden City in Beijing; the arabesque designs covering entire walls of mosques; the intricate, Gothicstyle cathedrals and elaborate stained glass of their magnificent interiors; the complex, embellished rococo decoration of the Mirror Room in Versailles. These kinds of mind-boggling accomplishments, which can be achieved only with immense amounts of time devoted by highly trained human hands, breed power. Until the arrival of the modern age, the world needed great powers. But the modern age is a world in which individuals, emancipated from such overwhelming power, can freely realize their own ways of life. The beginning of modern society and the collapse of central governments marked by popular revolutions and the rise of democracy were movements to free design from decoration that was used to produce a coercive force, and the impetus for discovering the value of rationality and simplicity.

Indeed, in the days of the inception of modern design, design still rested in the hands of craftspeople; and these hands, which underwent training for the likes of the aristocrats, which knew well both the severity and the pleasure of making things, maintained the quality of the furnishings of traditional life. By and by, thanks to the craftspeople, the general public was almost at the point of being able to enjoy a variety of devices and goods that had been cultivated over the course of history. But a new paradigm for making things, called "machine production," trampled the potential for such an environment. Using this as an opportunity, human beings recognized a manner of forming their environment that was rational and independent-minded; they became conscious of design.

## The Origin of Design

According to the book "Pioneers of Modern Design" by the art historian Sir Nikolaus Pevsner (1902-1983), the concept of design arose from the thinking of two figures: John Ruskin (1819-1900), an advocate of social thought, and William Morris (1834-1896), a theorist and the founder of the Arts and Crafts Movement. This was only about 150 years ago, so it's not an old story. Thanks to the machine production system, which sprang from the Industrial Revolution, England flourished in the mid-19th century. However, the early machine-made products weren't much to look at; they were mere imitations produced by the awkward hand of machinery, aiming to reproduce furniture and other kinds of objects that maintained a vestige of aristocratic decoration. Glancing through reference material from the London's Great Exhibition of 1851, we can imagine what they looked like. The cultivated forms that were refined over time by manual skills were superficially interpreted, distorted and mass-produced at extraordinary speed.

Under circumstances like these, it seems that anyone with any affection for his own lifestyle and culture must have felt the sense of crisis over the loss of something, and must have been concerned about the deterioration of aesthetics. Those crude machine-made goods would never be embraced by the sophisticated traditional culture of Europe without a fight. In fact, the appearance of these substandard objects resulted in the unearthing of both the culture that had been nurtured by manual work and the sensibilities hidden beneath that culture. Ruskin and Morris represented the collective snort of the people: "We absolutely cannot bear it!" This was their protest against machine production, which threatened to roll right over the intricate, delicate sensibilities awakened in us by objects. Their activities were a warning and a great booing against the aggressive, impatient reform of the era. Clearly, the concept of design, or the beginning of its way of thinking, was the backlash of aesthetic sensibility against the dullness and immaturity lurking in the industrial mechanism that was so violently changing man's living environment.

However, there was no turning back for machine production as long as it continued to fuel the trends of mass production and mass consumption. Even though the common intellect and aesthetic sensibilities leveled some criticism against it, nothing was going to slow the momentum of the production and consumption explosion ignited by the Industrial Revolution.
